# Bullinger's Apocalypse Sermons: Parallel Edition

*Bullinger, Heinrich, 1504-1575.*

---

## SERMONS OF HENRI BULLINGER, upon the Apocalypse revealed to the holy Apostle and Evangelist S. John.

## Sermon 1: ☞ Of the author of the book of Apocalypse, of the argument and parts thereof: Finally of the sundry use, and most profitable commodity of the same.

### Paragraph 1

**Original (1561):**

> THe Prophets of God of the old Testament,[note 1.1] were God his messengers to the people, expoſitours of Moyſes or of Gods law, and even ecclesiastical preachers, which applied the doctrine taken out of the law of God, to the people and times wherein they lived to the edifiyng of Congregations. And they all with one [illegible]ccorde have chiefly handled two things in their Sermos. For first the[illegible] have reproved the corrupt manners of all states [illegible]n their time, alleging the rule and prescript of Gods law: And exhorting all men to repentance unto God most acceptable. And to many that were uncurable they threatened all kind of plagues, which they setting forth with all beau [illegible]ie of speech, showed them plainly to be scene with the eye, if [illegible]apply they might so be made afraid, and healed. Secondly [illegible]hey promised and set forth by the mouth of God, the Lord Christ, the true Messiah: Whom also they described lively, [illegible]nd with all his holy church, teaching the faith in Christ, and [illegible]oath what good things are prepared for the faithful in Christ: And also what be the true duties of piety and godliness. Neither have they concealed such things as were needful [illegible]o be known of Antichrist: Admonishing us most diligently [illegible]hat we should beware of that wolf, or rather the most deep [illegible]ongeon of all abominations, and that we should stand fast [illegible]n the sincere faith of Christ. &c.

**Modern English:**

The prophets of God of the old Testament were God’s messengers to the people, expositors of Moses or God’s law, and even ecclesiastical preachers, who applied the doctrine taken out of the law of God to the people and times wherein they lived for the edifying of congregations. And they all with one accord have chiefly handled two things in their sermons. For first, they have reproved the corrupt manners of all states in their time, alleging the rule and prescription of God’s law; and exhorting all men to repentance unto God most acceptable. And to many that were uncurable they threatened all kinds of plagues, which they setting forth with all beauty of speech, showed them plainly to be seen with the eye, if they apply them so might be made afraid, and healed. Secondly, they promised and set forth by the mouth of God, the Lord Christ, the true Messiah: Whom also they described lively, and with all his holy church, teaching the faith in Christ, and oath what good things are prepared for the faithful in Christ; and also what be the true duties of piety and godliness. Neither have they concealed such things as were needful to be known of Antichrist: admonishing us most diligently that we should beware of that wolf, or rather the most deep pit of all abominations, and that we should stand fast in the sincere faith of Christ. &c.

---

### Paragraph 2

**Original (1561):**

> I have taken upon me, through the help of God and your [illegible]rayers, to expound unto you the Apocalypse,[note 1.2] which is a doc [illegible]rine concerning the matters of Christ's church revealed [illegible]rom heaven of Christ in glory, and a some of all godly religion, an exposition and brief declaration of the Prophets, and a prophecy of the new Testament and story of the church

**Modern English:**

I have taken upon me, through the help of God and your prayers, to expound unto you the Apocalypse,[note 1.2] which is a doctrine concerning the matters of Christ's church revealed from heaven by Christ in glory, and a summary of all godly religion, an exposition and brief declaration of the Prophets, and a prophecy of the New Testament and history of the church.

---

### Paragraph 3

**Original (1561):**

> How be it, forasmuch as this book is despised of man[illegible] good and well learned men, and for that all men for [illegible] most part are fully persuaded that it is an unprofitable by[illegible] I will speak here somewhat of the same matter.

**Modern English:**

However, since this book is despised by both good and learned men, and because most people are firmly convinced that it is an unprofitable labor, I will speak here somewhat about this matter.

---

### Paragraph 4

**Original (1561):**

> [note 1.3]First many abhor this book for this cause only that is full of visions, types and figures, for they suppose ho[illegible] this becomes not the Evangelical and Apostolical d[illegible] trine of the new Testament. But by the same mean a go[illegible] part of Daniel should be cast away: Which neuerthele[illegible] is commended to us of Christ himself in Matthew.[note 1.4] [illegible] greatest matter of all, namely the calling of the Gentiles, showed to S. Peter by a vision, as appears in the acts.

**Modern English:**

First, many abhor this book for this cause only that it is full of visions, types, and figures, for they suppose this does not accord with the Evangelical and Apostolic doctrine of the New Testament. But by the same reasoning, a portion of Daniel should be cast away. Nevertheless, this is commended to us by Christ himself in Matthew. The greatest matter of all, namely the calling of the Gentiles, was shown to Saint Peter by a vision, as appears in the Acts.

---

### Paragraph 5

**Original (1561):**

> [note 1.5]And the Prophet Ioel said also, howe the people of [illegible] new Testament should see visions. And so doth the bl[illegible] ſed Apostle S. Peter, expound the same place in the A[illegible] of the Apostles, speaking of the people of the new Teſ[illegible] meant. And our Saviour Christ in the Gospel, propoun[illegible] and declared to the people the most part of the mysteries parables, and in manner by feigned fables, as they call th[illegible] And how much think you doe these visions, types, and [illegible] gures of S. John, differ from the same? This kind of ſp[illegible] doth not darken matters, but makes them plain: And [illegible] keth much for the efficacitie and perspicuity, and for the c[illegible] firming of the memory. For by this mean, matters be [illegible] only declared with words, and heard with the ears: [illegible] are set forth also to be scene of the eyes, and after a sort be fi[illegible] in the memory.[note 1.6] Many for this cause attribute much to pa[illegible] ting: But I suppose that I may much more rightli attribu[illegible] very much to this manner of speaking and teaching, where[illegible] the matter is uttered, not by a coulored, domme, and dead pa[illegible] ting, but as it were with a speaking and lively manner set fo[illegible] to be scene of the eyes. Which is therefore propounded, th[illegible] men might rightly and exactly understand the same. Alb[illegible] therefore that this whole book in a manner consists of viſio[illegible] and figures:[note 1.7] Yet shall we indeed, through the inspiration [illegible] Gods grace, show in our exposition, that all that same m[illegible] keth for the perspicuity and plainenes, and not for the obſe[illegible] ring or darkening of most high and godly matters. I wy[illegible] bring my exposition out of the very scriptures, by coferring joining thereunto the rule of faith and charity. I will search out the circumstances, the things that follow and go before: I will bring ſimilitudes and diſſimilitudes: I will add also there unto the experience of things, and the faith of histories. Which manner of expounding the scriptures all interpreters have always granted to be sound and true.[note 1.8] If better things shall be revealed to others, I will gladly after the precept of the apostle give place unto my betters. For I offer these my doings to be weighed of the godly, upon this condition, that they should try all things, and that which they shall find to be good, to hold fast.

**Modern English:**

And the prophet Joel also said how the people of the New Testament should see visions. And so does the blessed Apostle Peter expound the same place in the Acts of the Apostles, speaking of the people of the New Testament. And our Savior Christ in the Gospel, proposed and declared to the people many mysteries by parables, and in a manner by feigned fables, as they call them. And how much do you think these visions, types, and figures of John differ from the same? This kind of speaking does not darken matters, but makes them plain. And it contributes much to the efficacy and clarity, and to the confirming of the memory. For by this means, matters are not only declared with words and heard with the ears, but are set forth also to be seen by the eyes, and after a sort are fixed in the memory.

Many for this cause attribute much to painting. But I suppose that I may much more rightly attribute very much to this manner of speaking and teaching, where the matter is uttered, not by a colored, dull, and dead painting, but as it were with a speaking and lively manner set forth to be seen by the eyes. Which is therefore proposed, that men might rightly and exactly understand the same. Although therefore this whole book in a manner consists of visions and figures, yet shall we, through the inspiration of God’s grace, show in our exposition that it pertains to the perspicuity and plainness, and not to the obscuring or darkening of most high and godly matters. I will bring my exposition out of the very scriptures, by comparing and joining thereunto the rule of faith and charity. I will search out the circumstances, the things that follow and go before. I will bring similitudes and dissimilitudes. I will add also thereunto the experience of things, and the faith of histories. Which manner of expounding the scriptures all interpreters have always granted to be sound and true.

If better things shall be revealed to others, I will gladly, after the precept of the apostle, give place unto my betters. For I offer these my doings to be weighed by the godly, upon this condition, that they should try all things, and that which they shall find to be good, to hold fast.

---

### Paragraph 6

**Original (1561):**

> Secondly they object, that as well new men as old,[note 1.9] of no small authority, have both doubted of this book, and of the author thereof, and also have contemned it, as full of fables, and unworthy to be reckoned canonical. Let those that so think give me the same liberty, I desire them, which they usurp tho the selves, and think it lawful. For if the book of the Apocalypse [illegible]hold therefore seem worthy to be contemned, for that some notable men, both old and new, have doubted of the authority [illegible]hereof: Why may it not recover his authority again, if I [illegible]hew that the best doctors of the church both old and new, have [illegible]ad a right good opinion of this book? And here to the intent [illegible] wold dissemble nothing at all, I am not ignorant that doctor Martin Luther, a man right notably learned,[note 1.10] in his first edi [illegible]ion of the new Testament in dutch, with a sharp preface set [illegible]efore, hath ſticked this book as it were with a dagger. Howe [illegible]e it good and well learned me were offended with him for this [illegible]is judgment, which in the same found lack both of wit and mo [illegible]eſtie. The same man therefore weighing all things more upright [illegible]y and diligely, what time he corrected his dutch Bible. &c. My [illegible]orſhipful master seems also not to have set very much by his book, and to have ascribed it not to John the Apostle, but to John who they called a divine. But herein there is no doubt but that he followed plainly Eraſmus of Roterda, who is his annotations upon the new Teſtamet. In all the Greek copies, [illegible]aith he, that I have scene, the title was not of John the Apostle, [illegible]ut of John the divine. Eraſmus adds that amongst the Greeks and certain old writers, men doubted of this author, which thing he declares by the testimonies of Eusebius, and Hierom, of whose opinion shall be spoken ſtreightways. But the exempler or Spanish copy which is set forth after the faith of the most ancient and approved Greeks, exhibiteth to us such a title of ths book. [illegible]: That is the Apocalypse of the holy Apostle and Evangelist S. John Divine. For the ancient writers say, how S. John the Apostle and Euangeliſte, for his excellent writing of the son of God, was commonly called, Divine. Whereof it follows, than[illegible] this title doth attribute and not take from S. John this book. Certes Arethas was also, a Greek Author, an[illegible] bishop of Caesarea: Of the auncientes, says he, certain hau[illegible] plucked this Apocalypse from the tongue of that welbeloue[illegible] John, ascribing it to another: but it is not so. For that sam[illegible] Gregory, which as well as he, is called a Divine, accom[illegible] teeth this amongst those scriptures, which utterly want a suspicion of counterfeiting: saying, as the Apocalypse of S[illegible] John teaches me. And the same man a little after: But th[illegible] this book was written by the mouth of the holy ghost, S. B[illegible] ſil, Cyril, Papias and Hippolytus,[note 1.11] fathers of the church, a mete men to be credited. Thus saith he. What shall we say[illegible] that Eraſmus confesses, that the consent of the world, an authority of the church to be of such force with him, th[illegible] he dare not refuse this book?

**Modern English:**

Secondly they object, that as well new men as old, of no small authority, have both doubted of this book, and of the author thereof, and also have contemned it, as full of fables, and unworthy to be reckoned canonical. Let those that so think give me the same liberty, which I desire them, which they usurp themselves, and think it lawful. For if the book of the Apocalypse should therefore seem worthy to be contemned, for that some notable men, both old and new, have doubted of the authority hereof: Why may it not recover his authority again, if I shew that the best doctors of the church both old and new, have had a right good opinion of this book? And here to the intent I would dissemble nothing at all, I am not ignorant that Doctor Martin Luther, a man right notably learned, in his first edition of the New Testament in Dutch, with a sharp preface set before, hath stuck this book as it were with a dagger. However, it grieved good and well learned men, who found lack both of wit and modesty in that judgment. The same man therefore weighing all things more uprightly and diligently, what time he corrected his Dutch Bible. My worshipful master seems also not to have set very much by his book, and to have ascribed it not to John the Apostle, but to John who they called a divine. But herein there is no doubt but that he followed plainly Erasmus of Rotterdam, who is his annotations upon the New Testament. In all the Greek copies, he says, that I have seen, the title was not of John the Apostle, but of John the divine. Erasmus adds that amongst the Greeks and certain old writers, men doubted of this author, which thing he declares by the testimonies of Eusebius and Jerome, of whose opinion shall be spoken straightways. But the exemplar or Spanish copy which is set forth after the faith of the most ancient and approved Greeks, exhibiteth to us such a title of this book: That is, the Apocalypse of the holy Apostle and Evangelist St. John Divine. For the ancient writers say, how St. John the Apostle and Evangelist, for his excellent writing of the Son of God, was commonly called, Divine. Whereof it follows, than this title doth attribute and not take from St. John this book. Certainly Arethas was also, a Greek author, a bishop of Caesarea: Of the ancients, says he, certain have plucked this Apocalypse from the tongue of that well-beloved John, ascribing it to another: but it is not so. For that same Gregory, which as well as he, is called a Divine, accounts this amongst those scriptures, which utterly want a suspicion of counterfeiting: saying, as the Apocalypse of St. John teaches me. And the same man a little after: But though this book was written by the mouth of the Holy Ghost, St. Basil, Cyril, Papias and Hippolytus, fathers of the church, are men to be credited. Thus saith he. What shall we say that Erasmus confesses, that the consent of the world, an authority of the church to be of such force with him, that he dare not refuse this book?

---

### Paragraph 7

**Original (1561):**

> Let us hear now the judgment of that most excellen[illegible] best man D. Ioh. Oecolampadius,[note 1.12] the most faithful p[illegible] ſtour of the church of Basil, and excellently learned in [illegible] prophetical and in all the Canonical scriptures, concerning this book, which he left us written in the xii. Chap. of they[illegible] [illegible] cond book of his commentaries upon Daniel: But S. Io[illegible] the Paraphraſte or expoſitour of the Prophets, saith he, ([illegible] how much he doth attribute to this our author) whom marvel, why certain with so raſſhe a judgment doe reject as a dreamer, and frantic, and an unprofitable writer[illegible] the church: Where nevertheless he propounds and set[illegible] forth very many of the most secret and hid things of the old[illegible] [illegible] ſtament and of the Prophets. But those great men do b[illegible] wray, what a wening they have of themselves: Whose in[illegible] gementes I would verily rather contemn as prophan[illegible] than I would cast away such a treasure. I could here bring forth goodly testimonies of other new writers, but that I make haste to the judgements of the ancient fathers.

**Modern English:**

Let us hear now the judgment of that most excellent man, Dr. John Oecolampadius, the most faithful pillar of the church of Basel, and excellently learned in prophetic matters and in all the canonical scriptures, concerning this book, which he left us written in chapter 12 of his second book of commentaries on Daniel. But St. Jerome, the paraphraser or interpreter of the Prophets, says—[he expresses how much he attributes to this author]—that it is to be wondered why some, with such hasty judgment, reject him as a dreamer, a madman, and an unprofitable writer to the church. Nevertheless, he proposes and sets forth many of the most secret and hidden things of the old covenant and of the Prophets. But those great men betray what a sense of their own importance they have. Their judgments I would rather scorn as profane than I would cast away such a treasure. I could here bring forth good testimonies of other new writers, but I hasten to the judgments of the ancient fathers.

---

### Paragraph 8

**Original (1561):**

> The eldest of all after the Apostles,[note 1.13] whose wrytynges as yet remain, Iuſtine and Ireney, the noble Martyrs of Christ, ascribe this book to John the Apostle. For Eusebius in the xviii. Chapter, of the fourth book of the Ecclesiastical story. Iuſtine, saith he, mentions of the Apocalypse of John, saying plainly that it is the Apostles. S. Hierom also in the life of blessed Iuſtine writes, that Iuſtine expounded the Apocalypse of S. John, but the same exposition remains not so far forth as I know. The same author writes that Ireney[note 1.14] set forth the Apocalypse of S. John, with a commentary, which also is not to be had. He him self, who is red to have lived about the year of our Lord, 160. Witnesses plainly in the fifth book against the Valentinians, that this revelation was exhibited to John the Apostle a little before his days. We allege certain words of his in the xiii. Chapter of this book. Tertullian,[note 1.15] who lived about the year of our Lord two hundred and xx. in the fourth book against Marcion. Albeit saith he, that Marcion refuses the Apocalypse of John, yet shall the order of Bishops reckoned up to the very beginning, affirm S. John to be author thereof. In grave matters, and reasoning against heretics, he uses gladly the testimonies of this book.

**Modern English:**

The earliest of all after the Apostles, Justin and Irenaeus, the noble martyrs of Christ, attribute this book to John the Apostle. For Eusebius, in chapter 18 of the fourth book of the Ecclesiastical History, states that Justin mentions the Apocalypse of John, saying plainly that it is the Apostle’s. Jerome also writes in the life of blessed Justin that Justin explained the Apocalypse of John, but that explanation does not survive to any great extent as far as I know. The same author writes that Irenaeus presented the Apocalypse of John with a commentary, which is also unavailable. He himself, said to have lived around the year of our Lord 160, plainly testifies in the fifth book against the Valentinians that this revelation was shown to John the Apostle a little before his time. We cite certain words of his in chapter 13 of this book. Tertullian, who lived around the year of our Lord two hundred and twenty, in the fourth book against Marcion, although he says that Marcion rejects the Apocalypse of John, nevertheless states that the list of bishops reckoned up to the very beginning affirms John to be its author. He readily uses the testimonies of this book in serious matters and when reasoning against heretics.

---

### Paragraph 9

**Original (1561):**

> The same things are also recited of the blessed Martyr S. Cyprian,[note 1.16] under the title of John the Apostle in this Epistles, treatises and Sermons. Eusebius also in the xviii. Chapter of the fifth book of the Ecclesiastical history,[note 1.17] shows, that Appollonius a most ancient writer uses the testimonies of the Apocalypse of S. John:[note 1.18] And likewise Theophilus Bishop of Antioche. Which he affirms in the xxiiii. Chapter of the fourth book of the Ecclesiastical story. Also Origin,[note 1.19] a great man in the church of God in the xxv. Chapter of the sixth book of the same Eusebius. And he wrote, says he, the Apocalypse, which rested upon the Lords breast. &c.

**Modern English:**

The same things are also recorded concerning the blessed Martyr Saint Cyprian,[note 1.16] under the name of John the Apostle in these letters, treatises, and sermons. Eusebius also in chapter 18 of the fifth book of the Ecclesiastical History,[note 1.17] shows that Apollonius, a very ancient writer, uses the testimonies of the Apocalypse of Saint John.[note 1.18] And likewise Theophilus, Bishop of Antioch, who affirms this in chapter 23 of the fourth book of the Ecclesiastical History. Also Origen,[note 1.19], a great man in the church of God, in chapter 25 of the sixth book of the same Eusebius. And he wrote, he says, the Apocalypse, which rested upon the Lord’s breast. &c.

---

### Paragraph 10

**Original (1561):**

> I have hitherto recited the opinions of the most auncie[illegible] Martyrs and Doctors of the christen church, touchyn[illegible] the Apocalypse, I mean Iuſtine, Ireney, Tertullian, [illegible] prian, Appolonius, Theophilus, and Origin: I will ſhor[illegible] lie after bring yet moo judgements boeth of the [illegible] and Latin writers, of most authority in the church, ag[illegible] yng with the minds of them that we have alleged [illegible] thereto: How be it I will first touche briefly such thing as Dioniſius of Alexandria,[note 1.20] left written of the same boo[illegible] in the five and twenty Chapter of the seventh book of [illegible] ſebius, whom I suppose they have followed, as many[illegible] after him have spoken against this book. He says h[illegible] diverse that were his predecessors, did utterly repr[illegible] and reject this book. Neither hydeth he the cause, w[illegible] they so did, for that the kingdom of Christ is affyrm[illegible] therein to be earthly. Whereunto doubtless they reſer[illegible] that precious city, and the rest which under terreſtr[illegible] kinds, figured spiritual things. Which when we [illegible] in the treating thereof have dissolved, declaring this by[illegible] not to edify the earthely kingdom of Christ, but a ſpi[illegible] tuall and celestial, no man I trow, will reject a good a[illegible] Godly book, for because certain abusing the teſtimon[illegible] thereof, give unto it a wrong sense.

**Modern English:**

I have hitherto recited the opinions of the most ancient Martyrs and Doctors of the Christian church, concerning the Apocalypse, meaning Justin, Irenaeus, Tertullian, Cyprian, Apollonius, Theophilus, and Origen. I will shortly bring yet more judgments both of Greek and Latin writers, of most authority in the church, agreeing with the minds of those that we have alleged thereto. However, I will first touch briefly such things as Dionysius of Alexandria left written of the same book in the twenty-fifth Chapter of the seventh book of Eusebius, whom I suppose they have followed, as many after him have spoken against this book. He says that diverse who were his predecessors did utterly reject and reject this book. Neither does he hide the cause why they so did, for that the kingdom of Christ is affirmed therein to be earthly. Whereunto doubtless they referred that precious city, and the rest which under terrestrial kinds, figured spiritual things. Which when we, in the treating thereof, have dissolved, declaring that it does not edify the earthly kingdom of Christ, but a spiritual and celestial, no man I trow, will reject a good and godly book, for because certain abusing the testimony thereof, give unto it a wrong sense.

---

### Paragraph 11

**Original (1561):**

> Heretics have wraſted verey many places of [illegible] scripture,[note 1.21] to the defence of their error, should therefore authority of the scripture it self be brought in doubt? I[illegible] favours nothing at all the Chiliaſtes or Millenaries this book: He gives them no weapons.

**Modern English:**

Heretics have twisted many passages of scripture to defend their errors; therefore, should the authority of scripture itself be called into question? I favor neither the Chiliasts nor the Millenaries in regard to this book; I give them no support.

---

### Paragraph 12

**Original (1561):**

> Eusebius says very well in the end of the third by[illegible] speaking of Papias, the first author of the Millenaries He thought, says he, that after the resurrection Chri[illegible] should reign here corporally with his a thousand yea[illegible] in earth. Which I suppose he thought for that he vnde[illegible] stood not well the Apostles words, neither that he co[illegible] dered not well those things that were spoken of him [illegible] der figures, for that he was indewed with a small judge[illegible] meant.

**Modern English:**

Eusebius says very well toward the end of the third book, speaking of Papias, the first author of the Millenaries. He thought, says he, that after the resurrection Christ should reign here corporally with his thousand years on earth. Which I suppose he thought because he did not understand well the Apostles' words, nor did he consider well those things that were spoken of him under figures, for that he was endowed with a small judgment.

---

### Paragraph 13

**Original (1561):**

> But in the mean time Dioniſius him self, I, saith h[illegible] dare not reject this book. He adds by and by, that he thinks it not yet to be the book of John the Apostle, but of some other, but yet who that should be, he knew not. He gathers also by certain conjectures, by the phrase of speech, and handling of the book, and by the vnlykenes of wit, that this book should be an other mans, than his that wrote the Gospel, and Epistle. But seeing that the arguments of the story and Epistle be so diverse, that neither they two be like, and the argument of the book of Revelation most diverse of all: Why should it seem marvel, though it agrees not with them in all things?

**Modern English:**

But in the meantime, Dionysius himself, I say, dare not reject this book. He adds immediately that he thinks it not yet to be the book of John the Apostle, but of some other, but who that should be, he knew not. He gathers also by certain conjectures, by the phrase of speech, and handling of the book, and by the unlikeness of wit, that this book should be another man's than he that wrote the Gospel and Epistle. But seeing that the arguments of the story and Epistle are so diverse, that neither are like, and the argument of the book of Revelation most diverse of all: why should it seem marvelous, though it agrees not with them in all things?

---

### Paragraph 14

**Original (1561):**

> This can no man deny, but that in consent of doctrine there is great agremet. The Epistle to the Hebrews seemed to many to favour in the ſyxt and tenth Chap. The Nouatians or Catharites. The diversity of style was noted to differ from the rest of Saint Paules Epistles. But if we should so judge of holy scriptures, I know not what should be firm and sure enough. Leaving therefore this disputation in suspense, I will now proceed to bring forth the judgements of other old writers concerning this book.

**Modern English:**

No one can deny that there is great agreement in doctrine. The Epistle to the Hebrews appeared to many to support the Novatians or Catharites in chapter six and ten. The difference in style was noted to differ from the rest of Paul’s Epistles. But if we were to judge Scripture in this way, I do not know what could be firm and sure enough. Leaving this disputation in suspense, I will now proceed to present the judgments of other ancient writers concerning this book.

---

### Paragraph 15

**Original (1561):**

> Eusebius[note 1.22] ſurnamed Pamphilus Bishop of Ceſarea, living in the time of great Constantine the Emperor, and a most diligent reader of old writers, whom many suppose in deminiſhing the authority of this book to favour them, right eloquently in the eighteth Chapter of the third book of his story, taunting the Tyranny of Domitian, affirms that John exiled into Pathmos, wrote there his Revelation.

**Modern English:**

Eusebius, nicknamed Pamphilus, Bishop of Caesarea, living in the time of Emperor Constantine, and a diligent reader of ancient writers, whom many suspect of diminishing the authority of this book to favor their own views, eloquently affirms in the eightieth chapter of the third book of his history that John, exiled to Patmos, wrote his Revelation there, while taunting the tyranny of Domitian.

---

### Paragraph 16

**Original (1561):**

> And where other Hiſtoriographers doe also the same. He again in the xxiiii. Chapter in the third book, concerning the Apocalypse, saith he, the opinion of men is diverse, some approving, and others reproving the same. Again when he should bring forth his opinion touching the Canon of the new Testament in the xxv. Chapter. He joins the Apocalypse with the books undoubted: although he dissembles not that he will show in another place what other men think thereof. Whilst he this performs, he recopteth many more and better, which judged the Apocalypse to be of S. John the Apostle, and embraced it as a most Godly book, than those which denied or reproved the same.

**Modern English:**

And where other historians also do the same. He again in chapter 24 of the third book, concerning the Apocalypse, says that the opinions of people are diverse, some approving and others reproving it. Again, when he presents his opinion regarding the canon of the New Testament in chapter 25, he includes the Apocalypse with the books of unquestioned authority, although he does not dissemble that he will show elsewhere what other people think of it. While he does this, he incorporates many more and better, who judged the Apocalypse to be of Saint John the Apostle, and embraced it as a most godly book, than those who denied or reproved it.

---

### Paragraph 17

**Original (1561):**

> [note 1.23]Epiphanius Bishop of Salamine in Cypres, a Greek Author also doth manifestly ascribe this book to S. John the Apostle. Read, that he hath left written against Thelogians in the xv. heresy. And S. Hierome attributeth very much to this Epiphanius.[note 1.24] And S. Hierome him self[illegible] aſcribeth this book to John the Apostle, to Paulinus: Th[illegible] Apocalypse of S. John, saith he, hath so many Sacramentes, as it hath words. Moreover Philaſtrius Bishop o[illegible] Griria,[note 1.25] whom S. Austen saith he saw with S. Ambro[illegible] at Millan, accounts them for Heretics, that reject th[illegible] Apocalypse of John, and say that it is not of John the Apostle, but of Cerinthus an Heretic. Verily S. Ambroſ[illegible] [note 1.26] him self alleges in his books testimonies of the Apocalypse, under the name of S. John the Apostle.

**Modern English:**

Epiphanius, Bishop of Salamis in Cyprus, a Greek author, also clearly ascribes this book to Saint John the Apostle. He has written against heretics in the fifteenth heresy, and Saint Jerome attributes much to this Epiphanius. Saint Jerome himself ascribes this book to John the Apostle, to Paulinus: “The Apocalypse of Saint John,” he says, “has so many sacraments as it has words.” Furthermore, Philastrius, Bishop of Griria, whom Saint Augustine says he saw with Saint Ambrose at Milan, considers those who reject the Apocalypse of John to be heretics, claiming it is not from John the Apostle but from Cerinthus, an heretic. Indeed, Saint Ambrose himself in his writings offers testimonies from the Apocalypse, under the name of Saint John the Apostle.

---

### Paragraph 18

**Original (1561):**

> S. Austen[note 1.27] embraced this book as Apostolical: and re[illegible] the same to his church, leaving certain treatises upon th[illegible] same. Primasius also Bishop of Utica in Africa, expou[illegible] ded the same as Apostolical.[note 1.28] Of Bed and the residue o[illegible] that sort, I speak nothing, since his opinion is know[illegible] to all men.[note 1.29] Andreas also Bishop of Caesarea, wrote upon[illegible] this book: As Arethas reports in his commentaries, who opinion I declared before.

**Modern English:**

Augustine embraced this book as Apostolic and referred the same to his church, leaving certain treatises upon the same. Primasius, also Bishop of Utica in Africa, explained it as Apostolic. Of Bed and the residue of that sort, I speak nothing, since his opinion is known to all men. Andreas, also Bishop of Caesarea, wrote upon this book, as Arethas reports in his commentaries, an opinion I declared before.

---

### Paragraph 19

**Original (1561):**

> Me thinks I have sufficiently confirmed the authoriti[illegible] of this book, against them that diminish the same. But th[illegible] same seems strongest of all, that the thing it self, and t[illegible] handling thereof proves, that it did proceed from the Ap[illegible] ſtle. Which thing we shall prove in the treatise it self. But in case those blessed fathers in their time did expound the Apocalypse to their churches,[note 1.30] why should it not be lawful for us also in our time, to expound it to our men, which[illegible] are in the end of the world, where now all things [illegible] more fully then they were than accomplished? Yea the[illegible] things serve most chiefly for us and for our time, fine that we travel and be exercised under Antichrist.

**Modern English:**

I believe I have sufficiently demonstrated the authority of this book, against those who diminish it. But the strongest evidence seems to be the book itself, and the way it is handled, proving that it proceeded from the Apostle. We will prove this in the treatise itself. But if those blessed fathers in their time did interpret the Apocalypse for their churches, why should it not be lawful for us also in our time to interpret it for our people, who live at the end of the world, where all things are now more fully realized than they were in their time? Indeed, these things serve most chiefly for us and for our time, especially as we endure and are tested under Antichrist.

---

### Paragraph 20

**Original (1561):**

> In vain therefore many prattell, that this book is obſcu[illegible] and can not bee understand: And for the same cause to b[illegible] read in the Church without any profit or fruit.

**Modern English:**

Therefore, it is in vain for many to claim that this book is obscure and cannot be understood, and for that very reason should not be read in the church without any profit or benefit.

---

### Paragraph 21

**Original (1561):**

> For to speak nothing hereof, that nothing is set forth in holy scripture, which hath not an excellent fructe: Neither must we by and by despair of the true understanding, although at the first sight of the holy scripture it be obscure, which is opened of God himself, and to be opened, is obtained by prayers, and Godly exercises: Certes we are not ignorant that many had rather nothing were spoken of Antichrist, to the intent he might reygne more carelessly, and they them selves be less subject to perils. But Christ commands us to trouble him. Let us therefore go forward in the work of the Lord.

**Modern English:**

To say nothing of this, nothing is presented in holy scripture that does not have an excellent fruit. Nor must we immediately despair of true understanding, even though at first sight the holy scripture may seem obscure, which is opened by God himself and is obtained through prayer and godly exercises. Certainly, we are not ignorant that many would prefer that nothing be spoken of the Antichrist, so that he might reign more carelessly and they themselves be less subject to perils. But Christ commands us to trouble him. Let us therefore proceed in the work of the Lord.

---

### Paragraph 22

**Original (1561):**

> And where it offends them,[note 1.31] that John makes little mention or none of Christ, where notwithstanding the manner of Apostles is, to intimate always Christ, and the grace of redemption: We suppose this same book more thoroughly looked upon, to prove the contrary. Whose argument now I will recite.

**Modern English:**

And where it offends them that John makes little mention or none of Christ, whereas the manner of the Apostles is always to intimate Christ and the grace of redemption, we suppose this same book will be looked upon more thoroughly to prove the contrary. I will now recite that argument.

---

### Paragraph 23

**Original (1561):**

> The Prophet Zacharie in the third Chapter,[note 1.32] obiecteth the whole mystery of Christ to all men's eyes in a most evident figure to be scene. For he sees Jesus the high priest, appareled in vile garments, and like a coal that is taken out of the fire, to suffer much contradiction of the Devil: by and by the same to have put of the vile clothing, and put on white garments, to be glorified, and proclaimed king and priest and Saviour of all.

**Modern English:**

The prophet Zechariah in the third chapter [note 1.32] presents the entire mystery of Christ before all people’s eyes in a most evident figure. For he sees Jesus the high priest, clothed in shameful garments, and like a coal taken from the fire, suffering much contradiction from the Devil. Immediately afterward, he sees the same person cast off the shameful clothing and put on white garments, to be glorified, and proclaimed king and priest and savior of all.

---

### Paragraph 24

**Original (1561):**

> This figure the Apostle and Evangelist S. John expounds: And first in deed the Gospel being set forth, he describes Christ in vile apparel, howe much contradiction being taken he suffered of the wicked, till at the length he was nailed to the cross. He touches there also his glory: Which notwithstanding, the Apocalypse thereto annexed, he declares more at large, showing the same unto us in a white garment and in glory, howe verily after this humblyng being exalting, he obtained a name aboue all names: And now being in glory, howe he works nevertheless in the church, the saviour of all the faithful in the church. In his Epistle he commends this whole mystery of piety and beats in to all men.

**Modern English:**

The Apostle and Evangelist John explains this figure. First, after the Gospel is presented, he describes Christ in humble clothing, showing how much opposition he endured from the wicked until he was finally nailed to the cross. He also touches on his glory there. However, the Apocalypse appended to it makes this even clearer, showing him in a white garment and in glory, how, after this humbling, he was exalted and obtained a name above all names. And now, being in glory, he nevertheless works in the church, the savior of all the faithful within the church. In his Epistle, he praises this entire mystery of piety and urges it upon all people.

---

### Paragraph 25

**Original (1561):**

> For the whole book is divided into six parts.

**Modern English:**

For the whole book is divided into six parts. The first part doth treat of the state of the Church before the coming of our Lord Jesus Christ. The second part doth shew the state of the Church from the time that our Lord Jesus Christ came in the flesh until his ascension into heaven. The third part doth declare the state of the Church from the time that our Lord Jesus Christ ascended into heaven until the last day. The fourth part doth speak of the time when the kingdom of our Lord Jesus Christ shall be set up upon earth. The fifth part doth shew the state of things in heaven, and the sixth part doth describe the last judgment, and the eternal glory of the saints. And as Hierome, Augustine, Irenaeus, and Dionysius have taught, so also doth this division agree with the order of the Apocalypse. For it is not to be understood of six several ages, but of six parts of time, wherein God doth shew his will and purpose, according to his good pleasure. And in every dispensation, he doth work and govern all things, according to his most wise and holy will. And though some men do judge that the Apocalypse is a prophecy of the Antichrist, and of the things that shall come to pass in the end of the world, yet it is not so. For it doth not speak of one particular time, but of all times, and of all the events that shall happen until the last day. And therefore, it is not to be understood of a certain number of years, but of the order and manner of God’s working, and of the things that shall be done in every age, according to his will. And as much as concerneth the authority of the Scripture, it is to be received and understood according to the rule of the Church, and the consent of the Fathers. And in Zurich, we do follow this rule, and do teach the people according to the word of God.

---

### Paragraph 26

**Original (1561):**

> [note 1.33]1 For first is set the title with the beginning and some o[illegible] the work, and with a brief narration: And all this in th[illegible] first part of the first chapter.

**Modern English:**

For first is set the title with the beginning and some of the work, and with a brief narration: And all this in the first part of the first chapter.

---

### Paragraph 27

**Original (1561):**

> 2 Secondly from the midst of the first Chapter to th[illegible] fourth Chapter, is described Christ reignyng in glory, o[illegible] the right hand of the Father, and is declared, howe he i[illegible] conversant in the Church by his spirit, and ministry o[illegible] his word. What thing he teaches from heaven, and what is the sincere doctrine of the church: what is also th[illegible] repairing of Churches that are fallen, and the preſeruatio[illegible] of the same.

**Modern English:**

Secondly, from the midst of the first chapter to the fourth chapter, Christ’s reigning in glory is described, at the right hand of the Father, and it is declared how he is active in the Church by his Spirit and ministry of his word. What things he teaches from heaven, and what the sincere doctrine of the church is: what also is the restoring of churches that have fallen, and the preservation of them.

---

### Paragraph 28

**Original (1561):**

> 3 Than from the fourth Chapter to the xii. Christ still admonishes his Church diligently by seven Seals, and seven trumpets, what things shall happen to the Church[illegible] all the which are most justly governed of God him se[illegible] by the Lamb Christ.

**Modern English:**

3 Then from the fourth Chapter to the twelfth, Christ still diligently admonishes his Church through seven seals and seven trumpets, describing the events that will befall the Church, all of which are most justly governed by God himself through the Lamb, Christ.

---

### Paragraph 29

**Original (1561):**

> 4 Moreover from the xii. Chapter to the xv, is more fully described the conflict of the Church with the old Serpent, and with the old and new beast. Where also the f[illegible] myſhe Tyranny both old and new, and verey Antichrist him self is trymly painted forth in his colours: N[illegible] withstanding that afterward also these things are agay[illegible] more plainly declared.

**Modern English:**

4 Moreover, from chapter twelve to fifteen, the conflict of the Church with the ancient Serpent and with the old and new beast is more fully described. Here, too, that monstrous tyranny, both old and new, and the very Antichrist himself, are clearly depicted in their colors; nevertheless, these things are afterward also declared again more plainly.

---

### Paragraph 30

**Original (1561):**

> 5 And from the fiftenth Chapter to the two and twenti[illegible] Chapter, are recited the pains and tourmentes of Antichrist, and Antichristians, and the destruction of the sam[illegible] and the condempnation of all the wicked. Also the Judge[illegible] Christ is set forth, and the process of an external judgment is figured. There is also rehearsed the triumph, Joy, and reward of Saints. Where also heaven it self is opened to bee seen of our eyes, that now[illegible] we may by faith look in to the same. The depth o[illegible] Hell is opened, that we may look in to it also: And may take good head that we bee not thrown thither headlong.

**Modern English:**

5 And from the fifteenth Chapter to the twenty-second Chapter, are recited the pains and torments of Antichrist, and Antichristians, and the destruction of the same and the condemnation of all the wicked. Also the Judge, Christ, is set forth, and the process of an external judgment is figured. There is also rehearsed the triumph, joy, and reward of Saints. Where also heaven itself is opened to be seen by our eyes, that now we may by faith look into the same. The depth of Hell is opened, that we may look into it also: and may take good heed that we be not thrown thither headlong.

---

### Paragraph 31

**Original (1561):**

> 6 Finally in the end of the xxii. Chapter, follows the conclusion, and commendation of the work, with the sealing up of the same.

**Modern English:**

Finally, at the end of chapter twenty-two, follows the conclusion and commendation of the work, with its sealing.

---

### Paragraph 32

**Original (1561):**

> And here I will not hyde an other division of this work not to bee contempned,[note 1.34] which I see the expoſitours have followed. For first they rehearse the Title and beginning. After they annexe the whole work it self, divided by seven visions. And indeed the seventh number is most frequent, and as it were peculiar to this book. Finally they add to the conclusion of the work in a manner comprised in the last Chapter. And these visions be compassed within their limits.

**Modern English:**

And I will not hide another division of this work, not to be scorned, which I see the commentators have followed. For first, they rehearse the title and beginning. Afterward, they annex the entire work itself, divided into seven visions. Indeed, the number seven is most frequent, and as it were, peculiar to this book. Finally, they add to the conclusion of the work, in a manner comprised in the last chapter. And these visions are contained within their limits.

---

### Paragraph 33

**Original (1561):**

> In the three first Chapters the first vision is expounded exhibityng Christ unto us reigning in glory, governing, ordering, correcting, and preserving his church.

**Modern English:**

In the first three chapters, the initial vision is explained, exhibiting Christ to us reigning in glory, governing, ordering, correcting, and preserving his church.

---

### Paragraph 34

**Original (1561):**

> The second vision begins in the fourthand, reaches to the eight Chapter. That sets forth God him self and his Christ to be looked upon, whose most just government of all things in the world it commends, and opens seven Seals.

**Modern English:**

The second vision begins in the fourth chapter and extends to the eighth. That passage depicts God himself and his Christ, presenting them for consideration, and commends their just governance of all things in the world, opening seven seals.

---

### Paragraph 35

**Original (1561):**

> The third vision hath seven Angles ſoundynge with seven Trumpets. Which treatise stretches to the xii. Chapter.

**Modern English:**

The third vision has seven angels sounding with seven trumpets. This section extends to chapter twelve.

---

### Paragraph 36

**Original (1561):**

> The fourth Vision shows the fight of the woman with the Serpent, and sets forth to us the old seven headed, and the new two horned beast to be seen, the description of Antichrist, and this in the xii. xiii. and xiiii. Chapter.

**Modern English:**

The fourth vision depicts the struggle of the woman with the serpent, and presents to us the old seven-headed beast and the new two-horned beast, which are to be understood as the description of Antichrist, and this is found in chapters twelve, thirteen, and fourteen.

---

### Paragraph 37

**Original (1561):**

> In the fifth vision seven Angels power out seven vials of Gods wrath, unto the xvii. chapter.

**Modern English:**

In the fifth vision, seven angels pour out seven vials of God’s wrath, up to chapter seventeen.

---

### Paragraph 38

**Original (1561):**

> From thence begins the sixth vision, and extends to the xxi. Chapter, and disputes of the most just judgment of God against Babylon, the whore of Babylon, and the Antichristians, finally against all wicked and impenitent persons.

**Modern English:**

From there begins the sixth vision, and it extends to chapter twenty-one, discussing God’s just judgment against Babylon, the great prostitute, and against those who oppose Christ, ultimately against all unrepentant and wicked people.

---

### Paragraph 39

**Original (1561):**

> The seventh and last vision propounds to the eyes of all the faithful, the glory and bliss everlasting[illegible] of Saints. And verily this division of the work hath a great grace and affinite with the rest of the things, which in this book are all in a manner treated by the seventh nubr[illegible] Let the reader follow which he will.

**Modern English:**

The seventh and final vision presents to the eyes of all the faithful the glory and eternal blessedness of the Saints. Indeed, this section of the work possesses great grace and affinity with the rest of the things which in this book are all, in a manner, treated by the seventh number. Let the reader follow whichever he prefers.

---

### Paragraph 40

**Original (1561):**

> [note 1.35]Now of these things every man may perceive the this book is altogether Apostolical, and exceadynge profitable to us all, especially whom the end of the world[illegible] hath overtaken. And this book shall bee easier for us, f[illegible] that all things now are in a manner accomplished.

**Modern English:**

Now concerning these things, every person may perceive that this book is entirely apostolic and exceedingly profitable to us all, especially those whom the end of the world has overtaken. And this book will be easier to understand now that so many things are, in a manner, fulfilled.

---

### Paragraph 41

**Original (1561):**

> Daniel was thought to have told of stark dream[illegible] when before the Monarchies, he prophesied the Mona[illegible] chies. But after those things were accomplished, which[illegible] he prophesied, he seemed unto many to have compiled an h[illegible] story. The self same, I am sure, thou will judge also: this same book of Saint John. A few profittes only of m[illegible] ny we shall recite.

**Modern English:**

Daniel was thought to have told of stark dreams when before the Monarchies, he prophesied the Monarchies. But after those things were accomplished which he prophesied, he seemed unto many to have compiled a history. The self same, I am sure, thou will judge also: this same book of Saint John. A few prophecies only we shall recite.

---

### Paragraph 42

**Original (1561):**

> First we have in this book a most full description Christ, reignyng in glory, our king I say, and Bishop And howe he governs the Church, and is the Sauio[illegible] of all faithful. We have also a most gallant deſcri[illegible] tion of Christ's Church, and howe the same may be bu[illegible] ded, repared, and maintained. Than have we a perf[illegible] description of Antichrist, of his members, and Synagog[illegible] of his counsels, crafty devises, kingdom, cruelty, a[illegible] deſtructions of the same: From the which it byddeth [illegible] beware. Moreover we have an abrydgement of Hiſtor[illegible] from Christ's time, unto the worlds end.

**Modern English:**

In this book, we have a most complete description of Christ reigning in glory, our king, I say, and how he governs the Church and is the Savior of all the faithful. We also have a most excellent description of Christ’s Church, and how it may be built up, repaired, and maintained. Then we have a perfect description of Antichrist, of his followers, and the synagogue of his counsels, crafty schemes, kingdom, cruelty, and destruction. From this, it bids us beware. Moreover, we have a summary of history from Christ’s time until the end of the world.

---

### Paragraph 43

**Original (1561):**

> Finally an absolute and certain prophecy of thing[illegible] to come, that we need not to have the prophecies of M[illegible] thodius, Cyrill, Merline, Briget, Nolhard and certain triflers

**Modern English:**

Finally, a certain and absolute prophecy of things to come, so that we need not the prophecies of Methodius, Cyril, Merlin, Bridget, Nolhard, and certain triflers.

---

### Paragraph 44

**Original (1561):**

> Furthermore we have a great consolation and comfort of the church in adversity, whyleſt boeth we see th[illegible] Lamb to open the Seals, and that all things are do[illegible] by Gods providence, and that there is an end of euylle[illegible] And that the church shall bee evermore, in diſpitee of all th[illegible] Devils in hell. Last we have a most plentiful and sure do[illegible] trine of the Judge and last judgment, of pains and of t[illegible] wards.

**Modern English:**

Furthermore, we have great consolation and comfort for the church in adversity, while we see the Lamb open the Seals, and that all things are accomplished by God’s providence, and that there is an end to evil. And the church shall endure forever, despite all the devils in hell. Lastly, we have a most plentiful and sure doctrine of the Judge and final judgment, of punishments and of rewards.

---

### Paragraph 45

**Original (1561):**

> All these things I say, shall the treatise it self show plainly, for our edefiyng through Jesus Christ our Lord.

**Modern English:**

All these things I say, the treatise itself will plainly demonstrate, for our edification through Jesus Christ our Lord.

---

## OF THE TITLE OF THE whole work, and exposition thereof. The second Sermon. ☞ I said the whole book was contained in six parts.Three members of the first part. Now must we look on the first part: Which hath chiefly three members: The title, beginning, and brief narration. For this present we will only speak of the Title, which is thus.

### Paragraph 46

**Original (1561):**

> THe reuelatio of Jesus Christ, [note 2.1] which God gave unto him, for to show unto his servants, things which must shortly come to pass: And he sent and showed by his angel unto his servant John, which bare record of the word af God, and of the testimony of Jesus Christ, and of all things that he saw. Happy is he that reads, and they that hear the words of the prophecy, and keep those things which are written therein. For the time is at hand.

**Modern English:**

The revelation of Jesus Christ, which God gave to him, to show to his servants what must shortly come to pass. And he sent and showed by his angel to his servant John, who bore witness to the word of God, and to the testimony of Jesus Christ, and to all things that he saw. Blessed is he who reads, and those who hear the words of the prophecy, and keep those things written therein. For the time is at hand.

---

### Paragraph 47

**Original (1561):**

> This title is plentiful,[note 2.2] and utters all profitable circumstances, that are to be declared in the beginnings of books.

**Modern English:**

This title is abundant and proclaims all beneficial matters that ought to be stated at the beginnings of books.

---

### Paragraph 48

**Original (1561):**

> First is set the Title, or inscription of the whole work, that is, the Apocalypse, or revelation of Jesus Christ, which verily was opened or revealed by Christ Jesus him self.

**Modern English:**

First, the title, or inscription, of the entire work is set forth: the Apocalypse, or revelation of Jesus Christ, which truly was opened or revealed by Jesus Christ himself.

---

### Paragraph 49

**Original (1561):**

> This title ſtreightway proves,[note 2.3] that this work is no mans invention, but a godly doctrine: As that which was opened by our Lord, king, and priest Jesus Christ, out of heaven, from the right hand of the father, executing there the office of the high Bishop, and as yet teaching us profitable things. and albeit it be called also the reuelatio of John, yet is it challenged to him for none other cause, than for that as scribe he wrote and set it forth.

**Modern English:**

This title immediately demonstrates that this work is not a human invention, but a divine doctrine: as it was revealed by our Lord, king, and priest Jesus Christ, out of heaven, from the right hand of the Father, exercising there the office of the high priest, and still teaching us profitable things. And although it is also called the Revelation of John, it is attributed to him for no other reason than that, as a scribe, he wrote and presented it.

---

### Paragraph 50

**Original (1561):**

> [note 2.4]Again it is yet more plainly declared, from whence this Revelation is: Even of God him self. For he says, which God, namely the Father, gave unto him, to wit, to Christ. For in the holy and blessed Trinity, there is a distinction of persons. And albeit that all things of the father, be the sons also: And all things of the son, the fathers likewise: Yet the scripture mentions the father to give unto the Son, and the Son to receive of the Father.

**Modern English:**

Again it is yet more plainly declared from whence this Revelation comes: Even from God himself. For he says, which God, namely the Father, gave unto him, to wit, to Christ. For in the holy and blessed Trinity, there is a distinction of persons. And although all things of the Father are also the Son's, and all things of the Son are likewise the Father's, yet Scripture mentions the Father giving to the Son, and the Son receiving from the Father.

---

### Paragraph 51

**Original (1561):**

> Which thing all the ancient writers, have full Godly expounded, to be done by the mystery of dispensation. For the Son received somewhat of the Father, as man which otherwise as the very Son of God says Father,[note 2.5] glorify thou me with the glory which I had with thee, before this world was.

**Modern English:**

The ancient writers have consistently explained this as occurring through the mystery of dispensation. For the Son received from the Father, as a man—and as the very Son of God says—"Glorify me with the glory that I had with you before the world existed."

---

### Paragraph 52

**Original (1561):**

> Moreover the Son is the wisdom, word, and mouth of the Father, by whom God in times paste and now, spake and speaks to the Fathers, Prophets, Apostles, and to the universal church. The Father by dispensation gave to his Son this office, that he should be Bishop. For no man hath scene God at any time: The only begotten which is in the bosom of the Father, he hath revealed unto us. Let us know therefore, this same to be a Revelation Divine, which God the Father, loving mankynde, hath revealed by the only Bishop Christ unto his Church. And so it joins together the Father and the Son, that nevertheless the holy distinction of persons remains safe.

**Modern English:**

Moreover, the Son is the wisdom, word, and mouth of the Father, by whom God in times past and now spoke and speaks to the Fathers, Prophets, Apostles, and to the universal church. The Father, by dispensation, gave to his Son this office, that he should be Bishop. For no man has seen God at any time; the only-begotten Son, who is in the bosom of the Father, has revealed him to us. Let us know, therefore, that this is a divine Revelation, which God the Father, loving mankind, has revealed by the only Bishop Christ to his Church. And so it joins together the Father and the Son, that nevertheless the holy distinction of persons remains safe.

---

### Paragraph 53

**Original (1561):**

> [note 2.6]Now also is added, to what end God the Father hath revealed, or given the gift of revealing, to wit, the office of priesthood, to his Son, our Lord Jesus Christ: to the intent verily that the same being revealed, he might show it, and as it were set it before the eyes of his servants, to wit, his worshippers, and Christians, which are called the servants of God for their willing obedience. And as the servant of a Lord is a servant, and owes to his Lord all that he hath, or is worth: So we owe unto God our selves whole, and all ours, or else we be free, and not bound.

**Modern English:**

Now also is added, for what purpose God the Father has revealed, or granted the gift of revealing, namely, the office of priesthood to his Son, our Lord Jesus Christ: so that, being revealed, he might demonstrate it, and as it were set it before the eyes of his servants, that is, his worshippers and Christians, who are called the servants of God for their willing obedience. And as a servant owes all that he has or is worth to his lord, so we owe ourselves whole to God, and all that is ours, or else we are free, and not bound.

---

### Paragraph 54

**Original (1561):**

> Here is also declared, unto whom this revelation is opened:[note 2.7] To all the servants of God. If therefore thou be glad to be called the servant of God, hear this book, and remember it: And know that this book is prepared for thee of God.

**Modern English:**

It is also declared to whom this revelation is opened [?]: to all the servants of God. If, therefore, you desire to be called a servant of God, hear this book and remember it; and know that this book is prepared for you by God.

---

### Paragraph 55

**Original (1561):**

> After he compryſeth in few words,[note 2.8] what Christ revealed to John, things that must shortly come to pass. The destinies therefore of the Church are recited, what good and evil things shall happen to the Godly, and likewise what punyſſhementes must be inflicted to the wicked.

**Modern English:**

After he summarizes briefly what Christ revealed to John, things that must shortly come to pass. Therefore, the destinies of the Church are recounted, what good and evil things will happen to the faithful, and likewise what punishments must be inflicted on the wicked.

---

### Paragraph 56

**Original (1561):**

> And let no man gather of this word, must, necessity, as though God wrought not freely:[note 2.9] Or that the wicked did evil, not through their own fault, but by Gods compulsion. Good things must be done, because God wyllyngly byndyng him self to us by his promise, can not but doe that he doth and promises: Nevertheless he works frely.

**Modern English:**

Let no one understand this passage as implying a requirement, necessity, as though God did not work freely;[note 2.9] or that the wicked commit evil not through their own fault, but by God’s compulsion. Good things must be done, because God, binding himself to us willingly through his promise, cannot fail to do what he promises and declares he will do; nevertheless, he acts freely.

---

### Paragraph 57

**Original (1561):**

> Good things must be done in the Godly, for because the nature of grace and faith is such, like as the property of vngodlines is to contemn and transgress. Wherefore they must also be punyſſhed. And because the world is such, as it is, there must needs bee Heresies and calamities innumerable. And he says these things must shortly be done that are revealed, for because certain things began in the very time of S. John. And although many things are found to be done a thousand years after,[note 2.10] yet says the Apostle saint Peter. A thousand years before the Lord, be as it were yesterday. Therefore this Revelation appertains to the times of the primatiue and last Church: And declares what things so ever shall happen unto it till the last judgment. Yea and howe it shall reign for ever.

**Modern English:**

Good things must be done in the godly community, for the nature of grace and faith is such that ungodliness is characterized by contempt and transgression. Therefore, it must also be punished. And because the world is as it is, there must needs be innumerable heresies and calamities. He says these things must shortly be done that are revealed, for certain things began in the very time of Saint John. And although many things are found to be done a thousand years after, Saint Peter says, “A thousand years before the Lord are as it were yesterday.” Therefore, this Revelation pertains to the times of the primitive and last church, and declares whatever things shall happen unto it until the last judgment. Yea, and how it shall reign forever.

---

### Paragraph 58

**Original (1561):**

> Moreover the manner of revealing is also touched.[note 2.11] For Christ revealed those things, sending by his Angel, or his Angel sent forth, unto whom he gave in commandment what he should say and doe. Whereupon this Angel is after also called Christ, because he represented the person of Christ. Therefore must not the Angel in this book, but Christ always be considered, the true Author of all these things. And indeed the Divinity of Christ is here commended unto us, what time we hey[illegible] that Christ is the Lord of angels. Whereof S. Paul to the Hebrews hath reasoned more at large.[note 2.12] Moyſes in the xii.[illegible] Numbres sets forth chiefly three manners of propheciyn[illegible] or revelation.[note 2.13] First by vision, of the which sort many are [illegible] ſcribed to Daniel, one notable to S. Peter in the x. of th[illegible] Acts, and likewyſe to S. Paul. And into this form Infer also the Apocalypse. Secondly by dream: of the which[illegible] sort were those of Pharaoh and Nebuchadnezzar kings whereof Joseph and Daniel were interpreters.[note 2.14] The Prophet Ioel in the second chapter, mentions of visions and dreams. For in the new Testament also there be very many holy and prophetical dreams. Last of all Moyſes rehe[illegible] ſeth a ſkylfull exposition, as many were made to Moyſes to the Apostles. Into whose fellowship the Apocalypse comes after a sort also, where visions are openly expound[illegible] Here appears the vnſpekeable goodness of God, which [illegible] many ways procures and works our salvation, and ſ[illegible] pleasantly prepared offers it unto us to enjoy. Unhappy is he, that knows not these things.

**Modern English:**

Moreover, the manner of revealing is also touched. For Christ revealed those things, sending by his angel, or his angel sent forth, to whom he gave commandment what he should say and do. Whereupon this angel is afterward also called Christ, because he represented the person of Christ. Therefore, the angel in this book, but Christ always, must be considered, the true Author of all these things. Indeed, the divinity of Christ is here commended to us, when we hear that Christ is the Lord of angels. Paul reasoned more at large about this to the Hebrews. Moses in the twelfth chapter of Numbers sets forth chiefly three manners of prophecy or revelation. First, by vision, of which sort many are ascribed to Daniel, one notable to Peter in the tenth chapter of Acts, and likewise to Paul. And into this form falls also the Apocalypse. Secondly, by dream: of which sort were those of Pharaoh and Nebuchadnezzar, kings, of whom Joseph and Daniel were interpreters. The Prophet Joel in the second chapter mentions visions and dreams. For in the New Testament also there are very many holy and prophetical dreams. Lastly, Moses sets forth a skillful exposition, as many were made to Moses and to the Apostles. Into whose fellowship the Apocalypse comes after a sort also, where visions are openly explained. Here appears the unspeakable goodness of God, which in many ways procures and works our salvation, and pleasantly prepared offers it to us to enjoy. Unhappy is he who does not know these things.

---

### Paragraph 59

**Original (1561):**

> [note 2.15]Besides this, much mention is made unto whom Chris[illegible] hath opened this divine and most excellent revelation, eu[illegible] to John. He commends him, that is himself (for so was i[illegible] expedient for the confuting of his adversaries, seeing that Pau[illegible] also many times maintained his authority against the fall[illegible] Apostles) by four Epithets. For first he calls himself th[illegible] servant of Christ. This is the eldest and noblest title, which[illegible] the fathers, Prophets, and Apostles have used. For they b[illegible] addict and consecrate to God. Secondly John testified th[illegible] word of God amongst the Apostles, most expressly declaring the divinity of Christ, especially where he testified and said In the beginning was the word.[note 2.16] &c. Moreover he testify[illegible] the witness of Jesus Christ: Under the which name the Lord[illegible] himself in the Gospel, and S. John in the xii. Chapter. of his Gospel, copriſed the whole Evangelical doctrine. And was a seeing witness of all these things. For in the first Chapter we have scene, saith he, his glory. And in the xix. Chapter he saw water and blood gush out of the Lords side. In his Epistle,[note 2.17] that we have scene, saith he, and have heard. &c.

**Modern English:**

Besides this, much mention is made unto whom Christ has opened this divine and most excellent revelation, even to John. He commends him, that is himself (for so it was expedient for the confuting of his adversaries, seeing that Paul also many times maintained his authority against the fallen Apostles) by four epithets. For first he calls himself the servant of Christ. This is the eldest and noblest title, which the fathers, Prophets, and Apostles have used. For they dedicate and consecrate to God. Secondly, John testified the word of God amongst the Apostles, most expressly declaring the divinity of Christ, especially where he testified and said, “In the beginning was the word.” Moreover, he is a witness of the witness of Jesus Christ: under which name the Lord himself in the Gospel, and John in the twelfth chapter of his Gospel, comprised the whole evangelical doctrine. And was a seeing witness of all these things. For in the first chapter we have seen his glory, he says, and in the nineteenth chapter he saw water and blood gush out of the Lord’s side. In his epistle, he says, “We have seen and have heard.”

---

### Paragraph 60

**Original (1561):**

> Arethas notes, that in certain Greek copies is added, that which is had also in the Greek copy of Spain. And what things soever he hath heard, and what so ever be, and what so ever must be done after this.

**Modern English:**

Arethas notes that in certain Greek copies is added what is also found in the Greek copy of Spain. And whatever he has heard, and whatever is, and whatever must be done after this.

---

### Paragraph 61

**Original (1561):**

> That same John therefore is author of this book,[note 2.18] which as he saw the Lord in flesh upon earth, so he saw the same in spirit revealing these things in heaven: and propounds to the church sights most certain and sure. This John was that beloved disciple of the Lord, which in the last supper, rested upon his breast, unto whom in his last will he bequeathed his mother on the cross, one virgin to another.

**Modern English:**

That same John is the author of this book,[note 2.18] who, as he saw the Lord in the flesh upon earth, so he saw him in spirit, revealing these things in heaven, and who presents to the church sights most certain and sure. This John was that beloved disciple of the Lord, who at the last supper rested upon his breast, to whom in his last will he bequeathed his mother on the cross, one virgin to another.

---

### Paragraph 62

**Original (1561):**

> He alone stood by at the altar of the cross when Christ died: witness of the true death, and of our purification. He lived till the time of the Emperor Traiane, which thing Eusebius in his cronicles citeth out of Ireney, in the noting of the year from the birth of Christ an hundred and three. Dorotheus a most ancient writer, affirms the John lived vi. score years.

**Modern English:**

He alone stood by at the altar of the cross when Christ died: a witness to the true death and to our purification. He lived until the time of Emperor Trajan, as Eusebius recounts in his chronicles, citing Irenaeus, who notes the year as one hundred and three from the birth of Christ. Dorotheus, a very ancient writer, affirms that John lived sixty years.

---

### Paragraph 63

**Original (1561):**

> Last is touched also the profit of this godly work or revelation,[note 2.19] that hereby the readers and hearers might be provoked to diligence. Where this book is called also a prophecy. For this book by reason of the telling before of things to come, is the prophecy, of the new Testament: Moreover a prophecy, that is an exposition,[note 2.20] which opens and and expounds the old Prophets. And promises blessedness to the readers, hearers, and keepers of the things that are written in this book. Blessedness comprehends the benefits of the life present, so far forth as the Lord permits them to the godly: but chiefly of the life to come. If the profit of this book hath been also spoken before in the first Sermon. And mark, that it is not enough to read or hear this book. It must be performed in deed, and kept diligently.

**Modern English:**

Lastly, the benefit of this godly work or revelation has been touched upon, so that readers and hearers might be spurred to diligence. For this book is also called a prophecy. This is because it foretells future events, and is thus prophecy in the New Testament. Moreover, a prophecy is an exposition that opens and explains the Old Prophets. It promises blessedness to those who read, hear, and keep the things written in this book. Blessedness encompasses the benefits of the present life, insofar as the Lord allows them to the faithful, but chiefly concerns the life to come. If the benefit of this book has been spoken of before in the first sermon. And note that it is not enough to simply read or hear this book; it must be put into practice and kept diligently.

---

### Paragraph 64

**Original (1561):**

> For the Lord said also in the Gospel: Blessed are they that hear the word of God and keep it. They therefore that shall frame their life after this book, are happy. For both they fly the seducing of Antichrist, abide in the faith of Christ, and live for ever more. &c.

**Modern English:**

For the Lord also said in the Gospel: Blessed are those who hear the word of God and keep it. Those therefore who shape their life according to this book are blessed. For both they avoid the deception of Antichrist, remain in the faith of Christ, and live forevermore. &c.

---

### Paragraph 65

**Original (1561):**

> And he finishes the Title with an acclamation, by the which he moves the hearers exceedingly: For the time is at hand, as though he should say: Let no man think here, that strange things, and the which concern him noth[illegible] are told here, which shall come to pass at length after man[illegible] worlds, they belong to every one of us. For they be wr[illegible] ten of matters that chiefly concern us, and even of our o[illegible] affairs. So shows he that this book is profitable for a worlds, men, and ages. God the father by his son teach[illegible] profitable things, and admonishing time enough and dew season, be praised world without end. Amen.

**Modern English:**

And he concludes the title with an exclamation, by which he greatly stirs up the listeners: For the time is at hand, as though he should say: Let no one think here that strange things, and those which do not concern him, are told here, which will come to pass at length after many ages; they belong to every one of us. For they are written of matters that chiefly concern us, and even of our own affairs. Thus he shows that this book is profitable for worlds, men, and ages. God the Father, by his Son, teaches profitable things, and admonishing in due season, be praised world without end. Amen.

---

## Sermon 3: OF THE BEGINNING OF THIS book, and the Apostles salutation: wherein are declared the mysteries chiefly of Christ, ſecondly of our whole faith and redemption.

### Paragraph 66

**Original (1561):**

> John to the seven cogregatios Asia. Grace be with you and pea[illegible] from him which is, and which[illegible] was, and which is to come, and [illegible] the seven spirits which are pr[illegible] sent before his throne. And fro Jesus Chri[illegible] which is a faithful witness, and first begot[illegible] of the dead: And Lord over the kings of t[illegible] earth. Unto him that loved us, and waſſh[illegible] us from sins in his own blood: And man[illegible] us kings and priests, unto God his fathe[illegible] be glory and dominion for evermore. Am[illegible] Behold he comes with clouds: And all ey[illegible] shall se him: And they also which pierced hi[illegible] and all kinredes of the earth shall whale over him Even so. Am. I am Alpha and Omega, the beginning and thending, saith the Lord almight[illegible] which is, and which was, and which is to come.

**Modern English:**

John to the seven congregations in Asia. Grace be with you and peace from him who is, and who was, and who is to come, and from the seven spirits which are sent before his throne. And from Jesus Christ, the faithful witness, the firstborn of the dead, and Lord over the kings of the earth. To him who loved us and washed us from our sins in his own blood, and made us kings and priests to God his Father, to him be glory and dominion forevermore. Amen. Behold, he comes with clouds, and every eye shall see him, and they also who pierced him. And all kindreds of the earth shall wail over him. Even so. Amen. I am Alpha and Omega, the beginning and the ending, says the Lord Almighty, who is, and who was, and who is to come.

---
