### Paragraph 67

**Original (1561):**

> [note 3.1]Another piece of the first part of this book, contains t[illegible] beginning or preface, wherein is the Apostles salutation, [illegible] the which he diſcribeth first the whole mystery of Christ, [illegible] condly of our faith and redemption. For so were the Apostles wont in the beginning of their writings to comprise a brief some of salutation: Which thing in Paules Epistles is every where to be scene. By the same description he gets the benevolence and attentiuenes of all men.

**Modern English:**

Another section of the first part of this book contains a beginning or preface, wherein is the Apostle’s salutation, whereby he first describes the whole mystery of Christ, and secondly of our faith and redemption. For so the Apostles were accustomed in the beginning of their writings to comprise a brief summary of salutation. This practice is evident everywhere in Paul’s Epistles. By this description, he gains the goodwill and attention of all people.

---

### Paragraph 68

**Original (1561):**

> The Apostles salutation or greting is nothing else,[note 3.2] but a blessing. Blessing is an old accustomed order, by the which the Patriarchs wished of God to their children all manner of good things, both of body and soul. Which verily in Genesis is described at large. And also the high priest had commandment given to bless the people: As we read in the sixth of Nu bres, especially he commands to put his name upon the people. Therefore it is a ſuperſticion to say God verily, from whom every good gift descends fro aboue, blesses, that is gives good things: but ministers or men wish only. And the Lord in deed in the law promises, that he will grant those things to the people, which the high priests should wish them. Therefore neither words nor shaven crowns: but the truth and power of God give the gifts. We ought not therefore to doubt, but that God will grant to us also thapoſtolical blessing, that being reconciled and accepted of God we might have peace. And first S. Iho repeteth his name, left we should any thing doubt of thautour,[note 3.3] who we see Christ to have used as scribe and interpretour unto all cogregatios. But he repeteth not himself to be that servant of god, and witness or Apostle of Jesus Christ. It sufficed to have heard that at the first beginnig. Therefore he teaches the modesty and humility also, which have obtained great gifts. Afterward he signifies, to who he writes, and to who this book appertains, to the seven churches of Asia, the names whereof he will utter shortly after. And Arethas bishop of Caesarea, by the vii. churches, saith he, and by the vii. nu bre, he signified the multitude of churches that be in all places. So also Primasius biſh. of Utica in Africa, expoudeth the vii. nu ber. Therefore this ſalutatio, this book, and the whole doctrine of Jesus Christ, writ by ſ. Iho,[note 3.4] appertains to the whole universal church of Christ throughout all the world, and in all times and ages. Wherupo it belogeth to all us also as many as be of us in the church of Christ. For albeit thepiſtles be entitled to the Romains and Galath. yet follows it not therefore, that they be not ours. And he writes expressly to the churches of Asia, not to t[illegible] churches of Jerusalem or Iewery: that he might so show[illegible] that the kingdom of Christ, is come also already to the g[illegible] tiles. And as God from the beginning chose Israel, in whi[illegible] he might set forth a perfect example of the church, and com[illegible] wealth: so fro the beginning of the new Teſtamet, he chose those[illegible] seven churches of Asia, which he might set forth to the wh[illegible] Christen world. But in case Rome had ben set in the first pla[illegible] amongst the churches, as Epheſus is, good God, ho[illegible] much wold the Roman sort make of it, for the eſtabiſhing[illegible] their supremacy.

**Modern English:**

The apostles’ salutation or greeting is nothing else, but a blessing. Blessing is an old, customary practice by which the patriarchs wished God’s good things—both of body and soul—upon their children. This is described at length in Genesis. Also, the high priest had been commanded to bless the people, as we read in Numbers 6, especially that he should put God’s name upon the people. Therefore, it is a superstition to say that God, from whom every good gift descends from above, blesses, that is, gives good things, but that ministers or men only wish. And the Lord in the law promises that he will grant those things to the people which the high priests should wish them. Therefore, neither words nor shaven crowns, but the truth and power of God give the gifts. We ought not therefore to doubt that God will grant to us also the apostolic blessing, that being reconciled and accepted of God we might have peace. And first, Saint John repeats his name, lest we should doubt of the authority who we see Christ to have used as scribe and interpreter to all congregations. But he does not repeat that he himself is that servant of God, and witness or apostle of Jesus Christ. It sufficed to have heard that at the first beginning. Therefore, he teaches modesty and humility also, which have obtained great gifts. Afterward, he signifies to whom he writes, and to whom this book pertains, to the seven churches of Asia, the names of which he will utter shortly after. And Arethas, bishop of Caesarea, by the seven churches, says he, and by the seven numbers, signified the multitude of churches that are in all places. So also Primasius, bishop of Utica in Africa, explains the seven numbers. Therefore, this salutation, this book, and the whole doctrine of Jesus Christ, written by Saint John, pertains to the whole universal church of Christ throughout all the world, and in all times and ages. Whereupon it belongs to all of us as many as are of us in the church of Christ. For although the epistles are entitled to the Romans and Galatians, it does not follow that they are not ours. And he writes expressly to the churches of Asia, not to the churches of Jerusalem or Judaea, that he might show that the kingdom of Christ is come already to the Gentiles. And as God from the beginning chose Israel, in whom he might set forth a perfect example of the church and commonwealth, so from the beginning of the new Testament, he chose those seven churches of Asia, which he might set forth to the whole Christian world. But if Rome had been set in the first place among the churches, as Ephesus is, good God, how much would the Roman sort make of it, for the establishing of their supremacy?

---

### Paragraph 69

**Original (1561):**

> [note 3.5]And the manner of the Apostles saluting wishes gran[illegible] peace. Grace is the favour of the deity, and the reconc[illegible] meant, whereby God the father for Christ his sake is made[illegible] one with us, our sins pardoned, and we adopted for his chi[illegible] dren. Thereof arriſeth the peace and tranquillity of mind[illegible] and the desire of concorde with all men.

**Modern English:**

And the manner of the Apostles’ salutations wishes grace. Grace is the favor of God, and the reconciliation meant, whereby God the Father for Christ’s sake is made one with us, our sins pardoned, and we adopted for his children. Thereof arises the peace and tranquility of mind and the desire of concord with all men.

---

### Paragraph 70

**Original (1561):**

> And here he shows abundantly, who gives the churc[illegible] his blessing, that is to wit, grace, reconcilement, and pea[illegible] God, and God three in persons, the father, the son, and th[illegible] holy ghost, one God in essence. But here he discerns th[illegible] persons very well. From him that is, to wit, the fathe[illegible] And from the ſeue spirits that is, from the holy ghost: And fro[illegible] Jesus Christ, this is the diversity of persons. And the signification of the unity is, when after the proprieties of persons d[illegible] clared,[note 3.6] he adds: I am Alpha and omega. &c. And that the ho[illegible] ghost is set here in the midst, it disorders not the mist[illegible] of the Trinity: but appears to be an argument that he [illegible] the spirit as well of the father, as of the son, and that h[illegible] proceeds from both. As it is also proved by the words [illegible] our lord, the xiiii.xv. and xvi. of John. Here is also describe[illegible] the whole wholesome mystery first of Christ, than of the catholic faith, and of our redemption, so that herein you may find[illegible] the chiefest articles of the Apostles cred, and have here a moſ[illegible] goodly deſcriptio of Christ our Lord. Hereof all me shall judge[illegible] how truly some men say,[note 3.7] that this book, contrary to the [illegible] ſtome of thapoſtles, makes little mentio of Christ and of faith

**Modern English:**

And he shows abundantly who grants the church his blessing, that is to say, grace, reconciliation, and peace—God, and God three in persons, the Father, the Son, and the Holy Ghost, one God in essence. But here he discerns the persons very well. From him that is, to wit, the Father; and from the seven spirits, that is, from the Holy Ghost; and from Jesus Christ—this is the diversity of persons. And the signification of the unity is, when after the properties of persons are declared, he adds, “I am Alpha and Omega.” &c. And that the Holy Ghost is set here in the midst, it disorders not the mystery of the Trinity, but appears to be an argument that he is the spirit as well of the Father as of the Son, and that he proceeds from both. As it is also proved by the words of our Lord, the xiv, xv, and xvi chapters of John. Here is also described the whole wholesome mystery first of Christ, then of the catholic faith, and of our redemption, so that herein you may find the chiefest articles of the Apostles’ Creed, and have here a most goodly description of Christ our Lord. Hereof all men shall judge how truly some say that this book, contrary to the common opinion of the Apostles, makes little mention of Christ and of faith.

---

### Paragraph 71

**Original (1561):**

> The father as fountain and original, of whom the son is engendered, is first described: for that it is he which is, which was, and which is to come. Those words toke John out of Moyſes in the iii. and xxxiiii. chap. of Exod. and out of many testimonies of Essay. And he saith nothing, but that God the father is an eternal enſence, which consists by and of it self, and is and gives life to all, and in all preserves the same. And that this essence is such, that it hath been always with out beginning. For this is it, that he joins to being, or existing, was. He adds, and he that shall come ([illegible]) which shall be, and shall remain even to the end, and to euerlaſtingnes without end. The Greeks derive [illegible] of running, for that conning and running, he medleth with all matters: is every where present, bringing help to the godly, or reſtreining and punishing the wicked.

**Modern English:**

The father, as source and origin, from whom the Son is generated, is first described: for it is he who is, who was, and who is to come. John took those words from Moses in the third and thirty-fourth chapter of Exodus, and from many testimonies of Isaiah. And he says nothing but that God the Father is an eternal essence, which exists by and of itself, and gives life to all, and in all preserves it. And that this essence is such that it has always existed without beginning. For this is what he joins to being, or existence; he was. He adds, and he who is to come, which shall be, and shall remain even to the end, and to everlastingness without end. The Greeks derive [illegible] from running, for that concerning and running, he meddles with all matters; is everywhere present, bringing help to the godly, or restraining and punishing the wicked.

---

### Paragraph 72

**Original (1561):**

> And the holy ghost where he is but one,[note 3.8] for the ſeuenfolde that is all manner of grace and gifts manifold, is here called, as I may say, Septenarie or of the seventh number: And from the seven spirits saith John, that is from that spirit, which is indewed with the ſeuenfolde grace. Those diverse gifts are after a sort declared of Essay in the xi. Chapter. and else where in the scriptures. He is said to be in the sight of the throne that is before the throne of God, joined verily in government with the father and the son. For the throne is many times usurped for the kingdom. The holy ghost therefore is of the same glory, power, and majesty with God.

**Modern English:**

And the Holy Spirit, where he is but one,[note 3.8] for the sevenfold—that is, all manner of grace and manifold gifts—is here called, as I may say, Septenary or of the seventh number. And from the seven spirits, John says that it is from that Spirit, which is endowed with sevenfold grace. Those diverse gifts are, in a way, explained in Essay in the eleventh chapter and elsewhere in the scriptures. He is said to be in the sight of the throne, that is, before the throne of God, joined truly in governance with the Father and the Son. For the throne is often used to represent the kingdom. Therefore, the Holy Spirit is of the same glory, power, and majesty as God.

---

### Paragraph 73

**Original (1561):**

> Now is he come to Christ,[note 3.9] whom by his properties he describes most abundantly. You know, that Jesus is the proper name of Christ: which Matthew expounds, a Saviour, Christ is the surname of his office and dignity: as you would say, anointed, that is, bishop and king.

**Modern English:**

Now he has come to Christ, whom by his attributes he describes most abundantly. You know that Jesus is the proper name of Christ, which Matthew explains: a Savior. Christ is the surname of his office and dignity, as if you were to say, “anointed,” that is, bishop and king.

---

### Paragraph 74

**Original (1561):**

> 1 First he calls Christ our Lord, a faithful wytnes,[note 3.10] and that out of the xlix. and l. chapter of Essay. For he was sent of the father to the world out of heaven, an Apostle, which should testify the will of God, what he wold have done with men. To wit that he would save the world by his son,[note 3.11] and by faith in him, which is obedient to the law of God. For he must do the will of his father. This Christ is a faithful witness, that is sure, constant and true: Of whose doctrine no man ought to doubt. No man hath seen God at any time: The only begotten which is in the bosom of the father,[note 3.12] he hath revealed. This therefore is the bishop and catholic d[illegible] tour of the church. Who so ever dissent from him, are to [illegible] eschewed. Hear him, saith the father.

**Modern English:**

First he calls Christ our Lord a faithful witness, as in chapters 49 and 50 of Isaiah. For he was sent by the Father to the world from heaven, an Apostle, who should testify the will of God, what he would have done with men. Namely, that he would save the world by his Son, and by faith in him, which is obedient to the law of God. For he must do the will of his Father. This Christ is a faithful witness, that is sure, constant, and true: of whose doctrine no man ought to doubt. No man has seen God at any time; the only-begotten Son, who is in the Father’s bosom, has revealed him. This, therefore, is the doctrine of the church. Whoever dissent from him are to be eschewed. Hear him, says the Father.

---

### Paragraph 75

**Original (1561):**

> [note 3.13]2 He is the first begotten of the dead. For he died for o[illegible] sins verily: and rose again from the dead, and was made t[illegible] first begotten of the dead, Lord and conqueror of death: I[illegible] whom we se that we shall also rise again, and in what sort. [illegible] whom the first of the Corinth. xv. And like as in the first pr[illegible] pertie he ſhaddoweth the humanity of Christ, wherein h[illegible] taught also his deity, in that he was the faithful, true, and [illegible] tholique bishop, and is yet at this day: So in the second, th[illegible] articles of our belefe concerning the death of Christ, and h[illegible] resurrection are confirmed. To these also may be added t[illegible] article of the resurrection of the dead.

**Modern English:**

He is the firstborn of the dead. For he truly died for our sins, and rose again from the dead, and was made the firstborn of the dead, Lord and conqueror of death; in him we see that we also shall rise again, and in what manner. As it is written in 1 Corinthians 15. And just as in the first part he shadows the humanity of Christ, wherein he also taught his deity, in that he was the faithful, true, and catholic bishop, and is yet at this day; so in the second, the articles of our belief concerning the death of Christ and his resurrection are confirmed. To these may also be added the article of the resurrection of the dead.

---

### Paragraph 76

**Original (1561):**

> [note 3.14]3 Christ is prince over the kings of the earth, a monar[illegible] verily, and Lord of all rulers: Which hath taken a name [illegible] above all names, the Lord of angels, and of all creatures, [illegible] whom all things be subject: As the Apostle expoudeth. Coloſ[illegible] Philip. 2. And he doth not abolish laws and Magiſtrat[illegible] which will be king of kings, and Lord of lords. For if than[illegible] were no kings, how should Christ be king of kings? The mo[illegible] sacred Emperors, Constance, Constantine, Theodoſe, an[illegible] Iuſtinian, knew them selves to be clients of Christ: than[illegible] kingdom was Christ's, and they to be subjects. These Chri[illegible] acknowledges for his, by who he governs those he ha[illegible] redeemed with his blood. They that proudly rule over the people, boast the selves to be lords of althings, and acknowled[illegible] not Christ to be monarch over all, be stark mad. And her[illegible] are comprehended such things as we confess in the articles of our faith, that Christ ascended into heaven, and sits on th[illegible] right hand of the father: that is, that he hath received high p[illegible] were of all things in heaven and earth. Ephe. 1. Act. 2.

**Modern English:**

3 Christ is prince over the kings of the earth, truly a monarch, and Lord of all rulers: He has taken a name above all names, the Lord of angels and of all creatures, to whom all things are subject, as the Apostle expounds. Colossians and Philippians 2. And he does not abolish laws and magistrates who will be king of kings and Lord of lords. For if there were no kings, how could Christ be king of kings? The most sacred Emperors, Constantine, Theodosius, and Justinian, knew themselves to be clients of Christ: their kingdom was Christ's, and they were to be subjects. Christ acknowledges these as his, by whom he governs those he has redeemed with his blood. Those who proudly rule over the people, boast themselves to be lords of all things, and do not acknowledge Christ to be monarch over all, are stark mad. And here are comprehended such things as we confess in the articles of our faith, that Christ ascended into heaven and sits on the right hand of the Father: that is, that he has received the highest power over all things in heaven and earth. Ephesians 1. Acts 2.

---

### Paragraph 77

**Original (1561):**

> [note 3.15]4 Christ hath loved us with incomparable love. For he hi[illegible] self saith: greater love hath no man, than that a man should[illegible] leave his life for his friends. This love the Apostle amplefiet[illegible] in the fifth to the Romains. And it was exceading great love [illegible] moved Christ to come down from heaven and be incarna[illegible] and to redeem us by his death. With a free love he love[illegible] us, provoked by no desert of ours. For as this same John in his Epistle canonical speaks the same of the father. In this is charity, not that we have loved God, but that he hath loved us, and sent his son a propiciation for our sins: So is it to be understand of the son, the he hath and doth bear us great good will, not moved thereto through our love, wherewith we have embraced him. And of the free love to mankind, he gave him self unto death, and washed us fro our sins. For straight ways is added, by his blood.[note 3.16] Where three things seem of us to be observed. First that Christ washes, purges, purifieth or cleſeth the faithful: and that most fully, not partly. He alluded to the waſhings of the law, which he expouded also. For David saith: Pourge me with Hyſope, and I shall be made clean, wash me, and I shall be whiter than snow. The same phrase of speech repeteth Essay in the first chap. Micheas also saith:[note 3.17] The Lord will return and will have mercy on us, he will tread under fete our iniquities: And thou shalt throw into the depth of the Sea all their sins. And the Lord saith,[note 3.18] I will cast upon you clean waters, and you shall be modified from all your filthines. The Lord Christ these things accomplishing, washes us, purges and cleanses thoroughly, as well from the falte as the pain. He cleanses us from our sins,[note 3.19] not from one, but from all. The which thing is proved both by former testimonies, and again in the first and second Epistle of S. John. Last, the manner also of purifiyng is set forth, by blood. For without the ſheding of blood no remission was made. Therefore through the mediation of death and bloudſheding there was full remiſſio of all sins obtained for the faithful.[note 3.20] They that bring forth any other manner of forgeuenes of sins, are injurious to the death and blood of the son of God. And here we may se plainly set forth an article of the Apostolical cred. I believe the forgeuenes of sins.

**Modern English:**

Christ has loved us with incomparable love. For he himself says: “Greater love has no one than that someone should lay down his life for his friends.” The Apostle amplifies this in the fifth chapter to the Romans. It was exceedingly great love that moved Christ to come down from heaven and be incarnate and to redeem us by his death. With a free love he loved us, provoked by no merit of ours. For as John in his canonical Epistle speaks the same of the Father: “In this is love, not that we have loved God, but that he has loved us and sent his son as an atoning sacrifice for our sins,” so it is to be understood of the Son, that he has and does bear us great good will, not moved thereto through our love, with which we have embraced him. And of the free love toward mankind, he gave himself unto death and washed us from our sins. For it is straightway added, by his blood.

Where three things seem to be observed concerning us. First, that Christ washes, purges, purifies, or cleanses the faithful: and that most fully, not partly. He alludes to the washings of the law, which he also expounded. For David says: “Purge me with hyssop, and I shall be made clean; wash me, and I shall be whiter than snow.” The same phrase of speech is repeated by Essay in the first chapter. Micah also says: “The Lord will return and will have mercy on us; he will trample underfoot our iniquities. And you will throw into the depths of the sea all their sins.” And the Lord says: “I will pour clean water upon you, and you will be cleansed from all your filthiness.” The Lord Christ accomplishing these things, washes us, purges, and cleanses thoroughly, as well from the fault as the pain. He cleanses us from our sins, not from one, but from all. This which thing is proved both by former testimonies, and again in the first and second Epistle of John. Lastly, the manner of purifying is set forth, by blood. For without the shedding of blood, no remission was made. Therefore through the mediation of death and bloodshedding, full remission of all sins was obtained for the faithful.

They who bring forth any other manner of forgiveness of sins, are injurious to the death and blood of the son of God. And here we may plainly see set forth an article of the Apostolic creed: I believe the forgiveness of sins.

---

### Paragraph 78

**Original (1561):**

> In the fifth place is showed theffect of our redeption and purifiyng. For Christ hath brought to pass,[note 3.21] that as many of us as believe in the father by the son of God, should be kings and priſts to God and to his father. Arethas and the copy of complute read not kings but [illegible], that is kingdom: the which is not red amiss. For we be the kingdo of God, for because God by his spirit, not the flesh nor the world, ought to reign in us: And when we permit the government to the spirit, we be the kingdo of God: The which thing S. Paul handles at large in the sixth chap. to the Romains. Moreover we be made kings that is free,[note 3.22] by Christ, that we should not serve the devil, th[illegible] flesh, and the world, according to that saying of Zacharie, [illegible] being delivered from the hands of our enemies, we migh[illegible] serve him without fear in holiness and rightuouſnes befo[illegible] him all the days of our life.[note 3.23] And Christ hath consecrated v[illegible] priests with his spirit and blood, that we should offer up t[illegible] God spiritual sacrifices, our selves pure, prayers and pra[illegible] ſes,[note 3.24] and almoſdedes. For that these be spiritual oblation[illegible] Peter and Paul do testify. And these things toke S. Ioh[illegible] out of Exodus: For we of the Gentiles that have believe[illegible] have succeeded in the place of the people of Israel reiecti[illegible] Christ through incredulity. And these things give a lyg[illegible] to that article of the Cred, I believe the holy catholic church the communion of saints. For we be as many of us as b[illegible] leave, the fellowship of Gods people, sanctified through Chris[illegible] to the service of God. Of whom be these things hitherto.

**Modern English:**

In the fifth place, the effect of our redemption and purification is shown. For Christ has brought it to pass that as many of us as believe in the Father through the Son of God should be kings and priests to God and to his Father. Arethas and the copy of Complutum read not “kings” but “kingdom,” which is not read amiss. For we are the kingdom of God, because God by his Spirit, not the flesh nor the world, ought to reign in us. And when we submit to the Spirit’s governance, we are the kingdom of God. Paul discusses this at length in the sixth chapter to the Romans. Moreover, we are made kings—that is, free—by Christ, so that we should not serve the devil, the flesh, and the world, according to that saying of Zechariah, being delivered from the hands of our enemies, that we might serve him without fear in holiness and righteousness before him all the days of our life. And Christ has consecrated priests with his Spirit and blood, that we should offer up to God spiritual sacrifices, ourselves pure, prayers and praises, and alms. For Peter and Paul testify that these are spiritual oblations. John took these things from Exodus: For we of the Gentiles who have believed have succeeded in the place of the people of Israel, rejected Christ through unbelief. And these things give light to that article of the Creed, “I believe in the holy catholic church, the communion of saints.” For we, as many of us as believe, are the fellowship of God’s people, sanctified through Christ to the service of God. Of whom are these things said?

---

### Paragraph 79

**Original (1561):**

> In the sixth place, in the diſcriptio of Christ he shows the glory and rule is dew unto God alone through Christ [illegible] the church for evermore:[note 3.25] We give glory unto God, wh[illegible] we ascribe to his goodness our salvation, and all goodne[illegible] not to our own strength and merits. We give him ru[illegible] when we acknowledge him to be Lord and head in the churc[illegible] working by him self, not by the saints in heaven, to wh[illegible] he hath granted power: Not by the Pope, whom he ha[illegible] constitute Vicar in earth. The whole glory and rule is Christs

**Modern English:**

In the sixth place, in the description of Christ, he shows that the glory and rule are due to God alone through Christ, for the church forevermore. We give glory to God when we ascribe our salvation and all goodness to his goodness, not to our own strength and merits. We give him rule when we acknowledge him to be Lord and head in the church, working by himself, not by the saints in heaven, to whom he has granted power. Not by the Pope, whom he has not constituted Vicar on earth. The whole glory and rule is Christ’s.

---

### Paragraph 80

**Original (1561):**

> Seventhly in the description follows the coming [illegible] Christ unto judgement,[note 3.26] and the manner of his coming. F[illegible] as a cloud toke him up from the eyes of the Apostles, e[illegible] so shall he come in clouds to judge the quick and the dea[illegible] The scripture witnessing. And he adds, that the eyes of a[illegible] men shall se the judge,[note 3.27] even of those which have peaſed him Whereof we gather two things, first, that the judgement sha[illegible] be universal. Wherein men arriſing shall se Christ with th[illegible] own eyes. An other thing, that Christ shall come to judge[illegible] meant in the same flesh,[note 3.28] in the which he was wounded and ſticked, honge upon the Cross, was buried and rose again This place is taken out of Zacharie, and is cited also in S. Iohns Gospel.[note 3.29] And it behooves that his body be showed to the whole world full of printes and marks, that hereof may be judged the Godly and also the ungodly: They that then have believed in such a redemer: These, that then have rejected and contemned such a one. Of these we understand that is added: And they shall whale, for that in deed they have neglected their own salvation: Which the wise man discourses at large.[note 3.30] Moreover lest any may should doubt of those things that are spoken of the judgment, and of the lamentation of the wicked (as S. Peter said,[note 3.31] the contemners and mockers of the judgment should be) he adds a kind of a confirmation, even so. Amen.

**Modern English:**

Seventhly in the description follows the coming of Christ unto judgment, and the manner of his coming. As a cloud took him up from the eyes of the Apostles, so shall he come in clouds to judge the living and the dead. The scripture witnesses. And he adds that the eyes of all men shall see the judge, even of those who have pleased him. Whereof we gather two things, first, that the judgment shall be universal, wherein men arising shall see Christ with their own eyes. Another thing, that Christ shall come to judge in the same flesh in which he was wounded and pierced, hung upon the cross, was buried and rose again. This place is taken out of Zachariah, and is cited also in Saint John’s Gospel. And it behooves that his body be showed to the whole world full of prints and marks, that hereof may be judged the godly and also the ungodly: they that then have believed in such a redeemer, these that then have rejected and contemned such a one. Of these we understand that is added: And they shall wail, for that in deed they have neglected their own salvation, which the wise man discourses at large. Moreover, lest any should doubt of those things that are spoken of the judgment, and of the lamentation of the wicked (as Saint Peter said, the contemners and mockers of the judgment should be), he adds a kind of confirmation, even so. Amen.

---

### Paragraph 81

**Original (1561):**

> In them also is expouded the article of the cred of Christ that shall judge the quick and the dead. He concludes this place with these words: I am Alpha and Omega, that which follows (the beginning and end) is omitted in some copies: As though that interpretation of that same, I am Alpha and Omega, crept in out of the margent. It is a proverb of S. John the Apostle, I am Alpha and Omega. Heretics, as Baſilides and Valentine, were wonderfully delighted in letters. But against those lettered Heretics John speaks plainly by the mouth of Christ. I am Alpha and Omega. If any thing ought to be ascribed to letters, I am all this whole that everlasting virtue, essence, and eternity. For the sense is, that God is the beginning and end, that is eternal, unspeakable, best and greatest. Those things are repeated: He that is, which was. &c. Which were expouded before: There is added, almighty. For hereby is declared the unity and majesty of God, of whom the Trinity was opened also before. Hereby also the authority of this book is confirmed, the author whereof is showed to be that God eternal and almighty. To whom be glory.

**Modern English:**

In them also is explained the article of the creed of Christ who will judge the living and the dead. He concludes this passage with these words: “I am Alpha and Omega,” with “that which follows (the beginning and end)” omitted in some copies. It seems that interpretation of that same, “I am Alpha and Omega,” crept in from the margin. It is a saying of Saint John the Apostle, “I am Alpha and Omega.” Heretics, such as Basilides and Valentine, were greatly delighted in letters. But against those lettered heretics, John speaks plainly by the mouth of Christ: “I am Alpha and Omega.” If anything ought to be ascribed to letters, I am all this whole everlasting virtue, essence, and eternity. For the sense is, that God is the beginning and end, that is, eternal, unspeakable, best, and greatest. Those things are repeated: “He that is, which was,” and so forth, which were explained before. There is added, “almighty.” For hereby is declared the unity and majesty of God, of whom the Trinity was opened also before. Hereby also the authority of this book is confirmed, the author of which is shown to be that God eternal and almighty. To whom be glory.

---

## Sermon 4: ¶ Of the Narration of this book, where also is discoursed of the place and time, and of the author of this Revelation.

### Paragraph 82

**Original (1561):**

> I John your brother and companion in tribulation, and in the kingdo and patiece which is in Jesus Christ, was in the Isle of Pathmos for the word of God, and testimony of Ieſ[illegible] Christ. I was in the spirit on the Sunday and heard behind me a great voice, as it ha[illegible] been of a trompe, saying: I am Alpha and Omega, the first and the last. That thou see[illegible] write in a book, and send it to the congregations which are in Asia, unto Epheſus, an[illegible] unto Smyrna, and unto Pergamos, and Thyatire, and unto Sardis, and unto Philade[illegible] phia, and unto Laodicia.

**Modern English:**

I, John, your brother and companion in tribulation, and in the kingdom and patience which are in Jesus Christ, was on the island of Patmos for the word of God and for the testimony of Jesus Christ. I was in the spirit on the Lord’s Day and heard behind me a great voice, as if of a trumpet, saying, “I am Alpha and Omega, the first and the last.” And I saw another vision, and it was commanded that I write in a book and send it to the congregations which are in Asia, to Ephesus, and to Smyrna, and to Pergamum, and to Thyatira, and to Sardis, and to Philadelphia, and to Laodicea.

---

### Paragraph 83

**Original (1561):**

> [note 4.1]The last place of the first shows unto us a brief narrati[illegible] wherein the Apostle S. John declares the time and place this Revelation, and by whose commandment he wrou[illegible] sent the same to the seven churches in Asia.

**Modern English:**

The last section of the first book shows us a brief account wherein the Apostle John declares the time and place of this Revelation, and by whose authority he wrote and sent it to the seven churches in Asia.

---

### Paragraph 84

**Original (1561):**

> And again now the third time is the name of John rehea[illegible] ſed. He saw undoubtedly, that there would be some which to the end they might take away the use and fruit of th[illegible] book, wold doubt of the author: Against whom he repeat[illegible] and reiterateth his name so oft, lest we should doubt and lac[illegible] the great commodity of so worthy a book.

**Modern English:**

And again now, for the third time, the name of John is repeated. He saw undoubtedly that there would be some who, to the end they might take away the use and benefit of this book, would question the author. Against whom he repeats and reiterates his name so often, lest we should doubt and lose the great value of so worthy a book.

---

### Paragraph 85

**Original (1561):**

> [note 4.2]He adds to his name certain things, which instruct[illegible] touching the state of the Apostle, and certain profitable man[illegible] ters. First he calls him self a brother, namely of those ſ[illegible] churches, and of all ours: As where I have admonished yo[illegible] that in the seventh number are comprised all churches of [illegible] times throughout the whole world. We are all, so many [illegible] believe, the children of one heavenly father: And therefore all spiritual brethren in Christ, coinheriters with Christ, and heirs of God. Which thing S. Paul taught after Christ.[note 4.3] And seeing our dignity is so great, let us ones be ashamed of our miſdedes, least our memory be put out of this most noble and celestial family. It is a shame the brother of Christ, of S. John and all the Apostles should degenerate. &c. But why have not they so instantly urged this brotherhood, as the Monks have beaten in their forged fraternities, the Rosaries of the virgin Mary and of Saints? Because that was free, and cost nothing: But the Monks sell theirs dere. They be therefore deceivers and seducers.

**Modern English:**

He adds certain things to his name, which instruct concerning the state of the Apostle, and certain profitable matters. First, he calls himself a brother, namely of those churches, and of all ours. As where I have admonished you that in the seventh number are comprised all churches of all times throughout the whole world. We are all, so many believe, the children of one heavenly father. And therefore all spiritual brethren in Christ, coheirs with Christ, and heirs of God. Which thing Paul taught after Christ.

And seeing our dignity is so great, let us once be ashamed of our misdeeds, lest our memory be put out of this most noble and celestial family. It is a shame that a brother of Christ, of John and all the Apostles should degenerate. And so on.

But why have they not so instantly urged this brotherhood, as the Monks have promoted their forged fraternities, the Rosaries of the Virgin Mary and of Saints? Because that was free, and cost nothing. But the Monks sell theirs dear. They are therefore deceivers and seducers.

---

### Paragraph 86

**Original (1561):**

> After he calls him self partaker in affliction, or oppression and persecution, as he that was even now banished by the Emperor Domitian, and lived in exile. And he joins together and not ſeparateth him self in the evil: That is co mon to all the faithful brethren.[note 4.4] And verily it is one and the same persecution, that vexed the Apostles, and tourmenteth us at this day. Let us therefore rejoice, that we have the Apostles and all the Martyrs of Christ fellows of our trouble and affliction, that we be broken and bruised with the heavy burden of evils. Let us therefore be paciet and long suffering. For it is not enough to be afflicted, and vexed with all kind of evils (for many without any fruicte or praise at all endure most grievous pains.) But it becomes us also to be patient in adversity. Therefore S. John at this present joins with all, patince. For the Lord said in the Gospel. In your patience shall you possess your souls.

**Modern English:**

After he calls himself a partaker in affliction, or oppression and persecution, as he who was even now banished by the Emperor Domitian and lived in exile. And he joins together and does not separate himself in the evil: that is common to all the faithful brethren. And truly it is one and the same persecution that vexed the Apostles and torments us at this day. Let us therefore rejoice that we have the Apostles and all the Martyrs of Christ as fellows in our trouble and affliction, that we are broken and bruised with the heavy burden of evils. Let us therefore be patient and long-suffering. For it is not enough to be afflicted and vexed with every kind of evil (for many without any fruit or praise at all endure most grievous pains). But it becomes us also to be patient in adversity. Therefore John at this present joins with all, patience. For the Lord said in the Gospel, “In your patience you shall possess your souls.”

---

### Paragraph 87

**Original (1561):**

> After he adds unto tribulation and patience a kingdom, and that an heavenly not a terrestrial kingdom.[note 4.5] And he brings in the kingdom for the comfort of the patient people. For also the Apostle S. Paul said, a certain and sure saying. For if we die with Christ, we shall live also with him: If we suffer, we shall reygne with him. &c. Let us always here with comfort our selves in adversity. For we are thrust down, that we might ones be exalted again ii. Corinthians iiii. And all these things are concluded in Christ Jesus, by who we be both the children and brethren of God, and suffer many things patiently, and are made partakers of his kingdom. For even for these things must we thank him, and his m[illegible] rites, and not our own desert.

**Modern English:**

After he adds unto tribulation and patience a kingdom, and that an heavenly not a terrestrial kingdom.[note 4.5] And he brings in the kingdom for the comfort of the patient people. For also the Apostle Paul said, a certain and sure saying. For if we die with Christ, we shall live also with him: If we suffer, we shall reign with him. &c. Let us always here with comfort our selves in adversity. For we are brought low, that we might once be exalted again, 2 Corinthians 4. And all these things are concluded in Christ Jesus, by whom we are both the children and brethren of God, and suffer many things patiently, and are made partakers of his kingdom. For even for these things must we thank him and his mercies, and not our own merit.

---

### Paragraph 88

**Original (1561):**

> [note 4.6]Let us here note also, what and howe great hath been the humility of the greatest and worthy Apostle of God, wh[illegible] was his state: Not pleasant, but harde, yet in the patience [illegible] Christ joyful. But where be they now, that glory in th[illegible] name of Apostles: Who in the mean time swelling wit[illegible] pride, are addict to filthy pleasures? Which I warn, th[illegible] we flee from them, as from Apoſtataes.

**Modern English:**

Let us here note also what and how great has been the humility of the greatest and worthy Apostle of God, considering his state: Not pleasant, but hard, yet in the patience of Christ joyful. But where are they now, those who glory in the name of Apostles? Who, meanwhile, swelling with pride, are addicted to filthy pleasures? I warn you, we must flee from them as from apostates.

---

### Paragraph 89

**Original (1561):**

> [note 4.7]And now he shows the place, where this divine revelation was made him, where also he was commanded of Go[illegible] to write the same. The place was the Isle of Patmos. Th[illegible] same is accounted amongst the Ilondes called Sporad[illegible] of Plinie in the fourth book and xii. cha. It lay over again[illegible] Asia, and the city of Epheſus, and was in thu sight boeth [illegible] Europe and Affricke, so that it seemed to be as it were a mi[illegible] dl seat, or holy chair, out of the which Christ preached [illegible] John from heaven to the whole world. And in deed the co[illegible] sells of God are wonderful, and his goodness is vnſpekeable, which reveals so great mysteries, as it were in th[illegible] Roman prison or Babilonicall captivity, to his faithful.

**Modern English:**

And now he shows the place where this divine revelation was made to him, where also he was commanded by God to write the same. The place was the Isle of Patmos. This island is accounted among the islands called Sporades by Pliny in the fourth book and twelfth chapter. It lay opposite Asia and the city of Ephesus, and was in the sight both of Europe and Africa, so that it seemed to be as it were a middle seat, or holy chair, out of which Christ preached through John from heaven to the whole world. And indeed the councils of God are wonderful, and his goodness is unspeakable, which reveals so great mysteries, as it were in the Roman prison or Babylonian captivity, to his faithful.

---

### Paragraph 90

**Original (1561):**

> [note 4.8]Neither hides he the cause of his coming into the sam[illegible] Island, I was there, says he, for the word of God, and th[illegible] testimony of Jesus Christ. The word of God is the very so[illegible] of God, called of John by a singular propriety of speech the word or ſermon of God, as appears the first of John and the testimony of Jesus Christ is the Gospel if self, which[illegible] Jesus testified, and the which his disciples have testified o[illegible] Jesus. Therefore for the confession and preaching of Ieſ[illegible] Christ, and of his wholesome Gospel (for so he expoundet[illegible] also how he is made partaker of thafflictio) John was appr[illegible] hended in Asia, and by soldiers led to Rome, that he might plea[illegible] his cause before the emperor Domitian, who of his cruel nature condened the inuocet. And he was put into a cawdro o[illegible] hot boiling oil. Out of the which when he escaped withou[illegible] harm, he was carried into Patmos. He answered no other matter before the emperor, than Paul did.27. years past, before Nero. This was done in that xiiii. or xv. year of Domitia And the xxiiii. year after the destruction of the city Hieruſale and after the birth of our Lord lxvi. Domician who would seem and be called a God, being slain of his own men, after many murders and cruel acts, died himself a shameful death, the xv. year of his reign. The authors hereof are, Suetonius in the life of Domitian, Tertullian in the heretic. prescript. Eusebius in his chronicles, and in the third book of the ecclesiastical history, in the xvii. and xviii. chapter. And hereto is added the common consent of all writers.

**Modern English:**

Neither does he hide the cause of his coming into the island, saying, “I was there for the word of God and the testimony of Jesus Christ.” The word of God is the very voice of God, called by John with a unique expression the word or sermon of God, as appears in the first chapter of John. The testimony of Jesus Christ is the Gospel itself, which Jesus testified and which his disciples testified concerning Jesus. Therefore, for the confession and preaching of Jesus Christ and his wholesome Gospel (for so he explained how he became a partaker of affliction), John was apprehended in Asia and led by soldiers to Rome, that he might plead his cause before Emperor Domitian, who, by his cruel nature, condemned the innocent. And he was put into a cauldron of hot boiling oil. When he escaped unharmed from that, he was carried to Patmos. He answered no other matter before the emperor than Paul did twenty-seven years before, under Nero. This occurred in the fourteenth or fifteenth year of Domitian and twenty-four years after the destruction of the city of Jerusalem and sixty-six years after the birth of our Lord. Domitian, who sought to be called a god, was slain by his own men after many murders and cruel acts, and died himself a shameful death in the fifteenth year of his reign. The authors of this account are Suetonius in the life of Domitian, Tertullian in *The Prescriptions Against Heretics*, Eusebius in his chronicles and in the third book of the ecclesiastical history, chapters seventeen and eighteen. And to this is added the common consent of all writers.

---

### Paragraph 91

**Original (1561):**

> Moreover he notes the time also,[note 4.9] in the which these mysteries began to be revealed to him, [illegible], in that solemn day of the Lord, namely the sunday. For so have the ancient fathers called one of the ſabbothes, that is to say the first day in the weke, wherein Christ rose again fro the dead. Math. xxviii. and Mark xvi. And this day have the churches chosen to them selves in stead of the Sabboth day, as holy in the remebrauce of the Lords reſurrectio, wherein they might keep their sacred and solemn aſſemblees. For that this day was solemnised and coſecrated for aſſemblees in the congregation of Corinthe, appears manifestly in the xvi. Chapter of the first Epistle to the Corintians, where the Apostle commands to lay a part their collections in one of the ſabbothes: The same day also the faithful did celebrate their service with S. Paul, in the xx. of the Acts. Where Sozomenus reports in the viii. Chapter. of the first book of the story tripertite, that great Constantine made certain holy days, and even the Lords day for one, which is called of the Heathen the sunday, it is to be understand that he renewed rather the custom of the Apostles and catholic church, than to have newly instituted the same. And frely of their own accord have the churches received that day: for we read not that it was any where commanded. And the congregations saw how it was altogether necessary that there should be a certain time, in the which the saints should mete and come together. They chose therefore the day of the resurrection, nether did they maliciously contend among themselves for these things, as the histories testify was done in the church afterward. And at this day verily, the superstitious holy days being abrogated, it is better to observe certain and moderat days, and to keep peace and quietness in the church.

**Modern English:**

Moreover he notes the time also, [note 4.9], in which these mysteries began to be revealed to him, [illegible], in that solemn day of the Lord, namely Sunday. For so have the ancient fathers called one of the Sabbaths, that is to say the first day of the week, wherein Christ rose again from the dead, as in Matthew xxviii and Mark xvi. And this day have the churches chosen for themselves in place of the Sabbath day, as holy in remembrance of the Lord’s resurrection, wherein they might keep their sacred and solemn assemblies. For that this day was solemnized and consecrated for assemblies in the congregation of Corinth, it appears manifestly in the sixteenth chapter of the first Epistle to the Corinthians, where the Apostle commands to lay apart their collections on one of the Sabbaths. The same day also the faithful did celebrate their service with Paul, in the twentieth chapter of the Acts. Where Sozomenus reports in the eighth chapter of the first book of the *ecclesiastical history*, that great Constantine made certain holy days, and even the Lord’s day, which is called by the Heathen Sunday, it is to be understood that he renewed rather the custom of the Apostles and catholic church, than to have newly instituted the same. And freely of their own accord have the churches received that day; for we read not that it was anywhere commanded. And the congregations saw how it was altogether necessary that there should be a certain time in which the saints should meet and come together. They chose therefore the day of the resurrection, neither did they maliciously contend among themselves for these things, as the histories testify was done in the church afterward. And at this day verily, the superstitious holy days being abrogated, it is better to observe certain and moderate days, and to keep peace and quietness in the church.

---

### Paragraph 92

**Original (1561):**

> But where this Apostle knew, that the faithful on the so[illegible] day served God in all aſſemblees, where he could not be p[illegible] sent in body,[note 4.10] in spirit and contemplation he was with the[illegible] And as he was thus in the spirit and contemplation of m[illegible] ters divine, and in holy prayers, he heard a voice, whereof [illegible] will speak hereafter. But here we are presently taugh[illegible] what is the religion of the sunday, and how it is mete to o[illegible] serve it. Finally wordly men are reproved, which pollut[illegible] break it with profane works and affairs. David w[illegible] time he suffered persecution of Saul, laments chiefly, t[illegible] he might not come to the Lords tabernacle. Our men a[illegible] count it a great felicity, never to enter into the felloſhip Saints. And to abuse the sunday, in gamenyng, drinkin[illegible] dancing, and worldly business.

**Modern English:**

But where this Apostle knew that the faithful on that day served God in all assemblies, where he could not be present in body, in spirit and contemplation he was with them. And as he was thus in the spirit and contemplation of divine matters, and in holy prayers, he heard a voice, of which I will speak hereafter. But here we are presently taught what is the religion of the Sunday, and how it is fitting to observe it. Finally, worldly men are rebuked, who pollute and break it with profane works and affairs. David, in his time, suffered persecution from Saul, lamented chiefly that he might not come to the Lord’s tabernacle. Our people, however, count it a great felicity never to enter into the fellowship of the Saints. And to abuse the Sunday, in gaming, drinking, dancing, and worldly business.

---

### Paragraph 93

**Original (1561):**

> [note 4.11]These things on this wise declared, he comes at len[illegible] to the revelation: setting forth before the express commau[illegible] dement of God, whereby he was commanded both to w[illegible] the things [illegible]euealed, and also to send the to the seven church[illegible] of Asia. To the manner and majesty of the reuelatio that sa[illegible] chiefly appertains, that he heard a voice, and that notab[illegible] as the sound of a trompet. For so we read it was done the law giving at the mount Sinay. Now is declared who voice it was, and who was the author of the revelation Verily the eternal God, which calls him self Alpha [illegible] Omega, that is the beginning and the end: Or as it is sa[illegible] in Essay, first and last. Whereof else where.

**Modern English:**

These things declared in this manner, he comes now to the Revelation, setting forth before the clear commandment of God, by which he was commanded both to write the things revealed and also to send them to the seven churches of Asia. What chiefly pertains to the manner and majesty of the Revelation is that he heard a voice, and that as the sound of a trumpet. For so we read it was done in giving the law at Mount Sinai. Now it is declared who that voice was, and who the author of the Revelation. Truly, it was the eternal God, who calls himself Alpha and Omega, that is, the beginning and the end; or as it is said in Essay, first and last. Of this elsewhere.

---

### Paragraph 94

**Original (1561):**

> Now follows the commandment which hath [illegible] parts. For first the Lord commands S. John to w[illegible] And to write such things as he saw, that is to wit, [illegible] Apocalypse. And that he should write neither in the san[illegible] nor on the wall, but in a book: Verily for the edifiyng[illegible] profit of the church present, and of all posterity. After he[illegible] also commanded to send those writings to seven cong[illegible] gations, and verily to all the churches of the whole world[illegible] all times and ages. Therefore all these things belong to the pro[illegible] of congregations, and that of all that be, have been or shall[illegible]

**Modern English:**

The commandment which has several parts follows. First, the Lord commands John to see and to write the things he saw, that is to say, the Apocalypse. And he should write neither with the sand nor on the wall, but in a book: truly for the edification and profit of the church present, and of all posterity. Afterward, he also commanded to send those writings to seven congregations, and truly to all the churches of the whole world for all times and ages. Therefore, all these things belong to the provision of congregations, and that of all that be, have been, or shall be.

---

### Paragraph 95

**Original (1561):**

> [note 4.12]Here of we learn, how great is the authority of the ſ[illegible] turs. It was not written nor compiled in books, but by Go[illegible] comaundment. There be notable testimonies of the bok[illegible] of Moyſes, in the xxxiiii. of Exod. and xxxi. of Deuter. And to say nothing of the residue of the Propetes, is not Ieremy commanded to write his Sermons again, which king Ioachim had cut in pieces and burnt?[note 4.13] Doubts S. Peter bears manifest witness, that the Prophets received the mysteries of God to none other end, than they should reveal them to us: Which in deed might only be done by the scriptures. Now is John most apertly commanded to write. What will we say, that he is also commanded to send his wrytinges to the congregations? Whereof again we gather that God wills right well to the congregations, and even to every one of us. Let us beware and take heed, that we put not from us unworthily so great benefits of God, to who be praise and glory.

**Modern English:**

Here we learn how great is the authority of the scriptures. It was not written nor compiled in books, but by God’s commandment. There are notable testimonies of the book of Moses, in the thirty-fourth chapter of Exodus and the thirty-first chapter of Deuteronomy. And to say nothing of the rest of the prophets, was not Jeremiah commanded to write his sermons again, which King Jehoiakim had cut in pieces and burnt? Doubtless Saint Peter bears witness that the prophets received the mysteries of God to no other end than that they should reveal them to us; which indeed could only be done by the scriptures. Now John is most clearly commanded to write. What shall we say, that he is also commanded to send his writings to the congregations? From this we gather again that God wills right well for the congregations, and even for every one of us. Let us beware and take heed that we do not unworthily reject so great benefits of God, to whom be praise and glory.

---

## Sermon 5: ¶ THE BEGINNING OF THE work is made, and a most goodly description to us exhibited of Christ king and bishop in glory, and nevertheless woorkyng in the Church.

### Paragraph 96

**Original (1561):**

> ANd I turned me, that I might see the voice that spake with me. And when I was turned, I saw seven golden candelſtickes: And in the midst of the seven candelſtickes, one like unto the son of man clothed with a linen garment [illegible]own to the ground, and gyrde about the paps with a golden girdell. His head and his ears were white, as white wolle and [illegible]now: And his eyes were as a flame of fire, [illegible]nd his feet like unto brass, as though they brunt in a fournace: and his voice as the sound of many waters. And he had in his right and seven Stars, and out of his mouth went out a sharpe two edged sword, and hi[illegible] face shone even as the Sun in his strength.

**Modern English:**

And I turned myself to see the voice that spoke with me. And when I had turned, I saw seven golden lampstands. And in the midst of the seven lampstands, one like the Son of Man, clothed in a linen garment down to the ground, and girded about the chest with a golden girdle. His head and his hair were white, as white wool, and like snow. And his eyes were as a flame of fire, and his feet like brass, as though they had been heated in a furnace, and his voice as the sound of many waters. And he had in his right hand seven stars, and out of his mouth went out a sharp two-edged sword, and his face shone even as the sun in its strength.

---

### Paragraph 97

**Original (1561):**

> Such things as have been treated of hitherto in th[illegible] book be in stead of the prologue or preface, as they term[illegible] Now at last shall the matter it self be propouded to us.[note 5.1] H[illegible] therefore follows the second part of this book, which r[illegible] cheth to the fourth chapter. In the which is Christ describe unto us with his catholic church. For first in deed is set [illegible] fore us the most sacred Image of Christ our Lord, teachi[illegible] what a one he is on the right hand of his father in glor[illegible] and how he sitting on the right hand of his father, works n[illegible] uertheleſſe in his church continually, never absent, preſe[illegible] always. Of what sort moreover the church is here[illegible] earth, is figured in those seven congregations. Here therfo[illegible] are showed the excellent gifts of churches, and again i[illegible] shameful errors: How the Lord Christ confirms such[illegible] are sliding and ready to fall, establishes those that stand[illegible] forteth the weak hearted, reſtreyneth the folyſhe hardy, a[illegible] preserves things that are corrupt: Finally how faithful [illegible] ſtours of the church must work and travel, with the pe[illegible] ple committed to their credit. For here is exceedingly w[illegible] taught, what is the repairing and preservation of church[illegible] Where also a brief sum of the whole ecclesiastical and [illegible] some doctrine brought in to an abridgement, shall be set be[illegible] us. For here is repeated from heaven of Christ in glory, [illegible] doctrine of true religion, which he had set forth more pl[illegible] tifully, when he was yet here in earth: And here most ap[illegible] applies it to churches, after consideration of the same.

**Modern English:**

Such things as have been treated of hitherto in this book are in place of a prologue or preface, as they term it. Now at last the matter itself will be presented to us. Therefore follows the second part of this book, which extends to the fourth chapter. In this, Christ is described to us with his catholic church. For first indeed is set before us the most sacred image of Christ our Lord, teaching what sort of one he is on the right hand of his Father in glory, and how he, sitting on the right hand of his Father, works nevertheless in his church continually, never absent, present always. Of what sort moreover the church is here on earth, is figured in those seven congregations. Here therefore are shown the excellent gifts of churches, and again shameful errors: How the Lord Christ confirms those who are sliding and ready to fall, establishes those that stand, strengthens the weakhearted, restrains the foolish hardy, and preserves things that are corrupt. Finally, how faithful ministers of the church must work and travel with the people committed to their care. For here is exceedingly taught what is the repairing and preservation of churches. Where also a brief summary of the whole ecclesiastical and some doctrine brought in to an abridgement, shall be set before us. For here is repeated from heaven of Christ in glory, the doctrine of true religion, which he had set forth more plentifully when he was yet here on earth: and here most aptly applies it to churches, after consideration of the same.

---

### Paragraph 98

**Original (1561):**

> And in most goodly order the words are knit togethe[illegible] (as likewyſe the whole book is written with plain worcester[illegible] and hanging right well together, they are deceived that thy[illegible] it to be lose beſomes or brooms unbound) John heard voice behind him crying: Whereupon he turned backwa[illegible] that he might se the voice speaking, that is to wit, him t[illegible] spake. For Arethas also admoniſſheth, that there is a trope[illegible] the words. For no man sees, but hears the voice. A[illegible] turning him to see, he saw a figure of Christ our ſauio[illegible] Therefore when the Lord speaks, let us turn also w[illegible] all our heart,[note 5.2] that we may likewise deserve to se the mysteries of the kingdom of God, for he gladly reveals him sell[illegible] to such as turn, and desire heavenly things: And from those that neglect the mysteries of the kingdom of God, all things of salvation are hid.

**Modern English:**

And in most goodly order the words are knit together (as likewise the whole book is written with plain words and hanging right well together; they are deceived who think it to be loose pieces or unbound collections). John heard a voice behind him crying. Whereupon he turned backward that he might see the voice speaking, that is to wit, him that spoke. For Arethas also admonishes that there is a trope in the words. For no man sees, but hears the voice. After turning to see, he saw a figure of Christ our Savior. Therefore, when the Lord speaks, let us turn also with all our heart,[note 5.2] that we may likewise deserve to see the mysteries of the kingdom of God, for he gladly reveals himself to such as turn and desire heavenly things. And from those that neglect the mysteries of the kingdom of God, all things of salvation are hidden.

---

### Paragraph 99

**Original (1561):**

> Further more S. John exhibiteth to us the Image of Christ, our catholic king and high bishop sitting in glory:[note 5.3] in the which description are comprised the chiefest matters of Christ. For such a taste of Christ is here given us, as in this world may be of our weak flesh perceived. But we shall se him at the length in the world to come such as he is, in the fulness of his majesty, wherein shall be joy and life everlasting: But this in this corrupt world, is yet granted to no man.

**Modern English:**

Furthermore, John depicts to us the image of Christ, our universal king and chief bishop, seated in glory.[note 5.3] Within this description are contained the principal matters concerning Christ. For such a glimpse of Christ is here given us as our frail flesh can perceive in this world. But we shall see him ultimately in the world to come, as he is, in the fullness of his majesty, wherein will be joy and everlasting life: but this in this corrupt world is yet granted to no man.

---

### Paragraph 100

**Original (1561):**

> So much therefore is permitted unto us that live yet in this world to be scene, as is profitable,[note 5.4] and as our infirmity may perceive. But this same is not little or nothing: but great and large and most full of spiritual pleasure, I mean if we behold these mysteries of God with a faithful eye, and mind desirous of Godly matters. And doubtless they be things certain and true, that here are revealed unto us. For they be revealed by the very son of God. Let us not wyſh than to se more, or desire greater things than these are: But take pleasure in those which Christ hath granted us. And let us know for certentie, that a wonderful benefit of God is given us in this vision. For who would not covet to se Christ in glory sitting on the right hand of the father? Who desires not to know what our Saviour doth in heaven? And home being in heave, is nevertheless present with his church in earth? But this sacred and holy Image instructs in all these points all the faithful of Christ most fully. How be it this Image of Christ is not to be set forth with colours, since that colours can not attain to the majesty thereof: but with the ecclesiastical doctrine, which hath the promise of the spirit of Christ: And is therefore more evident, and only mete for the true expressing thereof. Let us also prynt the same Image, not upon any dead table with colours that will peryſhe and fade, but in our hearts through the lively spirit of God, which may also keep it in our minds, never to be [illegible]wiped out. And such things as are spoken in the second and third chap. Of this book, are derived of this description[illegible] Christ, that the majesty of the thing might inuit us to a ſ[illegible] gular diligence. The matter is very plain.

**Modern English:**

So much therefore is permitted to us who live yet in this world to be seen, as is profitable, and as our weakness may perceive. But this is not small or insignificant; it is great, abundant, and full of spiritual joy, I mean if we behold these mysteries of God with a faithful eye and a mind eager for godly matters. And there is no doubt that these are certain and true things that are revealed to us here. For they are revealed by the very Son of God. Let us not wish to see more, or desire greater things than these are, but take pleasure in those which Christ has granted us. And let us know for certainty that a wonderful benefit of God is given to us in this vision. For who would not desire to see Christ in glory, sitting on the right hand of the Father? Who does not desire to know what our Savior does in heaven? And how, being in heaven, is he nevertheless present with his church on earth? But this sacred and holy image instructs in all these points the faithful of Christ most fully. However, this image of Christ is not to be portrayed with colors, since colors cannot attain to its majesty, but with the ecclesiastical doctrine, which has the promise of the Spirit of Christ, and is therefore more evident and only fitting for its true expression. Let us also print this image, not upon any dead surface with colors that will perish and fade, but in our hearts through the living Spirit of God, which may also keep it in our minds, never to be erased. And the things spoken in the second and third chapters of this book are derived from this description of Christ, that the majesty of the thing might invite us to singular diligence. The matter is very plain.

---

### Paragraph 101

**Original (1561):**

> [note 5.5]First we are taught who it is, whose Image is to us ex[illegible] bited: Not the son of man him self in his own ſubſtaun[illegible] but like the son of man. The son of man after the phr[illegible] of the Gospel, is called Christ him self very God and m[illegible] Here he showed not him self to be scene of John in his ou[illegible] substance, but in the form of an Angel, that represent[illegible] Christ: Which thing is oftener then once found in th[illegible] book. We shall therefore refer all these things unto Chris[illegible] not to the Angel, which is the minister of Christ in th[illegible] mystery. And we shall see Christ in his own ſubſtaun[illegible] what time our base body shall flitte from hence, and bay[illegible] raised from the dead shall be glorified. In the mean tie[illegible] the soul from the death of the body till it rise again, sh[illegible] clearly have the fruition of the sight of Christ: Wherein, [illegible] I said before shall be the chief joy and felicity. We sh[illegible] now therefore see Christ as it were in a glass, and so muc[illegible] as shall suffice us. The Lord open to us the eyes of o[illegible] mind.

**Modern English:**

First we are taught who it is, whose image is to be exhibited to us: not the Son of Man himself in his own substance, but like the Son of Man. The Son of Man, according to the Gospel, is called Christ himself, very God and man. Here he did not show himself to be seen as John in his own substance, but in the form of an angel, that represents Christ. This thing is found more than once in the book. We shall therefore refer all these things unto Christ, not to the angel, which is the minister of Christ in the mystery. And we shall see Christ in his own substance, what time our base body shall flit from hence, and being raised from the dead shall be glorified. In the meantime, the soul from the death of the body till it rises again, shall clearly have the fruition of the sight of Christ. Wherein, as I said before, shall be the chief joy and felicity. We shall now therefore see Christ as it were in a glass, and so much as shall suffice us. The Lord open to us the eyes of our mind.

---

### Paragraph 102

**Original (1561):**

> [note 5.6]He tells moreover, where he saw Christ, in the midd[illegible] of seven candelſtickes. By and by we shall perceive that [illegible] the candelſtickes must be understand the churches. Chri[illegible] is than in the midst of the church. He sits verily on[illegible] right hand of the father, and after the propriety of this [illegible] mane body, he is but in one place, and in no mo. As S. A[illegible] guſtin declares abundantly in the lvii. Epistle to Dor[illegible] nus. Yet for as much as he is also very God, he is lykew[illegible] in the midst of the church, as he promised in the Goſp[illegible] Wherſoeuer two or three be gathered in my name,[note 5.7] the[illegible] am in the midst of them. And again: behold I am w[illegible] you unto the worlds end. Therefore by his power diui[illegible] Christ remains and works in the church present, a[illegible] not absent. (Leave therefore to inquire, what Christ doth [illegible] the right hand of his father, whether he sit continually?) And he is verily in the midst of the churches, fixed to [illegible] place, but showing him self indifferently to all eagle as helpefull. For he neither accepts persons, nor sleep[illegible] He is not painted, he is not Idle, nothing regarding matters of the church: But is chiefly and only attentive to the salvation of the same. Such a one he promised him self to be in the.14.15. and.16. of John. And saying Christ is in the midst of the church, what Vicar moreover shall he have? Shall he have that ennemy which is directly against him?[note 5.8] For a Vicar is in stead of one absent: But Christ is in the middell of the church, present, not absent.

**Modern English:**

He tells moreover, where he saw Christ in the midst of seven candlesticks. By and by we shall perceive that the candlesticks must be understood as the churches. Christ is then in the midst of the church. He sits verily on the right hand of the Father, and according to the propriety of his divine body, he is but in one place, and in no more. As Saint Augustine declares abundantly in the 57th Epistle to Dionysius. Yet for as much as he is also very God, he is likewise in the midst of the church, as he promised in the Gospel: Wherever two or three are gathered in my name, I am in the midst of them. And again: Behold, I am with you unto the world’s end. Therefore, by his divine power, Christ remains and works in the present church, and is not absent. (Leave therefore to inquire, what Christ does on the right hand of his Father, whether he sits continually?) And he is verily in the midst of the churches, fixed to a place, but showing himself indifferently to all, as helpful. For he neither accepts persons, nor sleeps. He is not painted, he is not idle, nothing regarding matters of the church: but is chiefly and only attentive to the salvation of the same. Such a one he promised himself to be in the 14th, 15th, and 16th chapters of John. And saying Christ is in the midst of the church, what Vicar moreover shall he have? Shall he have that enemy which is directly against him? For a Vicar is in the stead of one absent: but Christ is in the middle of the church, present, not absent.

---

### Paragraph 103

**Original (1561):**

> In the text following Christ is described most plentifully, many things are ascribed unto him: And is declared in what sort Christ is in the midst of the church. And first indeed is showed, what garment he hath on: To wit both priestly and princely. By the which thing is figured, what manner of one Christ is in heaven and in earth: To wit bishop and king, intercessor, mediator, and sacrifice, a most perfect sanctification and justification, a redemer and deliverer of the faithful to his father, evermore working the salvation of his faithful. As S. Paul teaches, Roma. 8. Hebr. 7. Poderes[note 5.9] is found amongst the apparel of Aaron, and it is a priestly garment. Whereof S. Hierome writes to Fabiola, of the priestly garment. The second veſture of linen is a coat down to the foot, of double launde: Which Ioſephus calls Biſsina. And it is called in Hebrew ketheneth, in Greek, [illegible]. This cleaves just to the body, and is so narrow and ſtrayte ſleued, that there is no wryncle at all in the garment, and came down to the legs. This was verily white and clean.[note 5.10] For the Lord Christ is an undefiled Priest. Hebre. 7. Neither wears he again a foul veſture, as he did. Zachar. 3. Nor a purple, as in the.19. of John. But a bright one, as he that hath obtained a name aboue all names. But his gyrdell or belte is worn of Soldiers and triumphaunt persons: And it signifies in Christ the dignity royal. For Christ is king, delyuerer and redemer of the faithful. His victory is ours. He hath overcome Satan, Hell, sin and death.

**Modern English:**

In the text following, Christ is described most plentifully, many things are ascribed unto him: and it is declared in what sort Christ is in the midst of the church. And first indeed is showed what garment he hath on: to wit, both priestly and princely. By which thing is figured what manner of one Christ is in heaven and in earth: to wit, bishop and king, intercessor, mediator, and sacrifice, a most perfect sanctification and justification, a redeemer and deliverer of the faithful to his father, evermore working the salvation of his faithful. As Paul teaches, Romans 8; Hebrews 7. A pouch [?] is found amongst the apparel of Aaron, and it is a priestly garment. Whereof Jerome writes to Fabiola, concerning the priestly garment. The second vesture of linen is a coat down to the foot, of double linen: which Josephus calls *Bissina*. And it is called in Hebrew *ketheneth*, in Greek [illegible]. This cleaves just to the body, and is so narrow and straight-sleeved that there is no wrinkle at all in the garment, and came down to the legs. This was verily white and clean. For the Lord Christ is an undefiled priest, Hebrews 7. Neither wears he again a foul vesture, as he did, Zechariah 3. Nor a purple, as in John 19. But a bright one, as he that hath obtained a name above all names. But his girdle or belt is worn of soldiers and triumphant persons: and it signifies in Christ the dignity royal. For Christ is king, deliverer, and redeemer of the faithful. His victory is ours. He hath overcome Satan, Hell, sin, and death.

---

### Paragraph 104

**Original (1561):**

> But the belte or girdle of Christ is not set in the wonted place, to wit, about the loins. For as Arethas hath also admonished, there are no cocupiſcences to be restrained in Christ. Therefore is he not girded after the manner of sinners, but about the paps or breasts: to the intent we should understand by the girding, that he is king of kings, void[illegible] of all affections: Most rightuous and holy in judgements and government: But yet in the mean time furnished fo[illegible] the defence of his church, as we have red it written in the.9[illegible] Pſalme. The Lord hath put on strength and girded himself, &c. Christ might seem to have girded him self not after the manner that priests or kings use, for that he hath obtained [illegible] more excellent priesthood and kingdom, enduring for eve[illegible] To accomplish these things it behooved him to use a te[illegible] ple, and palace not transitory, but heaven it self. Hebre. vi[illegible] and ix. Yet in the mean time the effect pearſeth in to th[illegible] church it self, that so he may be present in the church also.

**Modern English:**

But the belt or girdle of Christ is not set in the customary place, namely, about the loins. For as Arethas has also admonished, there are no desires to be restrained in Christ. Therefore, he is not girded after the manner of sinners, but about the breasts: so that we should understand by the girding that he is king of kings, void of all affections; most righteous and holy in judgments and government.
