But yet, in the meantime, he is furnished for the defense of his church, as we have read it written in Psalm 9. The Lord has put on strength and girded himself, and so forth. Christ might seem to have girded himself not after the manner that priests or kings use, for that he has obtained a more excellent priesthood and kingdom, enduring forever. To accomplish these things, it behooved him to use a temple, and a palace not transitory, but heaven itself. Hebrews 6 and 9. Yet, in the meantime, the effect penetrates into the church itself, so that he may be present in the church also.

---

### Paragraph 105

**Original (1561):**

> [note 5.11]But the head of Christ appears hoar, and his hey[illegible] white, like most pure wolle, and whyteſt snow. Such a head is ascribed also to the father of our Lord Jesus Christ, [illegible] the seventh Chap. of Daniel. For they be of the same eſſen[illegible] And hereby is signified wisdom and age, and also the e[illegible] nitie and deity of Christ. And by reason that Christ is Go[illegible] therefore is he head of the church, ministering to the body li[illegible] spirit, wisdom, and all gifts celestial. Epheſ. v. Ca[illegible] Pope of Rome, that most wicked man of sin doe th[illegible] What an head is he than? without life, without brain[illegible] most folyſhe. As he is described in the xi. of Zacha. And is a shame, that we will not se these things, being blind ever. Christ is everlasting, omnipotent, and that knows thing: And he may be the health and head of the body. I[illegible] in the beginning, saith he, was the word, and the word [illegible] with God. &c. Christ him self: before Abraham was, saith [illegible] I am. Therefore the Heretics lye, deniyng Christ to be[illegible] ry God, of the same substance with the father. He is the [illegible] dome of God, all things are subject to him. Ephe. the [illegible] And he him self fulfills all thing, after the same sort be[illegible] present with his church.

**Modern English:**

But the head of Christ appears gray, and his hair white, like the finest wool and the whitest snow. Such a head is also ascribed to the Father of our Lord Jesus Christ, as in the seventh chapter of Daniel. For they are of the same essence. And hereby is signified wisdom and age, and also the divinity and deity of Christ. And because Christ is God, therefore he is the head of the church, ministering to the body life, spirit, wisdom, and all celestial gifts. Ephesians 5. The Pope of Rome, that most wicked man of sin, does what? What a head he is—without life, without brains, utterly foolish. As he is described in the eleventh chapter of Zechariah. It is a shame that we will not see these things, being ever blind. Christ is everlasting, omnipotent, and knows all things. And he may be the health and head of the body. “In the beginning was the Word, and the Word was with God,” says Jerome. Christ himself: “Before Abraham was, I am,” says he. Therefore, the heretics lie, denying Christ to be very God, of the same substance with the Father. He is the dominion of God; all things are subject to him. Ephesians 1. And he himself fulfills all things, after the same sort being present with his church.

---

### Paragraph 106

**Original (1561):**

> [note 5.12]Now are his eyes also not darkened nor blind, but fie[illegible] and bright. For Christ knows all thing. Christ's eye[illegible] watchful, nothing is hyd from him, he sees all things [illegible] are done, both good and evil. And he sees to the intent may judge and require. He is light in darkness, and the si[illegible] of Christ is to good men joyful in perils. Finally the judgements of Christ are rightuous. The Prophet David: The eyes of the Lord, saith he, upon the just, and his ears are to their prayer. Again: The face of the Lord is on them that do evil. And like as the head is not plucked from the body, so Christ can not be absent from his church. And saying that his eyes are quick sighted, and that the Lord forſeeth all our things, and hath the charge over us, how is he absent from his church? What needs there any vicar?

**Modern English:**

Now his eyes are not darkened nor blind, but clear and bright. For Christ knows all things. Christ’s eyes are watchful; nothing is hidden from him; he sees all things that are done, both good and evil. And he sees in order that he may judge and require. He is light in darkness, and the sight of Christ is to good men joyful in perils. Finally, the judgments of Christ are righteous. The prophet David says, “The eyes of the Lord are upon the just, and his ears are to their prayer.” Again, “The face of the Lord is against those who do evil.” And just as the head is not plucked from the body, so Christ cannot be absent from his church. And considering that his eyes are quicksighted, and that the Lord foresees all our things, and has charge over us, how is he absent from his church? What need is there of any vicar?

---

### Paragraph 107

**Original (1561):**

> And the fete of the Lord are of copper,[note 5.13] or like unto brass and frankincense burning in a fornace. For Chalcolibaum, is a word compounded of brass and frankincense.[note 5.14] This notes Eraſmus, and that Swidas shows also the same, that there is a kind of Copper more precious than gold: which he saith is made of Salt peter and of a stone. Plin. in the xxxiiii. and ii. Chapter. calls it a kind of brass, which is dug out of the veins of the earth, in times paste had in price. It should seem to me to be the same, which in the first and tenth of Ezekiel is called Haſmal, a present remedy against poisons. For if wine intoxicated be put into a cup thereof, it will hiss. And so is the death and poison detected.

**Modern English:**

The feasts of the Lord are like copper, or like brass and frankincense burning in a furnace. For Chalcolibaum is a word compounded of brass and frankincense. This Erasmus notes, and that Swidas also shows that there is a kind of copper more precious than gold: which he says is made of saltpeter and of a stone. Pliny, in the thirty-fourth and second chapter, calls it a kind of brass, which in past times was highly valued and dug out of the veins of the earth. It seems to me to be the same which in the first and tenth of Ezekiel is called Hasmal, a present remedy against poisons. For if wine is poured into it, it will hiss. And so death and poison are detected.

---

### Paragraph 108

**Original (1561):**

> The most clean brazen and fiery fete, do signify the conversation, and the ways of the Lord blameles, his judgements right and just. And that he so walk in the church, and governs all things, that in the mean season all vncleanes be detec [illegible]ed and consumed, but he him self remains always most holy and pure. For fire pourgeth. God is a consuming fire.

**Modern English:**

The most blatant, brazen, and fiery spectacle signifies the conduct and ways of the Lord, blameless, his judgments right and just. And that he so walks in the church and governs all things, that in the meantime all uncleanness be detected and consumed, but he himself remains always most holy and pure. For fire purges. God is a consuming fire.

---

### Paragraph 109

**Original (1561):**

> But the voice of Christ,[note 5.15] is as it were the noise of many waters, not so much for that all nations and people do commend and praise him: But for as much as the Gospel and word of God, came into the whole world. Which voice also most mighty kings could less assuage and appease, than they could do the guſſhing of waters,[note 5.16] or to stop the wyndes in ſackes. Therefore by the power of preaching the Lord is always present in his church.

**Modern English:**

But the voice of Christ,[note 5.15] is as it were the noise of many waters, not so much because all nations and people commend and praise him: but because the Gospel and word of God have come into the whole world. This voice also, no mighty kings could less assuage and appease than they could stop the gushing of waters, or hold back the winds in sacks.[note 5.16] Therefore, by the power of preaching, the Lord is always present in his church.

---

### Paragraph 110

**Original (1561):**

> The hand is an instrument of all instruments, especially the righthande.[note 5.17] In this Christ holds seven stars, to wit seven prelates or pastors of churches in Asia: And even all the bishops throughout the whole world Christ by his power gives to us as Pastors, and instructs, comfort[illegible] confirms and defends them, to the end they should preac[illegible] his word. Whereby he may join him self to the churc[illegible] Christ works by them in the church and preserves th[illegible]

**Modern English:**

The hand is an instrument of all instruments, especially the right hand. [note 5.17] In this, Christ holds seven stars, namely, seven prelates or pastors of churches in Asia. And even all the bishops throughout the whole world, Christ by his power gives to us as pastors, and instructs, comforts, confirms, and defends them, so that they should preach his word. By this means, he joins himself to the church; Christ works through them in the church and preserves them.

---

### Paragraph 111

**Original (1561):**

> [note 5.18]The same is more lively expressed in the words that f[illegible] lowe. For a sharpe two edged sword comes out of [illegible] Lords mouth. This swore, is the word of God, as [illegible] right well declared in the sixth to the Epheſ. and fourth to [illegible] Hebrews. And this word or sword hangs not upon [illegible] wall nor sticks fast in the sheathe, nor hangs by the f[illegible] but comes out of the mouth. He saith not, it came foort[illegible] it shall come forth: But it comes forth, as the thing th[illegible] in continual operation, or perpetual preaching through[illegible] the world. And it is two edged, sharpe and pearſing, aſ[illegible] in the heart of the Godly unto salvation, as in the heart[illegible] the wicked to payne and condemnation. And yet at [illegible] day comes out that sword of the mouth of Christ by mouths of Ministers. The word of Christ is in deed [illegible] temned of the world, and is called of many a fable: But [illegible] a sword, and that a sword out of Christ's mouth. All [illegible] unfaithful do find and shall find this, howe soever [illegible] resist. With this sword Christ kills the wicked. And effect of this sword is greater than was the sword of A[illegible] ander, Pompey, Iulius Caesar, or Marius, Attille, or [illegible] merlane. Neither makes it any ſkylle, though the w[illegible] now acknowledge it not. It shall doe in time to com[illegible] their greatest evil. Doubtless with this spirit of his m[illegible] the Lord continues always to comfort and govern[illegible] church, so that he is never absent from the same.

**Modern English:**

The same is more vividly expressed in the words that follow. For a sharp, two-edged sword comes out of the Lord’s mouth. This word is, indeed, the word of God, as Jerome declared right well in the sixth chapter to the Ephesians and fourth chapter to the Hebrews. And this word or sword does not hang upon a wall, nor sticks fast in the sheath, nor hangs by the frame, but comes out of the mouth. He does not say it came forth, or it shall come forth, but it comes forth, as the thing that is in continual operation, or perpetual preaching throughout the world. And it is two-edged, sharp and piercing, as in the heart of the godly unto salvation, so in the heart of the wicked to pain and condemnation. And yet at a time it comes forth that sword from the mouth of Christ by the mouths of ministers. The word of Christ is indeed condemned by the world, and is called by many a fable, but it is a sword, and that a sword out of Christ’s mouth. All the unfaithful do find and shall find this, however they resist. With this sword Christ kills the wicked. The effect of this sword is greater than was the sword of Alexander, Pompey, Julius Caesar, or Marius, Attila, or [illegible]. Neither does it matter, though the world now acknowledge it not. It shall do in time to come their greatest evil. Doubtless with this spirit of his mouth the Lord continues always to comfort and govern his church, so that he is never absent from the same.

---

### Paragraph 112

**Original (1561):**

> Finally the countenance of Christ shines as the [illegible] doth in his greatest strength about none, when it is [illegible] bright, clear, and pleasant, by the countenance we [illegible] men chiefly. Therefore by the countenance we know C[illegible] The countenance of Christ is light. Christ therefore is [illegible] And that verily a divine and eternal light, lyghtyng a[illegible] that they may also be made the children of light: and than[illegible] faces of saints may shine in that day, as bright as the si[illegible] and as the face of Christ shone. Math. 13. and.17. And f[illegible] he doth thus communicate this light unto us. (John [illegible] and.1. John. 1.) How is it to be thought that Christ should be absent from his church? Thou see how he is present.

**Modern English:**

Finally, the countenance of Christ shines as the sun does in its greatest strength around noon, when it is bright, clear, and pleasant; by the countenance we recognize men chiefly. Therefore, by the countenance we know Christ. The countenance of Christ is light. Christ, therefore, is light. And that truly a divine and eternal light, enlightening them that they may also be made the children of light; and then the faces of the saints may shine in that day as brightly as the sun and as the face of Christ shone. Matthew 13 and 17. And how does he thus communicate this light unto us? (John 1 and 1, John 1.) How is it to be thought that Christ should be absent from his church? You see how he is present.

---

### Paragraph 113

**Original (1561):**

> And so hath our Lord Christ exhibited him self to us to be scene unto salvation, and hath opened him self wholly unto us, as he is, what he doth for us, and howe he is in his church. In these things are all the mysteries of the Gospel comprehended. For what can you say of Christ, that you have not herein compryſed. Let us therefore remember them. and write them in our minds, that we may embrace Christ king and bishop, and that we never let him depart out of our arms. To him be glory.

**Modern English:**

And so our Lord Christ has revealed himself to us, so that he may be seen in salvation, and has opened himself wholly, so that we may understand what he is, what he does for us, and how he is in his church. In these things are all the mysteries of the Gospel comprehended. For what can you say of Christ that you have not here encompassed? Let us therefore remember them and write them in our minds, that we may embrace Christ, king and bishop, and that we never let him depart from our arms. To him be glory.

---

## Sermon 6: ¶ How John was affected towards the vision to him exhibited, the comfort of John, and the exposition of the vision, applied unto consolation.

### Paragraph 114

**Original (1561):**

> ANd when I saw him I fell at his fete even as dead: And he laid his right hand upon me, saying unto me: Fear not, I am the first and the last, and am alive, and was [illegible]ead. And behold I am alive for ever more, [illegible]nd have the keys of hell, and of death, write [illegible]herfore the things which thou haste scene, [illegible]nd the things which are, and the things which shall be fulfilled hereafter: And the mystery of the seven Stars which thou saw in my right hand, and the seven golden candelſtickes. The seven Stars are the messengers of the seven congregations: And [illegible]he seven candelſtickes which thou saw, [illegible]re the seven congregations.

**Modern English:**

And when I saw him, I fell at his feet as dead. And he laid his right hand upon me, saying to me, “Fear not. I am the first and the last, and I am alive, and was dead. And behold, I am alive forevermore, and have the keys of hell and of death. Write therefore the things which you have seen, and the things which are, and the things which shall be fulfilled hereafter. And the mystery of the seven stars which you saw in my right hand, and the seven golden candlesticks. The seven stars are the messengers of the seven congregations. And the seven candlesticks which you saw are the seven congregations.

---

### Paragraph 115

**Original (1561):**

> It follows how blessed S. John was moved with that celestial and wonderful vision: And how he received consolation, more over the exposition of the vision applied to his comfort, with a commandment to indite all these thing[illegible] diligently.

**Modern English:**

It follows how blessed John was moved by that celestial and wonderful vision, and how he received consolation, moreover, the exposition of the vision applied to his comfort, with a commandment to write down all these things diligently.

---

### Paragraph 116

**Original (1561):**

> [note 6.1]What time he had fully scene this divine and heavenly sight of Christ our Lord, sitting on the right hand of God i[illegible] glory, his strength failing him, he falls down on the earth: and lying at the fete of the Lord, is like a dead body. W[illegible] read that the same chanced to Daniel in the x. Chapter. And other men of God also have been feared with the visions o[illegible] Angels. The women also in the new Testament tremble[illegible] at the sepulchre, seeing Angels. Peter was amassed at th[illegible] greatness of the miracle. Luke v. And falling at the knees [illegible] the Lord, cries out, go from me, Lord, for I am a ſynful m[illegible] For Godly visions bewray our infirmity:[note 6.2] Neither be [illegible] apt or sufficiently pourged, to behold those ſuperceleſt[illegible] things with eyes and minds sick and not yet well pu[illegible] fied. Therefore must the elect be glorified in an other life, th[illegible] they may be made partakers of the glory celestial. In th[illegible] mean season here all godly are humbled and abased by hyg[illegible] visions and revelations.[note 6.3] For they advance not them ſelue[illegible] proudly through the glory of reuelatio: But perceiving th[illegible] natural corruption, they crave pardon, and the augmentation of the ſuperceleſtiall grace and light. For unless we b[illegible] illumined with the spirit of God, we shall lye like dead folk[illegible] how so ever we receive with our corporal senses the viſio[illegible] celestial.

**Modern English:**

What time he had fully seen this divine and heavenly vision of Christ our Lord, sitting on the right hand of God in glory, his strength failing him, he falls down on the earth: and lying at the feet of the Lord, is like a dead body. We read that the same happened to Daniel in the tenth chapter. And other men of God also have been frightened by the visions of Angels. The women also in the New Testament trembled at the sepulchre, seeing Angels. Peter was amazed at the greatness of the miracle. Luke 5. And falling at the knees of the Lord, cries out, “Go from me, Lord, for I am a sinful man.” For godly visions betray our infirmity.

Neither are we prepared or sufficiently purged to behold those celestial things with eyes and minds sick and not yet well purified. Therefore must the elect be glorified in another life, that they may be made partakers of the celestial glory. In the mean season here, all godly are humbled and abased by high visions and revelations.

For they do not advance themselves proudly through the glory of revelation, but perceiving their natural corruption, they crave pardon, and the augmentation of the celestial grace and light. For unless we be illumined with the spirit of God, we shall lie like dead folk, however we receive with our corporal senses the visions celestial.

---

### Paragraph 117

**Original (1561):**

> [note 6.4]But they that humble them selves before the Lord, find[illegible] a most present consolation at the Lords hand. Wherfo[illegible] there came to S. John immediately both in word and de[illegible] a full consolation. For the Angel representing the person[illegible] of Christ, lays his right hand upon John: The which is token of amity, protection, and of present help. For in [illegible] pressing this manner of speaking in dutche, we say therefore [illegible] laying on the hand is signified, that Christ is good to John, ready to help him. Which incontinently he makes play[illegible] by the addition of words, saying, fear not.[note 6.5] Which ſaiy[illegible] is common every where in the story of the Gospel: And th[illegible] fore is most gospel like, that is to say most lucky. For God commands the humbled to be of good hope, and to live assured under the protectio of the highest. Which verily we understand to be spoken not to John alone but to all us also, that we in like manner, albeit that we feel the infirmity of our flesh, should yet hope well of the goodness and mercy of God. Here follows that cause more fully declared, why John should not be afraid. For the vision showed was not exhibited for the terror of him: but that John might perceive how great and mighty he is which is prepared for the defence of him and all the faithful. As though he should say. Where thou see how great he is, which hath taken upon him to defend thee, who finally protects and governs the whole Church, there is no cause why thou shouldest be afraid. But rather execute boldly, that he comaundeth thee. Write that he co maunds to be written. Be not afraid of men, fear God rather. For if good me be so sore afraid at the sight of him, where shall the enemies and contemners of God appear?

**Modern English:**

But those who humble themselves before the Lord find great consolation at the Lord’s hand. Therefore, great consolation came to John immediately, both in word and in deed. For the angel representing the person of Christ lays his right hand upon John, which is a token of friendship, protection, and present help. For when pressing this manner of speaking in Dutch, we say that laying on the hand signifies that Christ is good to John, ready to help him. Which he immediately demonstrates by adding the words, “Fear not.” This saying is common throughout the story of the Gospel, and therefore is most gospel-like, that is to say, most fortunate. For God commands the humbled to be of good hope and to live assured under the protection of the highest. This truly we understand to be spoken not to John alone, but to all of us also, that we in like manner, even though we feel the weakness of our flesh, should yet hope well of the goodness and mercy of God. Here follows a fuller declaration of why John should not be afraid. For the vision shown was not exhibited for his terror, but that John might perceive how great and mighty he is who is prepared for his defense and for the defense of all the faithful. As though he should say: Where you see how great he is who has taken upon himself to defend you, who finally protects and governs the whole Church, there is no cause why you should be afraid. But rather execute boldly what he commands you. Write what he commands to be written. Do not be afraid of men, fear God rather. For if good men are so sore afraid at the sight of him, where shall the enemies and contemners of God appear?

---

### Paragraph 118

**Original (1561):**

> Therefore consequently he expoudeth the vision, teaching who he is, which was scene like to the son of man walking among the golden Candelſtickes. And he applies this expoſitio unto comfort: that both John and every faithful may perceive, how mighty Christ is, and what the faithful have by him obtained. For the Angel tempers his speech so, that we may seem to hear all things spoke to us not by the mouth of the Angel, but of Christ him self.[note 6.6] And this exposition hath his parts. For first he declares (as I said even now) whose Image it was that was showed. Than is annexed a commandment to write this book. After that is opened the mystery of the stars. Finally the ſecretnes of the cadelſticks is revealed: And all these things right plainly and briefly.

**Modern English:**

Therefore, consequently, he explains the vision, teaching who he is, who appeared like the Son of Man walking among the golden candlesticks. And he applies this explanation to comfort, so that both John and every faithful person may understand how mighty Christ is, and what the faithful have obtained through him. For the angel shapes his speech so that we may seem to hear all things spoken not by the mouth of the angel, but by Christ himself. [note 6.6] And this explanation has its parts. First, he declares (as I said just now) whose image was shown. Then a commandment is added to write this book. After that, the mystery of the stars is opened. Finally, the secret of the candlesticks is revealed: and all these things, plainly and briefly.

---

### Paragraph 119

**Original (1561):**

> First thou hast scene, says the Lord, a vision,[note 6.7] whereat thou waste amassed: but fear thou not. For thou hast not scene any evil or fearful spirit, boding any misfortune: but my shape, which am thy redemer and Lord. I am first and last. And this manner of speaking (as I warned a little before) he toke out of the Prophecies of Isaiah, as it is to be seen in the xli. xliiii. xlv. and xlviii. chapters. And he signifies him self to be coequal, and of the same substance with the father in all things, very God, eternal, and incoprehenſible. For look what things the father attributeth to himself, the same also doth the son usurp. But there is no order or time certain to be vnderſtade in first and last, but plainly euerlaſtingnes. Therefore Christ here signifies,[note 6.8] that he is very God, egalle and of the same essence with the father from all eternity. As the same is also much confirmed in John 1.5.10.14. and 17. chapter. This fights against the Heretics, which at that time also, as at this day the Seruetanes, deny the eternal deity of Christ the Lord. And thus, when the true God is of us acknoweledged and believed, he may be for our Salvation. If Christ be not very God, he is not our salvation. For I am God, says the verity: And besides me there is no God, no Salvation.

**Modern English:**

First you have seen, says the Lord, a vision whereat you were amazed: but do not fear. For you have not seen any evil or fearful spirit, foretelling any misfortune: but my form, which am your redeemer and Lord. I am first and last. And this manner of speaking (as I warned a little before) he took from the Prophecies of Isaiah, as it is to be seen in the 41st, 43rd, 45th, and 48th chapters. And he signifies himself to be coequal and of the same substance with the Father in all things, very God, eternal, and incomprehensible. For whatever things the Father attributes to himself, the same also the Son claims. But there is no order or certain time to be understood in “first and last,” but plainly everlastingness. Therefore Christ here signifies that he is very God, equal and of the same essence with the Father from all eternity. As the same is also much confirmed in John 1:5, 10, 14, and 17. This refutes the heretics, who at that time also, as now the Servetarians, deny the eternal deity of Christ the Lord. And thus, when the true God is acknowledged and believed by us, he may be for our salvation. If Christ is not very God, he is not our salvation. For I am God, says the truth: And besides me there is no God, no salvation.

---

### Paragraph 120

**Original (1561):**

> Secondly he says, I am living, and was dead: whereby he signifies that he toke the true humane nature. The which many also at the same time denied: In like case as the[illegible] be some at this day, which do plainly derogate from th[illegible] humanity of Christ. Against all such manner of heresies the Lord him self confesses that he was dead. Whereby it is now manifest, that he is very man, as he is also very God, of the same eſſece with his father in deity, as he is also of the same substance with us in humanity, like unto us in all things, sin excepted. For he toke not the nature of Angles, but the ſede of Abraham. And it behooved in deed than[illegible] the son of man should be incarnate, that both he might di[illegible] and shed blood.[note 6.9] For the Testament in the dead is finally ratified neither is there any remission made without blood she[illegible] ding. The Lord therefore dies, and ſhedeth blood, to the int[illegible] he might give full remiſſio of sins, and confirm the new[illegible] Testament. Yet even he that was thought to be dead, now lives,[note 6.10] and is that same living, who having vanquished death, the iii. day rose again from the dead, and repared life for a[illegible] beleuers, and inspires into them his own very life.

**Modern English:**

Secondly, he says, “I am alive, and was dead,” by which he signifies that he took on the true human nature. Many at that time also denied this, just as there are some today who plainly deny the humanity of Christ. Against all such heresies, the Lord himself confesses that he was dead. By this, it is now manifest that he is very man, as he is also very God, of the same essence with his Father in deity, as he is also of the same substance with us in humanity, like unto us in all things, sin excepted. For he took not the nature of angels, but the seed of Abraham. And it behooved indeed that the Son of Man should be incarnate, that both he might die and shed blood. For the testament in the dead is finally ratified; neither is any remission made without shedding of blood. The Lord therefore dies and sheds blood, to the intent he might give full remission of sins and confirm the new testament. Yet even he who was thought to be dead, now lives, and is that same living one, who having vanquished death, on the third day rose again from the dead, and restores life to all believers, and inspires into them his own very life.

---

### Paragraph 121

**Original (1561):**

> And therefore adds immediately: behold I am living[illegible] world without end. For now Christ dies no more, death[illegible] shall not rule over him. But rather he is the life of all his fait[illegible] full, who in rising again, brought again life: and that life everlasting, induringe I say world without end. As he him self declares more at large. John. 5.6.10. chapter. And the Apostle to the Rom. 4.1. Corinth. 15. and 2. Timoth. 1.

**Modern English:**

And therefore adds immediately: behold I am living world without end. For now Christ dies no more; death shall not rule over him. But rather he is the life of all his faithful, who in rising again brought again life, and that life everlasting, enduring, I say, world without end. As he himself declares more at large in John, chapter 5, verses 6 and 10. And the Apostle to the Romans, 4:1; to the Corinthians, 15; and to Timothy, 2:1.

---

### Paragraph 122

**Original (1561):**

> Moreover where many were wot to doubt of this life gotten and repared by Christ, the Lord him self confirms, that he said, by an other, and says. Amen. As though he should say, this is altogether true and undoubted that I say.

**Modern English:**

Furthermore, where many were known to doubt the life obtained and restored by Christ, the Lord himself confirms that he spoke through another, and says, “Amen.” As though he were saying, “This is altogether true and certain that I say.”

---

### Paragraph 123

**Original (1561):**

> Finally he adds,[note 6.11] and I have the keys of Hell and of death. By the which words again he comfort exceedingly, and expresses his power: and declares howe great he is, and what we have of him. Here must we speak by the way of the keep. The ordinary gloss says very well: he that hath the keys of any house, says he, lets in whom he will, and kepeth back whom he will from entering in. Therefore Christ possesses the keys of death and hell, for that whom he will, he delivers from perpetual codemnation of death: And whom he will,[note 6.12] he suffers to remain iuſtely in the same danger of damnation. And verily Isaiah in the 22. speaking of Eliachim, whom he says should be made Judge in the court of Ezechias: I will lay says he the key of the house of David upon his shoulder: which shall open, and no man shall shut: shall shut, and no man shall open. Therefore are the keys put in the Scripture for the charge and government of the house. Eliachim shall govern all thing in the Court of Ezechias uprightly. What soever he shall determine, no man shall infringe, that which he shall abrogate, no man shall restore. Christ therefore, a figure of whom Eliachim represented, shall him self have also the chief government in the house or kingdom of God: so that whom he will, he may quick, and pluck back from hell and from damnation: And again whom he list to condemn, he may diſtroie by his just judgment. For he hath most full power over death and hell.[note 6.13] For both two hath he overcome and made weak. And these things comfort the faithful most ſtrongely, and reteyne them in all Godly duties. And that same is chiefly to be observed, that he says not, he had the keys, or shall have, but I have, says he, I have I say. He gave not his power to the Bishop of Rome, but hath i[illegible] him self, and will keep it still for ever. And he gave not to the Apostles full power of life and death, of salvation and damnation,[note 6.14] and so unarmed him self: but he gave the keys of opening and shutting heaven, as it were to his Ministers and servitors, by the preaching of the Gospel: by the which he promised life to all that believed, Christ him self should give that life for the truth of the promise: To whom so ever they should threaten damnation, Christ him self should condemn for the truth of his word. We see therefore that the Lord kepeth still and exercises the power, and his ministers the ministry (by preaching, not by absolute power.) Therefore the Pope is Antichrist,[note 6.15] which usurps and takes upon him this full power and authority in Heaven and in Earth, and in the midst of the earth also, or beyond all the earth, i[illegible] those unfortunate Ilandes. I mean purgatory. By the which[illegible] crafty devise he hath subtly emptied the purses, Coffer[illegible], garuers and wine cellars,[note 6.16] of foolish people that ſwarne from the articles of their belief, to wit I believe the forgeuenes o[illegible] sins, the resurrection of the flesh, and life everlasting. Th[illegible] beast dare usurp the two horns of the Lamb,[note 6.17] namely th[illegible] authority of King and Bishop, and therefore to hang two key[illegible] under his triple Crown: that even by these Arms all the world may perceive, that this is very he, which, hauin[illegible] subdued three kings or horns, is crop up, and challenges t[illegible] him self all power in heaven and in earth, signified by the tw[illegible] keys. And surely the blindness of our time is wonderful an[illegible] to be lameted, that having eyes it sees yet nothing. Let ſuc[illegible] as be wise, remember, that Christ hath yet the keys of deat[illegible] and Hell, his ministers the denouncing of life and death.

**Modern English:**

Finally he adds,[note 6.11] and I have the keys of Hell and of death. By these words again he comforts exceedingly, and expresses his power: and declares how great he is, and what we have of him. Here we must speak by the way of the key. The ordinary gloss says very well: he who has the keys of any house, says he, lets in whom he will, and keeps back whom he will from entering in. Therefore Christ possesses the keys of death and hell, for that whom he will, he delivers from perpetual condemnation of death: And whom he will,[note 6.12] he suffers to remain justly in the same danger of damnation. And verily Isaiah in the 22nd verse, speaking of Eliakim, whom he says should be made Judge in the court of Hezekiah: I will lay, says he, the key of the house of David upon his shoulder: which shall open, and no man shall shut; shall shut, and no man shall open. Therefore, keys are put in Scripture for the charge and government of the house. Eliakim shall govern all things in the court of Hezekiah uprightly. Whatever he shall determine, no man shall infringe; that which he shall abrogate, no man shall restore. Christ therefore, a figure of whom Eliakim represented, shall himself have also the chief government in the house or kingdom of God: so that whom he will, he may quicken, and pluck back from hell and from damnation; and again, whom he lists to condemn, he may destroy by his just judgment. For he has most full power over death and hell.[note 6.13] For both two he has overcome and made weak. And these things comfort the faithful most strongly, and retain them in all godly duties. And that same is chiefly to be observed, that he says not, he had the keys, or shall have, but I have, says he, I have; I say. He gave not his power to the Bishop of Rome, but has it himself, and will keep it still forever. And he gave not to the Apostles full power of life and death, of salvation and damnation,[note 6.14] and so divested himself: but he gave the keys of opening and shutting heaven, as it were to his Ministers and servitors, by the preaching of the Gospel: by which he promised life to all that believed, Christ himself should give that life for the truth of the promise: To whom so ever they should threaten damnation, Christ himself should condemn for the truth of his word. We see therefore that the Lord keeps still and exercises the power, and his ministers the ministry (by preaching, not by absolute power). Therefore the Pope is Antichrist,[note 6.15] which usurps and takes upon him this full power and authority in Heaven and in Earth, and in the midst of the earth also, or beyond all the earth, in those unfortunate lands. I mean purgatory. By this crafty device he has subtly emptied the purses, coffers, garners, and wine cellars,[note 6.16] of foolish people that swarm from the articles of their belief, to wit, I believe the forgiveness of sins, the resurrection of the flesh, and life everlasting. The beast dares usurp the two horns of the Lamb,[note 6.17] namely the authority of King and Bishop, and therefore hangs two keys under his triple Crown: that even by these arms all the world may perceive that this is very he, who, having subdued three kings or horns, is cropped up, and challenges himself all power in heaven and in earth, signified by the two keys. And surely the blindness of our time is wonderful and to be lamented, that having eyes it sees yet nothing. Let such as be wise remember that Christ has yet the keys of death and Hell, his ministers the denouncing of life and death.

---

### Paragraph 124

**Original (1561):**

> [note 6.18]And now when he had declared these great and moſ[illegible] wholesome matters, and had comforted the mind of John, h[illegible] adds the commandment: write the vision exhibited, f[illegible] nally write those things also which must be done short[illegible] after this. He places in the midst, and those that be: th[illegible] is, which are in deed, and true, and be not false. And these[illegible] things are to get authority to this book, finally to the who[illegible] scripture, which is revealed with like truth of the self sam[illegible] Author. And as John is commanded to write without fear[illegible] so are we commanded to Preach and publish the same boldly though the world be never so mad thereat.

**Modern English:**

And now when he had declared these great and wholesome matters, and had comforted the mind of John, he adds the commandment: write the vision exhibited, finally write those things also which must be done shortly after this. He places in the midst, and those that be: that is, which are in deed, and true, and be not false. And these things are to give authority to this book, finally to the whole scripture, which is revealed with like truth of the same Author. And as John is commanded to write without fear, so are we commanded to preach and publish the same boldly though the world be never so mad thereat.

---

### Paragraph 125

**Original (1561):**

> He adds moreover, the exposition that remains, and says: The mystery of the seven stars, &c. The reason seems almost vnperſit. Therefore must we understand this is the mystery or sacrament of the seven Stars and Candelſtickes: that it may be as it were a proposition,[note 6.19] and that the exposition should follow immediately: the seven stars, are seven Messengers, &c. And by Sacrament understand a secret mystery, and the very exposition of the mystery. As if you would ſ[illegible] here is to be noted the goodness of Almighty God,[note 6.20] which declares to us him self the hardest places of the Scripture. Where be they therefore that accuse the Scripture of obſcurenes, and contend that it can not be vnderſtade? let us here mark also the common manner of speaking of the whole scripture: seven stars,[note 6.21] be seven Messengers: The seven lights, are seven Churches. For signs receive the names of the things, although they be, and remain in their own substance, and bee not changed into another. This the very contentiouſe persons do grant also, which in the words of the supper, this is my body, will acknowledge no figurative speech at all.

**Modern English:**

He adds further the explanation that remains, and says: The mystery of the seven stars, &c. The reason seems almost unpersisted. Therefore we must understand this to be the mystery or sacrament of the seven stars and candlesticks, that it may be as it were a proposition, and that the explanation should follow immediately: the seven stars are seven messengers, &c. And by sacrament understand a secret mystery, and the very explanation of the mystery. As if it is to be noted here the goodness of Almighty God, which declares to us himself the hardest passages of Scripture. Where then are those who accuse the Scripture of obscurity, and contend that it cannot be understood? Let us mark also here the common manner of speaking of the whole scripture: seven stars are seven messengers; the seven lights are seven churches. For signs receive the names of the things, although they be, and remain in their own substance, and are not changed into another. This the very contentious persons do grant also, who in the words of the supper, “This is my body,” will acknowledge no figurative speech at all.

---

### Paragraph 126

**Original (1561):**

> Stars be called Angles. Angles be Gods Messengers, pastors of churches, so called in the ii. and iii. chapter of Malachie. For God sends preachers as Ambassadors to the people, and wills them to be heard in like case as himself. Luke x. John xiii. Let no man therefore tarry till the Lord him self come down from heaven again, and preach [illegible]nto us. Even now he preaches to us by his Messengers, which preach his, that is to say the word of Christ, if [illegible]ou contemn them, you contemn Christ. Preachers be [illegible]alled stars, by reason of their bright and Heavenly doc [illegible]rine, and for their pureness of life.[note 6.22] Beware therefore you Preachers, that you be not wandering Plants, lest the [illegible]aue no light at all, neither in doctrine, nor conversation of [illegible]fe. For than the shall be likened to stars that fall down [illegible]ut of Heaven, as shall happen here after in this book to the [illegible]alſe teachers.

**Modern English:**

Stars are called Angels. Angels are God’s messengers, pastors of churches, so called in the second and third chapters of Malachi. For God sends preachers as ambassadors to the people, and wills them to be heard as if it were himself. Luke 10; John 13. Let no man therefore tarry until the Lord himself comes down from heaven again and preach[illegible] to us. Even now he preaches to us by his messengers, who preach his, that is to say, the word of Christ; if you contemn them, you contemn Christ. Preachers are called stars, because of their bright and heavenly doctrine, and for their purity of life.[note 6.22] Beware therefore you preachers, that you be not wandering plants, lest you have no light at all, neither in doctrine nor in conversation of life. For then you shall be likened to stars that fall down[illegible] out of heaven, as shall happen hereafter in this book to false teachers.

---

### Paragraph 127

**Original (1561):**

> But those stars are not in the head, or in the fete,[note 6.23] or on [illegible]he back or sides: but in the right hand of Christ. Which [illegible]ing hath in deed a great consolation for the Pastors be in the right hand of God, in Gods protection, neither sh[illegible] any man take them out of his hand. God him self also [illegible] ueth pastors, and furnishes them with necessary good[illegible] of the Church. Therefore is the whole government and gl[illegible] ry his. Wherefore the Apostle says also: he that water[illegible] and plants is nothing, but God that gives increase.

**Modern English:**

But those stars are not in the head, or in the feet, or on the back or sides: but in the right hand of Christ. This thing has indeed a great consolation for pastors, being in God’s protection, neither shall any man take them out of his hand. God himself also upholds pastors, and furnishes them with necessary goods of the Church. Therefore is the whole government and glory his. Wherefore the Apostle says also: he that waters and plants is nothing, but God that gives increase.

---

### Paragraph 128

**Original (1561):**

> Now as concerning the Candelſtickes, there was o[illegible] verily in the Tabernacle of Moses with seven ſockettes,[note 6.24] [illegible] set in seven Candelles. In Salomons temple were ten ca[illegible] delſtickes. The one represented a figure of Christ: And the [illegible] uen thereupon, and the ten, betokened the universality[illegible] Churches, which are lighted all of the only light Chris[illegible] and have of this one, what light so ever they have. And th[illegible] candelſtickes are of Gold. The mystery whereof Arethas e[illegible] pounding: They are all gold, says he, for the purity a[illegible] preciouſnes of faith lying hid in them. And in deed the [illegible] delſtickes of them selves give no light, but be receptacles [illegible] light. So of us arriſeth no light, but darkness. But in [illegible] that light everlasting, set a light in the candlestick, the lig[illegible] shines: if Christ illumine the Church with faith and [illegible] retie, than faith shows forth her self in open confeſſio[illegible] and the pureness of life in conversation. And this the Lord [illegible] quireth of his church in the v. chapter of Matthew. So let yo[illegible] light shine &c. And the apostle in the ii. to the Philipp. In t[illegible] midst of a froward and crooked natio shine like lights the world.

**Modern English:**

As concerning the candlesticks, there was certainly in the Tabernacle of Moses with seven sockets, set in seven candles. In Solomon’s temple there were ten candlesticks. One represented a figure of Christ: and even upon it, and the ten, signified the universality of the churches, which are lit by the only light Christ, and have of this one, whatever light they have. And the candlesticks are of gold. The mystery whereof Arethas expounds: They are all gold, says he, for the purity and preciousness of faith lying hid in them. Indeed, the candlesticks of themselves give no light, but are receptacles of light. So from us arises no light, but darkness. But in that everlasting light, set a light in the candlestick, the light shines: if Christ illumines the church with faith and grace, then faith shows itself in open confession and the purity of life in conversation. And this the Lord requires of his church in the fifth chapter of Matthew. So let your light shine, and so on. And the apostle in the second letter to the Philippians: Shine like lights in the world amidst a crooked and perverse generation.

---

### Paragraph 129

**Original (1561):**

> And hitherto we have handled the consolation of Chri[illegible] and the exposition of that great and celestial vision, when[illegible] we have learned the mysteries of the faith of Christ and of his Church: to the end we should know that Christ is the Lord reignyng in his Church, and applying all things to the salvation of his faithful. That he sends Preachers, teaches by them, and kepeth and defends them. To him be Glory &c.

**Modern English:**

And hitherto we have handled the consolation of Christ and the exposition of that great and celestial vision, whereby we have learned the mysteries of the faith of Christ and of his Church: to the end we should know that Christ is the Lord reigning in his Church, and applying all things to the salvation of his faithful. That he sends preachers, teaches by them, and keeps and defends them. To him be glory and so forth.

---

## Sermon 7: ¶ Of the Epistles revealed out of the throne of God from Christ by an Angel, and received and sent of John. Where also a part of the Epistle to the Epheſians is expounded.

### Paragraph 130

**Original (1561):**

> Unto the Messenger of the congregation of Epheſus write: These things says he that holds the seven Stars in his righthand, and walk in the midst of the Seven golden Candelſtickes. I know thy works, and thy labour and thy patiece, and [illegible]ow thou canneſt not forbear them which [illegible]re evil: And examineſt them which say they are Apostles, and are not: And haste found [illegible]hem liars: and hast suffered, and hast patience: [illegible]nd for my names sake haste laboured, and [illegible]aſte not fainted. Nevertheless I have some what against thee, because thou haste left [illegible]hy first love.

**Modern English:**

To the messenger of the congregation of Ephesus, write: These things says he who holds the seven stars in his right hand and walks among the seven golden lampstands. I know your works, your labor, and your patience, and how you cannot tolerate those who are evil. You examine those who claim to be apostles but are not, and you have found them to be liars. You have endured, showing patience, and for my name’s sake you have labored and have not grown faint. Nevertheless, I have something against you, because you have abandoned your first love.

---

### Paragraph 131

**Original (1561):**

> Your charity hath scene a certain Image of the lord christ,[note 7.1] [illegible]itting on the right hand of the father in glory: yet so, that [illegible] no wise he either forsakes or neglects his Church. Now follows it more fully and plainly howe our Saui [illegible]ur Christ in Heaven executes the office of the high Bishop, and teaches the whole church by his ministers, rebu [illegible]eth, comfort, and reteyneth it in her duty: Finally tour [illegible]eth always away things hurtful, and auaunceth it to greater things. For here follow seven Epistles, to the ſeue Congregations: that is to wit,[note 7.2] unto all the churches in the [illegible]hole world. For this most ample and wholesome doctrine [illegible]aye not be restrained to a few, sins Christ is Biſſhoppe [illegible]niuerſall.[note 7.3]

**Modern English:**

Your charity has seen a certain image of the Lord Christ, sitting on the right hand of the Father in glory: yet so, that in no wise he either forsakes or neglects his Church. Now follows it more fully and plainly how our Savior Christ in Heaven executes the office of the high Bishop, and teaches the whole church by his ministers, rebukes, comforts, and retains it in her duty: finally, turns away always things hurtful, and advances it to greater things. For here follow seven Epistles, to the seven Congregations: that is to wit, unto all the churches in the whole world. For this most ample and wholesome doctrine may not be restrained to a few, since Christ is Bishop universal.

---

### Paragraph 132

**Original (1561):**

> But great is the autoritie of these epistles. For they are revealed from the throne of God, by the son of Go[illegible] speaking by an Angel, which prescribes what is to b[illegible] written in those Epistles: S. John receives and writes th[illegible] same, through Christ his commandment, and sends the[illegible] to the seven congregations: And verily they aperteyne n[illegible] less unto us, than if now the bearer entering into the churc[illegible] should deliver these letters unto us.

**Modern English:**

But great is the authority of these epistles. For they are revealed from the throne of God, by the Son of God speaking through an angel, who dictates what is to be written in those epistles. John receives and writes the same, through Christ’s commandment, and sends them to the seven congregations. And truly, they pertain no less to us than if now a messenger entering into the church should deliver these letters to us.

---

### Paragraph 133

**Original (1561):**

> Moreover in these seven Churches is figured unto v[illegible] the nature,[note 7.4] manners, vices, medicines, rebukes, praises of [illegible] Churches in all times, and what soever is wont to chaun[illegible] about them. Then by examples of most excellent mean[illegible] mixed, of Hypocritical also and wicked. And these our Lo[illegible] doth evidently instruct, reprove, rebuke, and blame, praiſ[illegible] correct, move, exhort, comfort, the same he threatens, an[illegible] promises them also joyful things, &c. This is no light [illegible] common example, but of the son of God, the high and mo[illegible] blessed Bishop: teaching us, howe we should deal with [illegible] congregations after the capacity and disposition of every on[illegible]

**Modern English:**

Moreover, in these seven Churches is figured the nature, manners, vices, medicines, rebukes, praises of all Churches in all times, and whatsoever is wont to happen about them. Then by examples of most excellent means, of hypocritical also and wicked ones. And these our Lord doth evidently instruct, reprove, rebuke, and blame, praise, correct, move, exhort, comfort, the same he threatens, and promises them also joyful things, and so forth. This is no light or common example, but of the Son of God, the high and most blessed Bishop, teaching us how we should deal with congregations after the capacity and disposition of every one.

---

### Paragraph 134

**Original (1561):**

> And not without cause he chouſeth unto him seven th[illegible] most noble cities of Asia. Certain it is, that Asia was of [illegible] first inhabited, and from thence they were dispersed into [illegible] there parts of the world. Certain it is also, that the Deu[illegible] set up his Throne in Asia, and there reigned in men throug[illegible] Idolatry, murder, ambition, avarice, vncleanes, and filth[illegible] pleasures. For the proverb is know, the laughter Iouica[illegible] It is known what the apostle wrote to the Epheſias in th[illegible] iiii. and v. chapter. Therefore wold our Saviour Christ ove[illegible] throw that Throne of the devil, and set up that his Thron[illegible] of rightuouſneſſe and holiness. Therefore goes he firs[illegible] and chiefly to them of Asia, that by their example the whole[illegible] world might be corrected and amended.

**Modern English:**

And not without cause he chooses to the seven most noble cities of Asia. It is certain that Asia was among the first regions inhabited, and from there people were dispersed into other parts of the world. It is also certain that the Devil set up his throne in Asia, and there reigned in men through idolatry, murder, ambition, avarice, uncleanness, and carnal pleasures. For the proverb says, "the laughter of Jovial [?]." It is known what the apostle wrote to the Ephesians in the fourth and fifth chapters. Therefore, our Savior Christ would overthrow that throne of the Devil and set up his throne of righteousness and holiness. Therefore, he goes first and chiefly to those of Asia, that by their example the whole world might be corrected and amended.

---

### Paragraph 135

**Original (1561):**

> [note 7.5]And amongst other cities of Asia and Iouia, Epheſu[illegible] was most famous, called in the old time the light of Aſi[illegible] And amongst the xii. cities of Iouia accounted the princ[illegible] pall.[note 7.6] Neither was there any other richer or more beautifu[illegible] Church scene any where in Asia, than the temple of Diana [illegible] Epheſus. It stood in the midst of the city, a great wond[illegible] of the Greek magnificence, as writes Plinie. This temp[illegible] is said to have ben two hundredth and twenty years in m[illegible] king of all Asia: And set in a fenny ground, that it should feel of no earth quakes nor opening of the earth. The length of [illegible]t was ccccxxv. foot, the breadth ccxx. It had pillars an ho dreth twenty and seven, dedicated of so many kings. Seek the rest out of the epitome of the famous D. Ioachim Vadiane. The apostle S. Paul first illumined this city with the [illegible]eame of the Gospel. Whose epistle to the same city remains, and a plentiful story in the acts of the Apostles. After Paull was executed, John went to Epheſus,[note 7.7] and from thence preached to all Asia: from thence also was he brought to Rome [illegible]o the emperor Domitian: To Epheſus he returned after his exile: and there at length as the ecclesiastical stories do testify, he slept in the Lord.

**Modern English:**

And among other cities of Asia and Ionia, Ephesus was most famous, called in old times the light of Asia. And among the twelve cities of Ionia, it was accounted the principal palace. Neither was there any other church richer or more beautiful anywhere in Asia than the temple of Diana at Ephesus. It stood in the midst of the city, a great wonder of Greek magnificence, as Pliny writes. This temple is said to have been built in two hundred and twenty years by the king of all Asia. And it was set in a marshy ground, so that it would feel no earthquakes or openings of the earth. The length of it was 425 feet, the breadth 220 feet. It had pillars numbering twenty-seven, dedicated by so many kings. Seek the rest from the summary of the famous Doctor Joachim Vadian. The apostle Paul first illuminated this city with the name of the Gospel. His epistle to the same city remains, and a plentiful story in the Acts of the Apostles. After Paul was executed, John went to Ephesus, and from thence preached to all Asia; from thence also he was brought to Rome to the emperor Domitian. To Ephesus he returned after his exile, and there at length, as ecclesiastical stories testify, he slept in the Lord.

---

### Paragraph 136

**Original (1561):**

> And before every epistle, much more to that of the Epheſians, is set a commandment, write.[note 7.8] This commandment gives authority to the writynge, so that we may not axe, whether this writing, ought to be credited? and why it should [illegible]e believed? For here is the expres commandment of God, [illegible]nd the divine authority, whereof curiouſely to inquire is [illegible]hought not without cause unlawful. Moses wrote by the [illegible]ommaundement of God. And by the same commandment of God wrote also the prophets and Apostles. Which than are not their wrytinges believed to be Anthenticall? Certes John said truly and wittily: he that knows God, hey [illegible]eth us: he that is not of God, hears us not.1. John.5. Curious questions cease, where the mind of the godly, or of any poor sheep knows the voice of his Lord and Shepherd.

**Modern English:**

And before every epistle, especially that of the Ephesians, a command is placed. This command lends authority to the writing, so that we may not ask whether this writing ought to be credited, and why it should be believed. For here is God’s express command and divine authority, and to inquire into this with excessive curiosity is not considered unlawful without cause. Moses wrote by God’s command. And the prophets and Apostles also wrote by the same command of God. Then are not their writings believed to be authentic? Certainly John spoke truly and wisely: he who knows God hears us; he who is not of God does not hear us. 1 John 5. Curious questions cease where the mind of the godly, or of any poor sheep, knows the voice of his Lord and Shepherd.

---

### Paragraph 137

**Original (1561):**

> And let no man think, that this epistle being written to one Angel, that is to a Bishop or pastor, appertains nothing to the Church. For to the end of the epistle is added an acclamation. He that hath an ear let him hear what the spirit says to the congregations. Therefore the pastor is named, but the Sheep are not excluded. All degrees and states in the church know, what is said unto them. Ignorauce says: That which is writ to the Romans, cocerneth me nothing. Yet nevertheless it is entitled to the Angel,[note 7.9] to the intent they pastors may be admonished, what the state of the Church is.

**Modern English:**

And let no man think that this letter, being written to one angel—that is, a bishop or pastor—appertains nothing to the church. For at the end of the letter is added an acclamation: “He that hath an ear let him hear what the Spirit says to the congregations.” Therefore, the pastor is named, but the sheep are not excluded. All ranks and states in the church know what is said to them. Ignorance says: “That which is written to the Romans concerns me nothing.” Yet nevertheless it is addressed to the angel [note 7.9], so that pastors may be admonished about the state of the church.

---

### Paragraph 138

**Original (1561):**

> The arg[illegible]meant of the first epistle is thus. Christ declares that he rules over his church, that he takes charge and government of the same. Some thing therein he praises and somewhat he blames: In the mean time he exhorts to repentance, threatening grievous things, and promising most ioyefull. And also he applies this epistle to all churches and comunicateth it to all cogregations in the whole worcester[illegible]. But the epistle is exhortatiue, for it instructs the churches exhorts and directs.

**Modern English:**

The argument of the first epistle is thus. Christ declares that he rules over his church, that he takes charge and government of the same. Some things therein he praises and somewhat he blames; in the meantime he exhorts to repentance, threatening grievous things and promising most joyful ones. And also he applies this epistle to all churches and communicates it to all congregations in the whole world. But the epistle is exhortative, for it instructs the churches, exhorts, and directs.

---

### Paragraph 139

**Original (1561):**

> And first in deed he shows, who he is, from whom th[illegible] epistle proceeds, that he may give authority thereunto: an[illegible] may declare also that he is the head of his church, the B[illegible] shop, Duke and governor. That part is take of the imag[illegible] set forth in the first chapter. And follows the prophetic[illegible] manner of speaking: This says he which holds the ſeu[illegible] Stars in his righthade. For the Prophets say likewiſ[illegible] Thus says the lord God of Israel, thus says the lor[illegible] of hostess. Thus says the Lord, which brought thee out [illegible] Aegipte, &c. And two especial things he repeteth of the f[illegible] mer description,[note 7.10] whereby he will be known and do us to v[illegible] derſtande, howe he being Lord and Bishop rules and m[illegible] keth in his church. First he affirms that he holds in h[illegible] hand the seven stars. The hand is a token of working, [illegible] protection, or deliuerauce. The stars we have heard to [illegible] the ministers, and the ministry of the word, or the church Therefore Christ holds the ministry in the church, and [illegible] ministers works the salvation of the faithful. After he [illegible] firmeth that he walk, not ſlepyng, or doyng nothing [illegible] the midst of seven golden candelſtickes. In the midst [illegible] said, to the end we should understand, that he gives h[illegible] self indifferently to all men, and rules over all with like [illegible] and government.[note 7.11] Fulwel wrote hereof D. France lamb[illegible] What, says he is to walk or to be in the midst of cong[illegible] gations, but to assist them, keep, instruct, help them, [illegible] by all means to watch over them. For the which cause [illegible] says also in the last of S. Matthew. Behold I am with you [illegible] ways unto the worlds end. Hereof you have a most [illegible] figure in the law: wherein amongst other things which[illegible] apperteined to the ministry of the high priest, he had [illegible] charge of oil and of seven candels for those must he pour[illegible] and ſnafte, and pour in oil, when it wanted. So Chris[illegible] the high and true Bishop hath the charge of the seven candelles, that is to say, of all congregations: and is careful that they want not that oil, which is mentioned in the 44. Psalm. He watches, that they want not the fire and light of the verity. Finally he ſnafteth and pourgeth by faith, what thing so ever hath need to be pourged in them. Thus far he. Which things when they hear, which make the Bishop of Rome head of the church, It is marvel if by and by they understand not their folly and madness. Here the lord adds also, that he knows the works to wit all both good and evil, as well of the Bishop as of his Church. For the Lord knows all things, and is head Bishop of the Catholic or universal Church which also remembers the thoughts of all men in the world at one instant: who sees what is donne, and what is not donne, and what things are needful, nothing escapes him. And such in deed ought he to be, that is head universal of his Church. And this sentence is repeated, I know thy works, in the beginning of every epistle. And verily it is full of comfort, when we hear that Christ knows all our doings. For we believe also that he hath a faithful care of all our matters.

**Modern English:**

And first, he shows who he is, from whom the epistle proceeds, that he may give authority thereunto, and may declare also that he is the head of his church, the Bishop, Duke, and governor. That part is taken from the image set forth in the first chapter. And follows the prophetic manner of speaking: This says he which holds the seven stars in his right hand. For the Prophets say likewise: Thus says the Lord God of Israel, thus says the Lord of hosts. Thus says the Lord, which brought thee out of Egypt, &c. And two especial things he repeats of the former description, whereby he will be known and do us understand how he being Lord and Bishop rules and maintains in his church. First, he affirms that he holds in his hand the seven stars. The hand is a token of working, protection, or deliverance. The stars we have heard to be the ministers, and the ministry of the word, or the church. Therefore Christ holds the ministry in the church, and the ministers work the salvation of the faithful. After, he affirms that he walks, not sleeping or doing nothing, in the midst of seven golden candlesticks. In the midst said, to the end we should understand, that he gives himself indifferently to all men, and rules over all with like power and government. Fulwel wrote hereof. What, says he, is to walk or to be in the midst of congregations, but to assist them, keep, instruct, help them, and by all means to watch over them? For which cause he says also in the last of Saint Matthew: Behold, I am with you always, even unto the end of the world. Hereof you have a most excellent figure in the law: wherein amongst other things which pertained to the ministry of the high priest, he had charge of oil and of seven candles; for those must he prepare and snuff, and pour in oil, when it wanted. So Christ, the high and true Bishop, hath the charge of the seven candles, that is to say, of all congregations: and is careful that they want not that oil, which is mentioned in the 44th Psalm. He watches, that they want not the fire and light of the verity. Finally, he snuffs and purges by faith, whatsoever thing so ever hath need to be purged in them. Thus far he. Which things when they hear, which make the Bishop of Rome head of the church, it is marvelous if by and by they understand not their folly and madness. Here the Lord adds also, that he knows the works, to wit, all both good and evil, as well of the Bishop as of his church. For the Lord knows all things, and is head Bishop of the Catholic or universal Church, which also remembers the thoughts of all men in the world at one instant: who sees what is done, and what is not done, and what things are needful, nothing escapes him. And such in deed ought he to be, that is head universal of his Church. And this sentence is repeated, I know thy works, in the beginning of every epistle. And verily it is full of comfort, when we hear that Christ knows all our doings. For we believe also that he hath a faithful care of all our matters.

---

### Paragraph 140

**Original (1561):**

> Now this great Bishop comendeth some things in this congregation of Epheſus. For good works in deed are allowed of Christ, and he praises the same, to the end he might give a spur unto such as run in his way. For first he allows the labour and patience both of the Bishop and Church.[note 7.12] Labour comprises thought and care in the way of God, mortifiyng of the flesh, study of good works, but chiefly the cross and persecution, which the story testifies to have ben extreme and cruel in the time of Domitian. And except the persecuted have patience, they can not endure the labour. Holy patience kepeth us in work and holy labour.

**Modern English:**

This great Bishop commends certain things in the congregation of Ephesus. For good works in deed are allowed by Christ, and he praises them, so that he might encourage those who run in his way. First, he approves the labor and patience of both the Bishop and the Church. Labor encompasses thought and care in the way of God, mortifying the flesh, the study of good works, but chiefly the cross and persecution, which history testifies to have been extreme and cruel in the time of Domitian. And unless the persecuted have patience, they cannot endure the labor. Holy patience keeps us in work and holy labor.

---

### Paragraph 141

**Original (1561):**

> But lest that patience should be stretched to those things wherein to be impatient is accounted praise worthy,[note 7.13] he adds the second point that he praises in them, that thou can not bear evil men. And by these evil he means not weaklings, or such as err without maliciouſnes: But the prophet David says also, Psalm. 119. I have hated the wicked, thy law have I loved. What we should do with the weak in the faith, or with them that err of ignorauce, rather than of o[illegible] ſtinate ſtubbernes, the Apostle hath taught us in the 14. [illegible] the Romans. The example of our Saviour. Hath thaugh[illegible] also, bringing again that strayed Sheep upon his ſhulde[illegible] into the ſhepefolde. Therefore the lord speaks here of t[illegible] obstinate, of the deceivers which delight to err the ſelue[illegible] and to draw others with them into errors, no Christ p[illegible] tience bids to bear with such men.

**Modern English:**

But lest patience should be stretched to those things wherein to be impatient is considered commendable,[note 7.13] he adds the second point that he praises in them, that you cannot bear evil men. And by these evil men he means not weaklings, or those who err without malice: but the prophet David also says, Psalm 119, “I have hated the wicked, your law I have loved.” What we should do with the weak in the faith, or with those who err through ignorance rather than obstinate stubbornness, the Apostle has taught us in the 14th chapter of Romans. The example of our Savior has also taught, bringing again that strayed sheep upon his shoulders into the fold. Therefore, the Lord speaks here of the obstinate, of the deceivers who delight to err themselves and to draw others with them into errors; no Christian patience bids us to bear with such men.

---

### Paragraph 142

**Original (1561):**

> And in the words following he declares of what ſor[illegible] those evil men were. And thou haste examined them whi[illegible] say they be Apostles, and be not, and haste found them[illegible] airs.[note 7.14] Lo he speaks of the false apostles, of whom in ſ. Ioh[illegible] time there was exceadyng great plenty. For they were [illegible] zareans mixing the law with grace, and attributing I[illegible] fication to the law and to our own rightuouſnes. Wh[illegible] the holy and great counsel at Jerusalem condemned, a[illegible] [illegible] pereth in the xv. chapter of the acts of the Apostles. S[illegible] a false Apostle was Hebion. Eusebius mentions in th[illegible] book of the Ecclesiastical story the xxvii. chapter. Here [illegible] was added Cerinthus that heretic not Apostle. There [illegible] more also, whereof some denied the humanity of Chri[illegible] some his deity. Against whom wrote John in his Gos[illegible] and in his Epistle: And Ireney in the first book against h[illegible] tickes. These the Lord denies to be Apostles, or Apoſt[illegible] call: which the Apostles have also denied Acts. 15. And [illegible] wise the Apostle S. John in his epistle canonical: who [illegible] liar, says he, but he that denies Jesus to be Christ? But if such trouble were in Churches whilst the Apoſ[illegible] were yet living,[note 7.15] if there were than so many deceive[illegible] what marvel is it, though in the dregs of the world, wit in this our last time, there be not a few ſoun[illegible] where be they now that wraſte diſſentions and troubles the defence of their error? The Goſpellers them sell[illegible] say they are at diſſention. God is God of concorde, he[illegible] than should I believe that God is among those that [illegible] sent? So might the Sophiſters also have reasoned in th[illegible] [illegible] poſtles time.

**Modern English:**

And in the words following he declares of what sort those evil men were. And you have examined those who say they are Apostles, but are not, and have found them liars. Behold, he speaks of the false apostles, of whom in John’s time there was exceedingly great abundance. For they were Zarbians, mixing the law with grace, and attributing justification to the law and to our own righteousness. Which the holy and great council at Jerusalem condemned, as it appears in the fifteenth chapter of the Acts of the Apostles. Such a false Apostle was Hebion. Eusebius mentions in the book of Ecclesiastical History the twenty-seventh chapter. Here was added Cerinthus, that heretic, not Apostle. There were more also, of whom some denied the humanity of Christ, some his deity. Against whom John wrote in his Gospel and in his Epistle, and Ireney in the first book against heretics. These the Lord denies to be Apostles, or Apostolic, which the Apostles have also denied, Acts 15. And therefore the Apostle Jerome in his canonical epistle: who is a liar, says he, but he that denies Jesus to be Christ? But if such trouble were in the churches while the Apostles were yet living, if there were then so many deceivers, what marvel is it, though in the dregs of the world, that is, in this our last time, there are not a few sounding where they are now that wraste dissensions and troubles in the defense of their error? The Gospelers themselves say they are at dissension. God is God of concord; how then should I believe that God is among those that cause strife? So might the Sophists also have reasoned in the Apostles’ time.

---

### Paragraph 143

**Original (1561):**

> And here have we a pefitte way, in what sort the ch[illegible] ches should work, whileſte troublesome persons like f[illegible] Apostles attempt to divide the Church a ſonder.[note 7.16] For such ringeleaders must be tried and examined: And tried must they be after the christen belefe, and doctrine of the Apostles, and inquisition must be made, whether they be Apostles and true men, or false Apostles and liars. When we shall have found them to be false Apostles and liars, and that they go foreward obstinately in their wickedness, they be not to be suffered: as in deed the Epheſians did not vouch safe to bear with such trompers. And we must know that the Pastors ought one way, and the Christen Magistrate another way, and the people the third way not to abide open heretics. For the Pastor not only bears not with them, in dissembling and taking heed to him self of those wolves, but assails them with wholesome doctrine, and re [illegible]ulſeth them from the ſhepefoldes of Christ: But the Magistrate, because he is a christen Magistrate, and by his duty also, not only as a private person but also as a Magistrate, [illegible]ught to serve Christ, he aught also with the sword of [illegible]uſtice to drive away poison from the church, and to pu [illegible]iſhe manifest blasphemies.[note 7.17] And the people are commaun [illegible]ed, neither to hear them, nor receive them, nor to have [illegible]ny thing to do with heretics, and so not to abide them. They may therefore be ashamed of their naughtiness, and [illegible]retence of their perverse patience, which think it no shame [illegible]o maynteyne Heretics, and to flatter the manifest en [illegible]ies of Christ and the Church. Psalm. 15. He is praised, [illegible]hiche makes not much of the wicked: that is to wit, [illegible]n whose sight the wicked man is vile. Therefore is he [illegible]ghtly blamed, who so ever flatters the ungodly. And [illegible] the hatred in deed is rather against wickedness, than a [illegible]aynſte the person of the wicked, which of it self is com [illegible]aunded to be loved. The Devil at this day reyſeth up the [illegible]de heresies of Hebion, Cerinthus, and of others in Terueto a [illegible]paniarde, and in the Anabaptiſtes, Libertines, and other [illegible]onſters, so that the thing it self, and the danger there [illegible] commands us to watch, and to drive away the most [illegible]uell wolves from the holy Church of Christ, which no [illegible]ertheleſſe do set forth nothing more than patience and cha [illegible]tie, for this intent verily that they might be spared, and might unpunished teach what they lift against Christ, a[illegible] work against his church, yea tear it in pieces with th[illegible] wicked teeth.

**Modern English:**

And here we have a fitting way, in what sort the churches should act, while troublesome persons like false Apostles attempt to divide the Church. For such ringleaders must be tried and examined: and tried they must be according to the Christian belief and doctrine of the Apostles, and inquiry must be made, whether they are Apostles and true men, or false Apostles and liars. When we shall have found them to be false Apostles and liars, and that they go forward obstinately in their wickedness, they are not to be tolerated; as indeed the Ephesians did not vouchsafe to bear with such deceivers. And we must know that the Pastors ought to act one way, and the Christian Magistrate another way, and the people a third way, not to tolerate open heretics. For the Pastor not only bears not with them, by being watchful and wary of those wolves, but assails them with wholesome doctrine, and refuses them from the flocks of Christ. But the Magistrate, because he is a Christian Magistrate, and by his duty also, not only as a private person but also as a Magistrate, ought to serve Christ; he ought also with the sword of justice to drive away poison from the church, and to punish manifest blasphemies.
