And the people are commanded neither to hear them, nor receive them, nor to have anything to do with heretics, and so not to tolerate them. They may therefore be ashamed of their naughtiness, and the slowness of their perverse patience, which thinks it no shame to maintain heretics, and to flatter the manifest enemies of Christ and the Church. Psalm 15. He is praised, who does not think much of the wicked; that is to say, in whose sight the wicked man is vile. Therefore is he rightly blamed, whoever flatters the ungodly. And the hatred in deed is rather against wickedness, than against the person of the wicked, which of itself is commanded to be loved. The Devil at this day raises up the old heresies of Hiero[m?], Ireney[us?], and of others in Terueto a Spaniard, and in the Anabaptists, Libertines, and other monsters, so that the thing itself, and the danger there, commands us to watch, and to drive away the most cruel wolves from the holy Church of Christ, which nevertheless do set forth nothing more than patience and charity, for this intent verily that they might be spared, and might unpunished teach what they list against Christ, and work against his church, yea tear it in pieces with their wicked teeth.

---

### Paragraph 144

**Original (1561):**

> But when these evil men are not suffered, but impugn[illegible] which seduce and are seduced,[note 7.18] a great conflict arriſe[illegible] whereof again are labours, thoughts, carefulnes, watc[illegible] ynges, injuries to be suffered for the name of Christ, and d[illegible] fence of the verity. For unless we be here diligent and p[illegible] tient, the deceivers over come. But herin did the churc[illegible] of the Epheſians behave them notably well, in so much th[illegible] the Lord now commends exceedingly the magnani[illegible] tie, patience and constancy of the pastor and of his churc[illegible] For neither ought these things to be expounded, that [illegible] should refer them unto that patience, whereby evil me[illegible] are suffered, and permitted to proceed in their malice a[illegible] diſceiptfulneſſe. For so should this place strive against th[illegible] things which went a little before. Which thing the [illegible] mon interpretour minded as it seems to have eschew[illegible] For thus he reads, and thou haste patience, and hast ſuff[illegible] red, where it is in the Greek, and hast suffered and hast p[illegible] tience. He altered the order, and would not set, haste suffer[illegible] before: but haste patience: lest any man should vnd[illegible] stand that they had suffered the false Apostles. But set b[illegible] fore patience and put after sufferance: That we might v[illegible] derſtande that they suffered not evil men, but evil wrought by evil men. So they with patience travailed [illegible] Christ's name, to wit, to be maintained against naugh[illegible] heresies. And he adds, thou hast not fainted, being were[illegible] and broken with labours. For we are taught to ouerco[illegible] through patient constancy, which is rightly called in d[illegible] the accomplishment of every good work.

**Modern English:**

But when these evil men are not tolerated, but those who deceive and are deceived are challenged [note 7.18], a great conflict arises, involving labor, thought, care, watchfulness, and injuries to be suffered for the name of Christ, and the defense of the truth. For unless we are diligent and patient here, the deceivers will prevail. But the church of the Ephesians behaved notably well in this regard, so that the Lord now commends exceedingly the courage, patience, and constancy of the pastor and his church. For these things should not be understood as referring to that patience whereby evil men are suffered, and permitted to proceed in their malice and deceitfulness. For so this passage would contradict what came before. This is what the interpreter, Jerome, seems to have intended to avoid. For he reads, “You have patience, and have suffered,” where the Greek says, “You have suffered and have patience.” He altered the order, and would not place “you have suffered” before “you have patience,” lest anyone misunderstand that they had suffered the false apostles. But he placed “patience” first and “suffering” afterward, so that we might understand that they did not suffer evil men, but evil wrought by evil men. Thus, with patience, they labored for Christ’s name, namely, to be maintained against false heresies. And he adds, “You have not fainted,” though weary and broken with labors. For we are taught to overcome through patient constancy, which is rightly called the fulfillment of every good work.

---

### Paragraph 145

**Original (1561):**

> All and every of these things we must apply unto [illegible] and understand with what things we may now also this day please Christ our redemer, king and Bishop, truly we walk in the same steppes, wherein we see the co[illegible] gregation of the Epheſians to have walked.

**Modern English:**

All and every of these things we must apply to our lives and understand with what things we may now also this day please Christ our Redeemer, King and Bishop. Truly, we walk in the same steps wherein we see the congregation of the Ephesians to have walked.

---

### Paragraph 146

**Original (1561):**

> It follows what thing he blamed in the same churc[illegible] that they have left their first love. What time they fir[illegible] received the Gospel by S. Paul, and after by John, a[illegible] other godly men, there was scene a great feruentnes in the words and deeds of the faithefull: which thing may be gathered both by the acts of the Apostles, and also by the epistle of S. Paul to the Epheſians. They loved God and their neighbours with a most fervent zeal. They burned in reforming of manners. But in process of time this heate was well couled, and they wared colder in true godliness. This great mischief he rebukes in them, and as follows desires to have it redressed. And here let us note howe not only revolting Idolatry, and other great crimes are imputed to the church, but also if we slack any thing in holy zeal: so that hereof we may learn, how holy and blameles we ought to be before God. Doubtless we can not here excuse our selves before the divine majesty, which were xxx. years paste more ferueter in this congregation, than we be at this day. &c. Our lord God lighten our minds, that we may please him. To whom be glory.

**Modern English:**

It follows what thing he blamed in the same church that they have left their first love. When they first received the Gospel by Paul, and after by John and other godly men, there was seen a great fervor in the words and deeds of the faithful; which thing may be gathered both by the acts of the Apostles and also by the epistle of Paul to the Ephesians. They loved God and their neighbors with a most fervent zeal. They burned in reforming of manners. But in process of time this heat was well cooled, and they grew colder in true godliness. This great mischief he rebukes in them, and as follows desires to have it redressed. And here let us note how not only revolting from idolatry and other great crimes are imputed to the church, but also if we slack anything in holy zeal; so that hereof we may learn how holy and blameless we ought to be before God. Doubtless we cannot here excuse ourselves before the divine majesty, which has been thirty years past more fervent in this congregation than we are at this day. &c. Our Lord God enlighten our minds, that we may please him. To whom be glory.

---

## Sermon 9: ¶ The second part of the Epistle to the Epheſians where is spoken of Penance and of the Nicolaites.

### Paragraph 147

**Original (1561):**

> Remember therefore from whence thou art fallen, and repent, and do the first works. Or else I will come unto thee shortly, and will remove thy Cadelſticke out of his [illegible]lace, except thou repeat. But this thou hast, [illegible]icauſe thou hate the deeds of the Nicolai [illegible]anes, which deeds I hate also. Let him that [illegible]ath ears, hear what the spirit says to [illegible]he congregations. To him that ouercometh will I give to eat of the tree of life, which is [illegible]n the midst of the Paradise of God.

**Modern English:**

Remember therefore from whence you have fallen, and repent, and do your first works. Or else I will come to you shortly, and will remove your candlestick out of its place, except you repent. But you have this, because you hate the deeds of the Nicolaitanes, which deeds I hate also. Let him that has ears, hear what the Spirit says to the congregations. To him that overcomes will I give to eat of the tree of life, which is in the midst of the Paradise of God.

---

### Paragraph 148

**Original (1561):**

> [note 8.1]The accusations of our Saviour Christ, which he uses [illegible] gainſte his servants that be sins, tend not to this en[illegible] doubtless, that men overwhelmed with reproaches might b[illegible] ashamed, despair, and perish: but rather that they should am[illegible] be restored and live. Therefore the Lord Jesus to his blameyn[illegible] by and by annexeth an exhortation to repentance, that th[illegible] may be saved: And also describes the true and lawful penau [illegible]

**Modern English:**

The accusations our Savior Christ uses against his servants who are sinners do not, without doubt, aim to cause men overwhelmed with reproaches to be ashamed, despair, and perish; but rather that they should be restored and live. Therefore, the Lord Jesus, immediately after his rebukes, adds an exhortation to repentance, that they may be saved. And also describes the true and lawful penalty.

---

### Paragraph 149

**Original (1561):**

> For we heard what thing he rebuked in the cogregati[illegible] of the Epheſians: let us hear now what the lord require[illegible] of the same, and how he seeks to have the error reform[illegible] verily by repentance, to the which he exhorts. For [illegible] have said that the Lord strikes and heals: chiefly in th[illegible] case. Which doctrine surely is proper and perpetual to th[illegible] church of Christ.

**Modern English:**

For we heard what thing he rebuked in the congregations of the Ephesians: let us hear now what the Lord requires of the same, and how he seeks to have the error reformed verily by repentance, to which he exhorts. For he has said that the Lord strikes and heals: chiefly in this case. Which doctrine surely is proper and perpetual to the church of Christ.

---

### Paragraph 150

**Original (1561):**

> He mentions chiefly of three things in this matter, in his counsel or exhortation to amendment.[note 8.2] First he co[illegible] sells or exhorts to remebre, [illegible] from which[illegible] they are fallen: that is to say, with how great love they ha[illegible] burned hitherto, and now howe could they be wared: [illegible] how fortunate and blessed a state they have stand hither[illegible] and now in howe unfortunate and shameful they lie [illegible] For the acknoweledging of the trespass is the beginning repentance,[note 8.3] if being illumined by faith we consider we[illegible] what benefits we have lost, and in what misery we no[illegible] are in. He that weans he has lost nothing, will no[illegible] be moved to make any search or inquisition, he that th[illegible] keth him self to have fallen from no felicity, he will thin[illegible] of nothing how he may be restored. Therefore in amen[illegible] meant of life it behooves that acknoweledgyng and conf[illegible] zion of sins go before, by the which we may lament b[illegible] fore God our poverty and misery. And in deed they fa[illegible] not from felicity, which were never in any felicity. The[illegible] fore say we,[note 8.4] that holy men may fall, and also be reſtor[illegible] by repentance. Than after the acknoweledgyng of our [illegible] ſerie follows repentance: that is the coming again[illegible] our mind, that we go not forth on always like mad[illegible] men and fools, walking in the way of vanity an[illegible] vnrightuouſneſſe.[note 8.5] For repentance is a returning a co[illegible] uerſion and change, whereby we are turned away fro[illegible] evil, and are converted to good, in returning to our wit, and working rightuouſnes.

**Modern English:**

He speaks chiefly of three things in this matter, in his counsel or exhortation to amendment. First, he reminds or exhorts them to remember from what they have fallen: that is to say, with how great love they had burned hitherto, and now how they could have strayed; how fortunate and blessed a state they had maintained until now, and now in how unfortunate and shameful a state they lie. For the acknowledgment of transgression is the beginning of repentance, if, being illumined by faith, we consider what benefits we have lost and in what misery we now are. He who believes he has lost nothing will not be moved to make any search or inquiry; he who thinks he has fallen from no felicity will think nothing of how he may be restored. Therefore, in the amendment of life, it behooves that acknowledgment and confession of sins go before, by which we may lament before God our poverty and misery. And indeed, they do not fall from felicity who were never in any felicity. Therefore, we say that holy men may fall, and also be restored by repentance. Then, after the acknowledgment of failure, follows repentance: that is, the turning again of our mind, that we do not go forth always like madmen and fools, walking in the way of vanity and unrighteousness. For repentance is a returning and change, whereby we are turned away from evil and are converted to good, in returning to our reason and working righteousness.

---

### Paragraph 151

**Original (1561):**

> For last of all follows that, which expounds this repentance. Do the former works: To wit be hot again in love, work the works of faith, which are fruits worthy of repentance. Behold there needs no new laws,[note 8.6] or long disputations concerning amendment. Briefly, do the former works, not of the flesh, but those which thou began when thou first received the Gospel, and waſt borne again in Christ. Finally this is the true reformation, to do the former works of God. Not the latter, which the error of the world hath devised. This is verily, and shall be the true repentance, to wit, the acknowledging of the sin, the conversion unto God and to good, and a turning away from the Devil and from evil, and working of the first good, or Godly rightuouſnes of the faith in Christ. There be many and sundry disputations of repentance and parts of the same, of the contrition of the heart, confession of the mouth, and satisfaction of that work: But like as there is none briefer than this of Jesus Christ, so verily is there none better or more certain.

**Modern English:**

Finally, what follows explains this repentance. Do the former works: namely, be fervent again in love, perform the works of faith, which are fruits worthy of repentance. Behold, there is no need for new laws [note 8.6] or lengthy debates concerning amendment. Briefly, do the former works, not of the flesh, but those which you began when you first received the Gospel and were born again in Christ. Ultimately, this is the true reformation: to do the former works of God, not the latter, which the error of the world has devised. This truly is, and will be, the true repentance, namely, the acknowledgment of sin, conversion to God and goodness, and a turning away from the Devil and evil, and the working of the first righteousness, the righteousness of faith in Christ. There are many and diverse discussions about repentance and its parts, concerning the contrition of the heart, the confession of the mouth, and the satisfaction of that work. But just as none is briefer than that of Jesus Christ, so truly is none better or more certain.

---

### Paragraph 152

**Original (1561):**

> To these his exhortations and Godly coucelles he adds threatenings most grievous, if happily peril might pearſe,[note 8.7] that love could not ſtyre or move. And he speaks but few words in deed. But he understands a great evil that can not be spoken nor declared. Except thou repent, I will remove thy candelſtick out of his place. The candelſtick, as the lord him self hath expounded it, is the church. Which stands in her place, whyleſt she leans to Christ: and is of Christ preserved, so long as the preaching of the verity is in the same maintained, and is in deed the church of Christ: She is removed out of her place, when she is without the preaching of the truth, and now leans no more upon Christ, nether is of him defended, but is forsaken, and is no longer in deed the church of Christ. This is done of Christ himself, by his [illegible]uſt judgment, what time our vnthanfulnes, and life that can not repent, drives God to depart from us, to relinquish us to our error and darkness, and leave us to diſceauable men, &c. This sense Arethas acknowledges, who: to remove the church, saith he, is, when it is lest bare and destitute of Gods grace: by reason of the which nakedness, it wavers in doubtful perplexity and in storms that are cast to her by wicked men. And verily we se how at this day the church of the Epheſians is removed out of her place, and no longer inioyet[illegible] the wholesome doctrine of Christ nor that it stands upon th[illegible] wholesome rock Jesus Christ[illegible] But is oppressed with the peſ[illegible] ferous doctrine or rather madness of Mahomet, and lies i[illegible] sorrow under the fete of the Turks. We see at this day i[illegible] Germany (more is the pititie) many candelſtickes removed out of their place, not without the great triumph of Satha and loss of souls. Moreover that same is also to be observed in this threatening, that without terror he saith, I will come to thee shortly. For it is a Phrase of speech. Fo[illegible] we say also,[note 8.8] I will come to thee by and by: That is, I will come to revenge and punish, and that perauenture sooner th[illegible] thou lokeſt for. Most certainly, when so ever I chance [illegible] come, I will take punishment of thee. Let no man therfo[illegible] think to escape unpunished in a life that can not repent.

**Modern English:**

To these his exhortations and godly cautions, he adds most grievous threatenings, if happily peril might pierce, that love could not restrain or move. And he speaks but few words in truth. But he understands a great evil that cannot be spoken or declared. “Unless you repent, I will remove your lampstand from its place.” The lampstand, as the Lord himself has explained, is the church. It stands in its place while it leans toward Christ; and it is preserved by Christ so long as the preaching of the truth is maintained within it. It is, in truth, the church of Christ. She is removed from her place when she is without the preaching of the truth, and no longer leans upon Christ, nor is defended by him, but is forsaken, and is no longer, in truth, the church of Christ. This is done by Christ himself, through his just judgment, when our unthankfulness and a life that cannot repent drives God to depart from us, to abandon us to our error and darkness, and to leave us to deceivable men, and so on. This sense Arethas acknowledges, who says that to remove the church is when it is left bare and destitute of God’s grace; because of this nakedness, it wavers in doubtful perplexity and in storms cast upon it by wicked men. And truly we see how at this day the church of the Ephesians is removed from its place, and no longer enjoys the wholesome doctrine of Christ, nor that it stands upon the wholesome rock Jesus Christ, but is oppressed by the pestiferous doctrine, or rather madness, of Muhammad, and lies in sorrow under the feet of the Turks. We see at this day in Germany (more to be lamented) many lampstands removed from their place, not without the great triumph of Satan and the loss of souls. Moreover, that same is also to be observed in this threatening, that without terror he says, “I will come to you shortly.” For it is a phrase of speech. For we also say, “I will come to you by and by”: that is, I will come to avenge and punish, and perhaps sooner than you look for. Most certainly, whenever I happen to come, I will take punishment of you. Let no man therefore think to escape unpunished in a life that cannot repent.

---

### Paragraph 153

**Original (1561):**

> [note 8.9]Again where the Lord repeteth, except thou repent, he plai[illegible] lie testifies, that the bosom of Gods mercy and clemency, i[illegible] ready open, if we do penance, how soever we have offended him before, in the mean time we learn here openly and moſ[illegible] certainly that we can by no counsels or consultations, [illegible] no armies nor policies preuaile one whit on our perils, [illegible] les we repeat. Therefore unless we will have our churches to [illegible] subverted, and given over to be seduced and diſtroied of the devil [illegible] his seducers, let us repeat in time, and receive again the first love.

**Modern English:**

Again, where the Lord repeats, except you repent, he plainly testifies that the bosom of God’s mercy and clemency is readily open, if we do penance, however we have offended him before. In the meantime, we learn here openly and most certainly that we can by no counsels or consultations, by no armies nor policies, prevail against our perils unless we repent. Therefore, unless we will have our churches subverted and given over to be seduced and destroyed by the devil and his seducers, let us repent in time and receive again the first love.

---

### Paragraph 154

**Original (1561):**

> [note 8.10]Again he comendeth the singular virtue in this cogregat[illegible] especially for that they have hated the doings of the Nicolait[illegible] which God him self also hates. Let us here mark ever[illegible] word. He saith not thou did sly, or eschew and contin[illegible] but thou hast hated. The force of hatred is great, moving [illegible] to persecute, that thou hate.

**Modern English:**

Again he commends the singular virtue in this congregation, especially for that they have hated the deeds of the Nicolaitans, which God himself also hates. Let us mark every word here. He does not say you did slyly or shun and abstain, but that you have hated. The force of hatred is great, moving one to persecute, that you hate.

---

### Paragraph 155

**Original (1561):**

> [note 8.11]Moreover he saith not, thou hast hated the Nicolaita[illegible] but the works of the Nicolaitans. For we ought to hate t[illegible] person of no man for it self, but the vice in the man so the w[illegible] we shall forsake it, we should love the man with all our hart. A[illegible] that must needs be a great evil, which God him self confess[illegible] that he hates. Here all congregations shall understand, that th[illegible] ought also to hate by all means the heresy and abominatio of the Nicolaitans. Albeit that at this day the name be extingwiſhed yet the heresy and abomination of the Nicolaitans remains.

**Modern English:**

Moreover, he does not say, “You have hated the Nicolaitans,” but the works of the Nicolaitans. For we ought to hate no man’s person for itself, but the vice in the man; so that if we forsake it, we should love the man with all our heart. It must needs be a great evil, which God himself confesses that he hates. Here all congregations shall understand that they ought also by all means to hate the heresy and abomination of the Nicolaitans. Albeit that at this day the name be extinguished, yet the heresy and abomination of the Nicolaitans remains.

---

### Paragraph 156

**Original (1561):**

> This Nicolas was of Antioche, one of the seven deacos, of whom mention is made in the.6. chap. of the Acts. He is said to have revolted from the purity of faith, as Iudas did: And where he was before a Gentile (for it is said howe he was a Proselyte) he returned in certain things to gentility as a dog to his vomit. The Nicolaitas be also Gnoſtici, and conſortes of Carpocrates, filthy and most wicked people. Clemes excuses this Nicolas somewhat in Eusebius in the.3. book. 29 chap. of the Ecclesiastical history. But that excuse seems not sufficient or just, since all the auncientes with one mouth accuse the same, and namely the very judgement of God in this present and in the Epistles following. Ireneus condemns him of this same place, in the first book against the Valentinians chap. 27. &c. Tertulian in thend of Heret. preſcrip. touches here wittily the facts of the Nicolaitans, and detests the same. Nevertheless he expounds them not, but passes them over. And I know not how wittily Epiphanius hath uttered and declared in order the wicked and abominable acts, neither to be thought nor told, and most beastly filthines, such as hath not been heard of the heresy. 25.26.27. &. 31. &c. Philaſtrius also and S. Austen have touched the Nicolaitans, either of them in their register of heresies. Shamefaſt [illegible]es will not suffer me to recite. It is enough, if we know that same which in the epistle to Pergamum the Lord him self hath expounded, calling the doctrine of Nicolaitans, the doctrine of Balaa the ſouthſaier. But who knows not, what counsel he gave to Balaac king of Moab and of Madian, and how he prostituted fair wenches to the young men of Israel, by whose acquaintance enticed, they both defiled the selves with fornicatio, and did eat also of meates offered up to Idols, being made partakers of Baalpeor. Let him that will read Ioſe [illegible]hus in the iiii. book of Antiquities. cap. 6. And doubtless the [illegible]acrifices of the Nicolaitans seem to differ nothing from the [illegible]ecrettes of Priapus, or Gerecinthia, or the mother of God [illegible]es, and the nightly service of Bacchus. Ireneus signifies [illegible]penly that the Carpocratites, who are called also Gnoſtici, did not abhor images, but paincted and facioned to the ſelue[illegible] the Images of Jesus and Paul, with the Images of certi[illegible] Philosophers: And that the Image of Jesus, as they talk, was made expressly of Pilate, who commanded the face [illegible] Jesus to be painted lively. &c. But howsoever that was, th[illegible] is certain, that thactes of the Nicolaitans were evil report[illegible] of for their fornications and adulteries: And that the Nicol[illegible] tans abſteined not from images, nor from meates offred [illegible] idols.[note 8.12] Against the which error S. Paul wrote also man[illegible] things.

**Modern English:**

This Nicholas was of Antioch, one of the seven deacons, of whom mention is made in the sixth chapter of the Acts. He is said to have revolted from the purity of faith, as Judas did: and where he was before a Gentile (for it is said how he was a Proselyte) he returned in certain things to gentility as a dog to his vomit. The Nicolaitans are also Gnostics, and associates of Carpocrates, filthy and most wicked people. Clement excuses this Nicholas somewhat in Eusebius in the third book, 29th chapter of the Ecclesiastical History. But that excuse seems not sufficient or just, since all the ancients with one mouth accuse the same, and namely the very judgment of God in this present and in the Epistles following. Irenaeus condemns him of this same matter, in the first book against the Valentinians, chapter 27 and following. Tertullian, at the end of Prescribed Heretics, touches here wittily the facts of the Nicolaitans, and detests the same. Nevertheless he expounds them not, but passes them over. And I know not how wittily Epiphanius has uttered and declared in order the wicked and abominable acts, neither to be thought nor told, and most beastly filthiness, such as has not been heard of in heresy, 25, 26, 27, and 31 and following. Philastrius also and Augustine have touched the Nicolaitans, either of them in their register of heresies. Shameful details will not allow me to recite. It is enough, if we know that which in the epistle to Pergamum the Lord himself has expounded, calling the doctrine of the Nicolaitans the doctrine of Balaam the false prophet. But who knows not what counsel he gave to Balaac, king of Moab and of Midian, and how he prostituted fair women to the young men of Israel, by whose acquaintance they both defiled themselves with fornication, and did eat also of meats offered up to idols, being made partakers of Baalpeor. Let him who will read Josephus in the fourth book of Antiquities, chapter 6. And doubtless the sacrifices of the Nicolaitans seem to differ nothing from the secrets of Priapus, or Gerecinthia, or the mother of God cults, and the nightly service of Bacchus. Irenaeus plainly signifies that the Carpocratites, who are also called Gnostics, did not abhor images, but painted and fashioned to the likeness the images of Jesus and Paul, with the images of certain philosophers: and that the image of Jesus, as they say, was made expressly by Pontius Pilate, who commanded the face of Jesus to be painted lively. But howsoever that was, it is certain that the acts of the Nicolaitans were in evil report for their fornications and adulteries: and that the Nicolaitans abstained not from images, nor from meats offered to idols. Against this error Paul also wrote many things.

---

### Paragraph 157

**Original (1561):**

> Hereof let us learn to abhor and fly fornication, a[illegible] never to think of restoring the Stews or other places [illegible] whoredom. Fie for shame. Let us learn hereby to keep h[illegible] virginity, single life, and lawful marriages, fly those d[illegible] the Nicolaitans, Let us learn hereby, to keep well our sell[illegible] from Idols, idolatry, and from all strange kinds of w[illegible] ſhippinges. All those God hates.

**Modern English:**

Hereof let us learn to abhor and flee fornication, and never to think of restoring brothels or other places of whoredom. Fie for shame. Let us learn hereby to keep virginity, single life, and lawful marriages, and flee those called the Nicolaitans. Let us learn hereby to keep ourselves well from idols, idolatry, and from all strange kinds of worshipings. All those God hates.

---

### Paragraph 158

**Original (1561):**

> [note 8.13]And with an acclamation he pearſeth the ears of all me[illegible] moving all to attentiuenes and holy obedience. And apply[illegible] also this doctrine to all times and to all congregation[illegible] the world. He used his wonted speech, repeated so oft in t[illegible] Gospel: He that hath ears to hear, let him hear. Not th[illegible] it is in our strength to hear and obey God. For God pr[illegible] pares our ears, and with his grace frames and draw[illegible] our hearts. And let them to whom the grace of God is g[illegible] ted, beware least through their negligence, vanity, and lig[illegible] nes they lose it not. Let them show such diligence, as G[illegible] in his word requires and prescribes. They that do th[illegible] have ears to hear. He saith therefore, take the heed to wh[illegible] God now speaks, and whose hearts now he ſtyreth a[illegible] moves, that you lose not this grace through your neglig[illegible] be diligent, attentife, and circumspect, ſtyryng up in yo[illegible] selves the gift of God.

**Modern English:**

And with an acclamation he pierced the ears of all men, moving all to attentiveness and holy obedience. And he applied this doctrine to all times and to all congregations throughout the world. He used his accustomed speech, repeated so often in the Gospel: “He who has ears to hear, let him hear.” It is not in our strength to hear and obey God. For God prepares our ears, and with his grace frames and draws our hearts. And let those to whom the grace of God is given, beware lest through their negligence, vanity, and lightness they lose it not. Let them show such diligence as God in his word requires and prescribes. They that do have ears to hear. He says therefore, take heed to what God now speaks, and whose hearts he now stirs and moves, that you lose not this grace through your negligence. Be diligent, attentive, and circumspect, stirring up in yourselves the gift of God.

---

### Paragraph 159

**Original (1561):**

> [note 8.14]Now also he provokes to dilligece by authority diui[illegible] The spirit of God speaks and reveals these things, [illegible] the spirit of men or of error, for God speaks by his ſ[illegible] rite, which is red to be the spirit both of the father and o[illegible] son. Moreover he applies all and every thing to all co[illegible] gregations, where he saith, what the spirit saith to the co[illegible] gregations, not to the congregation. It is now than manifest, and out of all controversy,[note 8.15] that those seven churches do represent a figure of all churches throughout the whole world, and that all they be instructed in those seven.

**Modern English:**

Now also he incites to diligence by authority. The Spirit of God speaks and reveals these things, not the spirit of men or of error, for God speaks by his right, which is recognized as the spirit both of the Father and of the Son. Moreover, he applies all things to every congregation, where he says, “What the Spirit says to the congregations,” not to the congregation. It is now manifest, and beyond all controversy, that those seven churches represent a figure of all churches throughout the whole world, and that all are instructed in those seven.

---

### Paragraph 160

**Original (1561):**

> Furthermore least any thing should want to the just exhortation unto repentance, to faith and diligence, last he annexeth a most ample promise, and uses an allegorical speech, that it might have the more grace with it.[note 8.16] To them that overcome he promises to give the fruit of the tree of life, planted in the paradise of God. And alludeth to the.2. Chap. of Genesis. And he tranſlateth the sense from earthly things to celestial. The paradise[note 8.17] of God (by the which some understand the church) is that eurlaſting bless and felicity, whereof the Lord spake to the thief, saying: This day shalt thou be with me in paradise. Herein is the tree of life Christ, communicating to us his eternal life: Which we enjoy and have the fruition of, whilst being conveyed into heaven by him and with him we live. Finally this is that Ambrosia or Godly drink, which the heavenly father gives us to drink. But this great and wonderful good chances not to every one, but only to him that ouercometh. For Adam had not overcome, but vanquished had died. If we therefore shall overcome the flesh, the Devil and the world, and that through Christ, we shall live also in the world to come with Christ.

**Modern English:**

Furthermore, so that nothing might be lacking in the proper exhortation to repentance, faith, and diligence, he adds a most ample promise and employs an allegorical speech, so that it might have the greater grace. To those who overcome, he promises to give the fruit of the tree of life, planted in the paradise of God. He alludes to the second chapter of Genesis and translates the meaning from earthly things to celestial. The paradise [note 8.17] of God (which some understand to be the church) is that everlasting blessedness and felicity, of which the Lord spoke to the thief, saying, “This day you will be with me in paradise.” Herein is the tree of life, Christ, communicating to us his eternal life, which we enjoy and experience while being conveyed into heaven by him and living with him. Finally, this is that Ambrosia or godly drink, which the heavenly Father gives us to drink. But this great and wonderful good does not happen to everyone, but only to him who overcomes. For Adam did not overcome; he had been vanquished and would have died. If, therefore, we overcome the flesh, the Devil, and the world, and that through Christ, we shall also live in the world to come with Christ.

---

### Paragraph 161

**Original (1561):**

> The complutention book hath, which is in the midst of [illegible]he Paradise of my God. And Arethas expounds it,[note 8.18] and [illegible]ayth: Let no man herewith be offended. All humble things [illegible]gre to the dispensation of the incarnation, which was made for our cause, since that he himself in the Gospel saith: [illegible] ascend unto my father, and your father, to my God, and [illegible]o your God. &c.

**Modern English:**

The commentary book has, which is in the midst of the Paradise of my God. And Arethas explains it, and says: Let no man be offended by this. All humble things pertain to the dispensation of the incarnation, which was made for our cause, since he himself in the Gospel says: I ascend unto my Father, and your Father, to my God, and to your God. &c.

---

### Paragraph 162

**Original (1561):**

> And thus far hitherto concerning the Epistle of Jesus Christ by John to the Epheſians, and what profit our churches also, and every of us may receive thereof. The Lord lighten the eyes of our mind.

**Modern English:**

And thus far, concerning the Epistle of Jesus Christ by John to the Ephesians, and what benefit our churches, and each of us, may receive from it. May the Lord enlighten the eyes of our understanding.

---

## Sermon 9: ¶ The second Epistle of Jesus Christ by John to them of Smyrna is expounded. And is an exhortati [illegible][illegible] to patience, and consolation in afflictions.

### Paragraph 163

**Original (1561):**

> ANd unto the Angel of the cogr[illegible] gation of Smyrna write. These[illegible] things saith he that is first an[illegible] the last, which was dead and i[illegible] alive. I know thy works, and t[illegible] bulations and poverty, but thou art rich[illegible] And I know the blasphemy of them which call them selves Jews, and are not: but a[illegible] the congregation of Satan. Fear none [illegible] those things which thou shalt suffer. Behol[illegible] the Devil shall cast some of you into prison[illegible] to tempte you, and you shall have tribulat[illegible] ten days. Be faithful unto the death, and [illegible] will give thee a crown of life. Let him th[illegible] hath ears, hear, what the spirit saith to congregations, he that ouercometh shall n[illegible] be hurt of the second death.

**Modern English:**

And unto the angel of the congregation of Smyrna write. These things saith he that is first and last, which was dead and is alive. I know thy works, and thy labors and poverty, but thou art rich. And I know the blasphemy of them which call themselves Jews, and are not: but are the congregation of Satan. Fear none of those things which thou shalt suffer. Behold, the Devil shall cast some of you into prison to tempt you, and you shall have tribulations for ten days. Be faithful unto death, and I will give thee a crown of life. Let him that hath ears, hear what the Spirit saith to congregations; he that overcometh shall not be hurt of the second death.

---

### Paragraph 164

**Original (1561):**

> [note 9.1]Jesus Christ from the right hand of the father, throu[illegible] the ministry of an angel by the Apostle and Euange[illegible] S. John, exhorts the congregations of Smyrna, than [illegible] flicted with all kind of evils for the word of God, unto [illegible] feraunce, and comfort the same sighing now under [illegible] cross, promising great things to them that overcome. A[illegible] verily there can not of this manner and in this matter a be[illegible] or briefer exhortation and consolation be found. For in [illegible] wise it is couched of the eternal wisdom of the father, [illegible] unto all times,[note 9.2] and to all that morn under the cross it [illegible] right well agree. For like as Christ at the right hand of [illegible] father is the catholic or universal Bishop: so verily is [illegible] doctrine general, which he him self also applies to all c[illegible] gregations in the end of this Epistle, and in others. And ſ[illegible] he declares that he loves his church, and is present in the same by his power and aid.

**Modern English:**

Jesus Christ, from the right hand of the Father, through the ministry of an angel by the Apostle and Evangelist John, exhorts the congregations of Smyrna, afflicted with all kinds of evils for the word of God, to endurance and comfort, easing their present suffering under the cross, promising great things to those who overcome. Truly, there can be no exhortation or consolation of this manner or in this matter that is briefer. For it is wisely couched in the eternal wisdom of the Father, applicable to all times, and fitting right well to all who mourn under the cross. For just as Christ at the right hand of the Father is the catholic or universal Bishop, so too is his doctrine general, which he himself applies to all congregations in the end of this Epistle and in others. And he declares that he loves his church, and is present in it by his power and aid.

---

### Paragraph 165

**Original (1561):**

> And verily it is to be marveled,[note 9.3] that nothing is blamed in this church, since that some fault is found in manner with all others. Therefore was the church of Smyrna right excellent, how be it not without any spirit. For the Lord of his goodness doth not impute unto us small faults (of the which the Prophet speaks, who shall say, my heart is clean? And from my hid sins cleanse me) so that there be a fervent desire or zeal of Godliness in us, and that we be void of great enormities.

**Modern English:**

And indeed it is to be marveled that nothing is blamed in this church, since some fault is found in manner with all others. Therefore the church of Smyrna was truly excellent, though not without some shortcomings. For the Lord, in his goodness, does not hold small faults against us—of which the Prophet speaks, who will say, “My heart is clean? And from my hidden sins cleanse me”—so long as there is a fervent desire or zeal for godliness in us, and we are void of great evils.

---

### Paragraph 166

**Original (1561):**

> First is showed, unto whom this heavenly letter is sent,[note 9.4] to the Pastor of the church of Smyrna, and to the whole flock. For the captain is said to have ſoughten or fled, or to have taken peace, when the whole army together with him hath done this. And the stories bear witness that Policarpus was that same messenger or pastor of the church of Smyrna, ordained of the Apostles the selves,[note 9.5] namely of S. John, Bishop there, and that he lived in the mystery of this congregation lxxxvi. years. For so many he accounts him self before the Lieutenant Herod, what time he was brought to execution. For in the fourth persecution of the church, Aurelius Antoninus, and Aurelius Comodus, being Emperors, he was taken and brought to the governor: And at length for the open and sincere confessing of Christ he was burnt. He had this very much in his mouth: That nothing ought to be received for true, unless it were known to be set forth by the Apostles. Ireneus affirms that when he was a child he saw this old father a man of great years and reverence, in the third book and third Chapter. against heresies, where he tells many things of him besides. As also doth Eusebius in the iiii. book of theccle. history [illegible]he xiiii. and xv. chapters. And S. Hierom in the register of [illegible]he famous writers of the Church. Eusebius in his Chro [illegible]icis, notes that he suffered Martyrdom in the year of [illegible]ur Lord a. C ixx. Whereby it appears that he was [illegible]ade Bishop of Smyrna in the year of our Lord lxxxiiii. [illegible]r there about. For we said even now, that he had been in that ministry lxxxvi. years. And therefore had he been Bishop of Smyrna many years before the setting forth of th[illegible] Apocalypse which was written in the xcvii. year, would[illegible] God all Pastors would set before their eyes this good Policarpus to be followed, of whom there remains a not[illegible] ble Epistle to the Philippians.

**Modern English:**

First, it is shown to whom this heavenly letter is sent, to the pastor of the church of Smyrna, and to the whole flock. For the captain is said to have sought peace or fled, or to have taken peace, when the whole army together with him has done this. And the histories bear witness that Polycarp was that same messenger or pastor of the church of Smyrna, ordained by the Apostles themselves, namely by John the Apostle, Bishop there, and that he lived in the mystery of this congregation eighty-six years. For so many years he declared himself before the Lieutenant Herod, when he was brought to execution. For in the fourth persecution of the church, Aurelius Antoninus and Aurelius Commodus, being Emperors, he was taken and brought before the governor. And at length, for the open and sincere confessing of Christ, he was burned. He had this very much in his mouth: That nothing ought to be received as true, unless it were known to be set forth by the Apostles. Irenaeus affirms that when he was a child he saw this old father, a man of great years and reverence, in the third book and third chapter against heresies, where he tells many things of him besides. As also does Eusebius in the fourth book of the ecclesiastical history, the fourteenth and fifteenth chapters. And Jerome in the register of famous writers of the church. Eusebius in his Chronicles notes that he suffered martyrdom in the year of our Lord 190. Whereby it appears that he was made Bishop of Smyrna in the year of our Lord eighty-four or thereabout. For we said even now that he had been in that ministry eighty-six years. And therefore, had he been Bishop of Smyrna many years before the setting forth of the Apocalypse, which was written in the ninety-seventh year, would God all pastors would set before their eyes this good Polycarp to be followed, of whom there remains a notable Epistle to the Philippians.

---

### Paragraph 167

**Original (1561):**

> After again is the author of the Epistle[note 9.6] declared, which[illegible] is set forth with two titles, taken out of the former viſi[illegible] of John and deſcriptio of Christ. Thus saith the first and [illegible] last. &c. Whereby is signified the eternal divinity of Christ[illegible] which wants beginning and ending. And of him self is [illegible] uerlaſting. There is added, that he was dead, and lives again[illegible] that is to wit, hath risen from the dead. And this begi[illegible] ning accords right well to the matter. For they perceiu[illegible] that whosoever are afflicted for Christ and his Gospel of m[illegible] mighty kings and princes, have a Lord and patron m[illegible] mighty and more faithful, which in no wise can be ou[illegible] come. Who can also in death keep his, like as he raised [illegible] Christ from the dead, to the intent we might have an op[illegible] testimony, that we shall live with Christ, even in death it se[illegible]

**Modern English:**

After again is the author of the Epistle [note 9.6] declared, which is set forth with two titles, taken from the former visions of John and the description of Christ. Thus saith the first and the last. &c. Whereby is signified the eternal divinity of Christ, which lacks beginning and ending. And of himself is everlasting. There is added that he was dead, and lives again, that is to wit, has risen from the dead. And this beginning accords well to the matter. For they perceive that whoever are afflicted for Christ and his Gospel, even mighty kings and princes, have a Lord and patron mighty and more faithful, which in no wise can be overcome. Who can also in death keep his, like as he raised Christ from the dead, to the intent we might have an opportunity, that we shall live with Christ, even in death it seems.

---

### Paragraph 168

**Original (1561):**

> [note 9.7]And now comes he to the matter it self, and the which[illegible] thing he repeteth in all Epistles, he saith here also: I kno[illegible] thy works, to wit both good and evil. Think not that [illegible] neither know nor care for thy matters. Thou art verily to[illegible] ten out in my hands, I know, see and care for thee and [illegible] thine. And these things boeth provoke us marvelously virtue, when we know that we have God a loker on, [illegible] hath a care of us: And also comfort us greatly, which vnd[illegible] stand, how he that loves us, and in no matter neglect[illegible] hath us always as it were before his eyes.

**Modern English:**

And now he comes to the matter itself, and the thing which he repeats in all his letters, he says here also: I know your works, both good and evil. Do not think that I neither know nor care about your affairs. You are certainly laid out in my hands; I know, see, and care for you and all yours. And these things wonderfully provoke us to virtue, when we know that we have God watching over us, who has a care for us. Also, they greatly comfort us, who suffer, knowing that he who loves us and in no matter neglects us, has us always as it were before his eyes.

---

### Paragraph 169

**Original (1561):**

> And here particularly he declares what he knew: A[illegible] first in deed the afflictions,[note 9.8] which verily they suffered in t[illegible] present persecution of the Emperor Domitian. And aff[illegible] tion is as it were a general word, to the four kinds fo[illegible] wyng. For he rehearses, touching their substance the ſ[illegible] ling of their goods, and their poverty: In their name [illegible] estimation, cotumelies, reproaches, or blasphemies: In by[illegible] imprisonment, and bonds, yea and death also. For [illegible] these afflictions Godly men are exercised, for the truth sa[illegible] of the wicked. And in these may be copryſed all other kinds of tribulation. The which the Epistle of Jesus Christ recites in a Godly order. There is nothing therefore of these matters which the Lord Christ knows not.

**Modern English:**

And here particularly he declares what he knew: First in deed the afflictions, which truly they suffered in the present persecution of Emperor Domitian. And affliction is as it were a general word, encompassing four kinds of suffering. For he recounts, concerning their substance the loss of their goods, and their poverty; in their name, insults, reproaches, or blasphemies; by imprisonment, and bonds, yea and death also. For through these afflictions godly men are tested because of the wickedness of the wicked. And in these may be included all other kinds of tribulation. The which the Epistle of Jesus Christ recites in a godly order. There is nothing therefore of these matters which the Lord Christ does not know.

---

### Paragraph 170

**Original (1561):**

> Poverty hath the first place.[note 9.9] Neither ought we to take it here spiritually for the modesty and humbleness of mind: although it be certain, that the church of Smyrna wanted not the same virtue: But there was poverty and lack of all things by reason of the spoiling of their goods. For in time of persecution, by virtue of kings proclamatios, the goods of the faithful professors of Christ are cofiſcated to the kings use, or permitted to the soldiers, nobles, or promoters to take at their pleasure: The faithful thrust out of their houses, are either driven into exile, or go a begging: would God we wanted examples thereof at this day. Let us hereof learn to bear and suffer patiently the like chances also, being persuaded that God knows our necessity. And because it is an hard thing [illegible]or an honest man to honger and want with his family, for a [illegible]omfort and consolation he adds, but thou art rich.

**Modern English:**

Poverty has the first place. We should not understand this spiritually, as referring to a humble and modest mind—though it is certain that the church of Smyrna possessed that virtue—but rather as a literal poverty and lack of all things, due to the confiscation of their possessions. For during times of persecution, by virtue of royal proclamations, the goods of faithful Christians are seized for the king’s use, or permitted to soldiers, nobles, or others to take as they please. The faithful, driven from their homes, are either exiled or reduced to begging. Would God we lacked examples of this today. Let us learn from this to bear and suffer such circumstances patiently, being persuaded that God knows our needs. And because it is difficult for an honest man to hunger and want with his family, for comfort and consolation he adds, “But you are rich.”

---

### Paragraph 171

**Original (1561):**

> This to the world seems a paradoxe, or incredible.[note 9.10] What will they say, is he rich that hath nothing, and is brought [illegible]o the state of beggars? There be doubtless goods and ry [illegible]hes of the mind much better than corporal substance.

**Modern English:**

This appears to the world as a paradox, or something unbelievable. What will they say, that someone is rich who has nothing and is reduced to the state of a beggar? There are undoubtedly spiritual possessions and riches much better than material substance.

---

### Paragraph 172

**Original (1561):**

> For this may be had, without the true felicity, of rich men [illegible]f this world, that live a most miserable life. Again you [illegible]hall see a poor man, concerning worldly goods, but fur [illegible]iſhed with the rycheſſe of the mind, for this cause only to [illegible]e happy and most blessed. He covets nothing, he is con [illegible]ent with his vocation: Neither would he change his state with most wealthy and rich kings. Contrariwyſe you [illegible]hall see rich men but of an evil conscience, and therefore [illegible]houghtfull and burdened with cares, and never merry. You [illegible]hall see poor men, but with merry hearts to lead a joyful [illegible]fe. Why than should it seem marvel, if he that is ſpoy [illegible]ed of his worldly goods for Christ, and enriched with the [illegible]iftes of the mind, is glad and rejoices in God, and takes a good part all chances, and for the same cause is judged [illegible]o be verily rich? Doubtless the wise men of this world [illegible]we also, that the only wise man is trewly rich. Which is gallantly discoursed of Cicero. Arethas saith, in spiritual matters having a treasure hid in the field of thy heart, which is Christ, by reason of who thou art rich also: Sin[illegible] thou hast him thy protector, who also when he was rich, for us became poor. &c.

**Modern English:**

For this may be had, without the true felicity, of rich men of this world, that live a most miserable life. Again you shall see a poor man, concerning worldly goods, but furnished with the richness of the mind, for this cause only to be happy and most blessed. He covets nothing, he is content with his vocation: Neither would he change his state with most wealthy and rich kings. Contrariwise you shall see rich men but of an evil conscience, and therefore thoughtful and burdened with cares, and never merry. You shall see poor men, but with merry hearts to lead a joyful life. Why then should it seem marvelous, if he that is stripped of his worldly goods for Christ, and enriched with the gifts of the mind, is glad and rejoices in God, and takes a good part in all chances, and for the same cause is judged to be verily rich? Doubtless the wise men of this world know also, that the only wise man is truly rich. Which is eloquently discoursed of Cicero. Arethas saith, in spiritual matters having a treasure hid in the field of thy heart, which is Christ, by reason of whom thou art rich also: Since thou hast him thy protector, who also when he was rich, for us became poor. &c.

---

### Paragraph 173

**Original (1561):**

> [note 9.11]In the second place is recited blasphemy, by the which[illegible] we understand all manner of railings and ſclauders, when[illegible] by the name and estimation of the faithful is hurt. Of th[illegible] which sort are these: They be heretics and Schismatic[illegible] so many as be favourers of this religion: They be wicke[illegible] people, diſpiſers of God and his saints, the ennemies of [illegible] Gods service, and therefore the plagues of the common wealt[illegible] which if they be suffered, the common wealth must needs [illegible] destroyed. And these things in deed many times vex go[illegible] men more grievously, than the loss of their goods. For w[illegible] will not set more by a good name, than by great rich[illegible] Therefore the Lord in Gospel of S. Math. the.10. Chapter[illegible] With many words heals this disease: And exhorts [illegible] that for the avoiding of that infamy, they should commit[illegible] thing unworthy the name of christians.

**Modern English:**

Secondly, blasphemy is mentioned, by which we understand all manner of railing and slander, whereby the reputation and standing of the faithful are harmed. Of this sort are those who call believers heretics and schismatics, and who label those who favor this religion as wicked people, as despisers of God and his saints, as enemies of God’s service, and therefore as plagues of the common wealth. If such people are tolerated, the common wealth must needs be destroyed. And these things often vex good men more grievously than the loss of their possessions. For people will not value a good name less than great riches. Therefore, the Lord in the Gospel of Saint Matthew, chapter 10, heals this disease with many words and exhorts that, to avoid such infamy, they should not do anything unworthy of the name of Christians.

---

### Paragraph 174

**Original (1561):**

> [note 9.12]In the mean time he declares also, what moved the [illegible] thours of this mischief, whom he blames also exceadyn[illegible] to the intent that the godly should understand, how greatly th[illegible] ennemies of all Godliness are of God miſliked: And the[illegible] might also care the less for their hatred and perſecutio. T[illegible] say in deed, that they be Jews, where they be nothing [illegible] Thus also S. Paul handled the Jews in the ii. to the [illegible] mains. The Jews are called confessors, honorers, and faithful servants of God. But these blaspheme Gods na[illegible] they impugn the true faith, and oppress them that prof[illegible] and worship God. Therefore be they not Jews. W[illegible] than? The Synagoge, congregation or aſſemblee of Sa[illegible] Thus the very son of God plucks of the viſerne fr[illegible] these varlettes, to the comfort of all those that suffer pe[illegible] cution, of them that set forth them selves with stout titles the end it should never grieve them, that they are condemn[illegible] of such harlottes, the children of the Devil. Christ att[illegible] teeth to them the true title, and calls them not the o[illegible] holy, and catholic Church of God, but the conspire[illegible] and school of Satan, as in whom, not the spirit of God, but of Satan, inspires lies, iuggelinges, diſceiptes, blasphemies, fires and deaths. Therefore let it not grieve thee at this day, [illegible]n case it be thy fortune to be condemned for the Gospel, of those that call them selves most holy, most shining, most reverent, and most irreprehenſible Prelates and Patrones of the old church, religion, and catholic faith, which have on their side, counsels, fathers, so many successions of Biſhoppes, the prescript of so long time, and consent of so many Realms. They be nothing less than that they desire to be called: But rather the champions of Antichrist, and the professed ennemies, and treaders under feet of all christen piety. For whom is prepared everlasting destruction.

**Modern English:**

In the meantime, he declares also what motivated the authors of this mischief, whom he blames exceedingly, so that the godly might understand how greatly God disapproves of the enemies of all godliness, and so they might care the less for their hatred and persecution. They claim to be Jews, when they are nothing of the sort. Thus also Paul treated the Jews in his second letter to the Corinthians. The Jews are called confessors, honorers, and faithful servants of God. But these blaspheme God’s name, they attack the true faith, and oppress those who profess and worship God. Therefore, they are not Jews. What then? The synagogue, congregation, or assembly of Satan. Thus the very Son of God plucks the vile fruit from these varlets, to the comfort of all those who suffer persecution. It should not grieve them that they are condemned by such harlots, the children of the Devil. Christ assigns them the true title, and calls them not the holy and catholic Church of God, but the conspiracy and school of Satan, as in whom, not the spirit of God, but of Satan, inspires lies, jugglings, deceits, blasphemies, fires, and deaths. Therefore, let it not grieve you at this day, even if it is your fortune to be condemned for the Gospel, by those who call themselves most holy, most shining, most reverent, and most irreprehensible prelates and patrons of the old church, religion, and catholic faith, which have on their side, councils, fathers, so many successions of bishops, the prescription of so long a time, and the consent of so many realms. They are nothing less than that they desire to be called; but rather the champions of Antichrist, and the professed enemies and trample underfoot all Christian piety, for whom everlasting destruction is prepared.

---

### Paragraph 175

**Original (1561):**

> After this he puts an exhortation and a consolation most evident, before the which setting also the son,[note 9.13] he saith, fear nothing of all that thou shalt suffer. The son of God him self feared the cross and death, and it is a natural thing to fear evils, and death. Therefore we are not commanded, that we should non be men, and that like [illegible]toickes we should say howe the same things grieve us not, which nevertheless tourment us exceedingly: but [illegible]he faithful are encouraged, that they should stand strong [illegible]n the faith, neither that they should doe any thing vnwor [illegible]hy the same for fear of punishment. We be therefore com [illegible]aunded boldly and cherely to contemn or suppress fear, [illegible]nd to crave strength by the spirit of God, and to exercise it [illegible] temptations.

**Modern English:**

After this he puts an exhortation and a consolation most evident, before which setting also the Son [note 9.13], he says, “Fear nothing of all that you shall suffer.” The Son of God himself feared the cross and death, and it is a natural thing to fear evils and death. Therefore, we are not commanded to be inhuman, and like stones to say how the same things grieve us not, which nevertheless torment us exceedingly. But the faithful are encouraged to stand strong in the faith, and to do nothing unworthy of it for fear of punishment. We are therefore commanded boldly and freely to scorn or suppress fear, and to seek strength by the spirit of God, and to exercise it in temptations.

---

### Paragraph 176

**Original (1561):**

> There follow reasons,[note 9.14] whereby he may obtain that he [illegible]ath persuaded, may confirm, comfort and exhort them [illegible] patience and constancy. He prophecies therefore to the Godly, what thing they shall suffer: And touches also the [illegible]hird kind of affliction, imprisonment and bonds, under [illegible]hiche we understand all puniſhementes whereby our by [illegible]ies are tourmented. But to be warned before of the evil, is great benefit. We are more easily overcome of vnproui [illegible]ed perils. And therefore the Lord in the Gospel after S. [illegible]ath. the x. Chapter. and after John in the xv. and xvi. Chapters [illegible]lleth his disciples of many evils that should come unto them, and adds thereto: These things have I spoken to you, that when the time shall come, the might remembr[illegible] them, that I have told you before. So now also faithfully warns the faithful in this Epistle.

**Modern English:**

There follow reasons, [note 9.14] whereby he may obtain that he hath persuaded, may confirm, comfort and exhort them in patience and constancy. He prophesies therefore to the godly, what things they shall suffer. And he touches also the third kind of affliction, imprisonment and bonds, which we understand to mean all punishments whereby our bodies are tormented. But to be warned before of the evil is a great benefit. We are more easily overcome by unexpected perils. And therefore the Lord in the Gospel after Augustine, the tenth chapter, and after John in the fifteenth and sixteenth chapters, tells his disciples of many evils that should come unto them, and adds thereto: These things have I spoken to you, that when the time shall come, you might remember that I have told you before. So now also faithfully warns the faithful in this Epistle.

---

### Paragraph 177

**Original (1561):**

> [note 9.15]And he touches the author of these evils, saying: Th[illegible] Devil will cast some of you into prison. Therefore we perceive that those euillies arise of the common ennemy of mankynde, and of the salvation of the faithful: Whereof we may conjecture, that he goes about to intercept our salvation, and that we ought therefore to stand more earſtly against him. The soldiers when they hear that their old ennemy is at hand, wax not ſluggyſhe, but cherefull. But the Devil inspires evil men, corrupts Princes and Mag[illegible] ſtrates, which attempt persecution against the church. S[illegible] we read that Satan afflicted Iob, that is to have prou[illegible] ked the Chaldeis and Sabeis to kill his ſeruautes and dri[illegible] away his cattle. Here therefore they may see, with what ſ[illegible] rite they are encouraged, which at this day persecute t[illegible] church of Christ, for the profession of the verity. The Go[illegible] lie have that which may comfort them: For they hear th[illegible] the same filthy beast is set against them, which so oft be[illegible] vanquished of Christ the Prince of the faithful, and of t[illegible] faithful through Christ's aid, may without any difficul[illegible] be overcome. And verily the Lord permits to the De[illegible] and deuelyſhemen power over his servants. If thou m[illegible] uell why,[note 9.16] hear: That you may be tempted. God permit[illegible] not his to Satan, that they should peryſhe, but that t[illegible] should be tempted and tried. Therefore to a good end are [illegible] delivered to the fire, that we might be pourged from our thines, that the virtue of our faith mought shine, and G[illegible] might be glorified, and we made the purer. Who therf[illegible] will hereafter be impatient, when we hear that we for t[illegible] great good are put to evil? We read in the iii. of Wiſdo[illegible] As gold is tried in the fire, so are the faithful proved. T[illegible] parable hath S. Peter expounded at large in the iiii. Cha[illegible] of the first Epistle.[note 9.17] Where he that will may have it mor[illegible] boundauntly.

**Modern English:**

And he touches the author of these evils, saying: The Devil will cast some of you into prison. Therefore we perceive that those evils arise from the common enemy of mankind and of the salvation of the faithful. Whereof we may conjecture that he goes about to intercept our salvation, and that we ought therefore to stand more earnestly against him. The soldiers when they hear that their old enemy is at hand, do not become sluggish, but cheerful. But the Devil inspires evil men, corrupts Princes and Magistrates, which attempt persecution against the church. As we read that Satan afflicted Job, that is to have provoked the Chaldeans and Sabeans to kill his servants and drive away his cattle. Here therefore they may see, with what authority they are encouraged, which at this day persecute the church of Christ, for the profession of the truth. The godly have that which may comfort them: For they hear that the same filthy beast is set against them, which so often has been vanquished by Christ the Prince of the faithful, and by the faithful through Christ's aid, may without any difficulty be overcome. And verily the Lord permits to the Devil and devilish men power over his servants. If thou marvel why, hear: That you may be tempted. God does not permit his people to Satan, that they should perish, but that they should be tempted and tried. Therefore to a good end are delivered to the fire, that we might be purged from our sins, that the virtue of our faith might shine, and God might be glorified, and we made the purer. Who therefore will hereafter be impatient, when we hear that we for a great good are put to evil? We read in the third book of Wisdom: As gold is tried in the fire, so are the faithful proved. This parable has Saint Augustine expounded at large in the fourth chapter of the first Epistle. Where he that will may have it more abundantly.

---

### Paragraph 178

**Original (1561):**

> Moreover the time also of tribulation is assigned [illegible] that for ten days. The tenth number signifies a multitu[illegible] For Iacob says to his father in Law: Ten times haste thou changed my wages. Genesis xxxi. and Numeri xiiii. He saith he was tempted ten times, that is often, and many times. Iob also affirms him self in the xxix. chapter. To have been injuried ten times. The Lord therefore saith at this present. You shall be diversely and much molested with evils. Notwithstanding forasmuch as he puts not months, years, nor ages, but days, he prophecies that the evils shall not be continual, but that there shall always be spaces be twirte to breathe in, verily for the shortness of persecution, first Essay the.26. Chapter. Secondly S. Peter in the first. Petri. 1. Do comfort the faithful. It is the part of the faithful not to prescribe God: But whether we be put to payne a long time or short, to take it patiently. Let us think rather, that in the long continuance of evils, there is some end also forſene of the Lord: And that in the same time of breathing, we must repare the evils, and return unto battle.

**Modern English:**

Moreover, the time of tribulation is also assigned as ten days. The tenth number signifies a multitude. For Jacob says to his father-in-law, “Ten times you have changed my wages.” (Genesis 31) And Numbers 14 says he was tempted ten times, that is, often and many times. Job also affirms himself in chapter 29 to have been wronged ten times. Therefore, the Lord says at this present, “You will be diversely and greatly troubled with evils.” Nevertheless, because he does not specify months, years, or ages, but days, he prophesies that the evils will not be continuous, but that there will always be spaces between to breathe in. Indeed, for the shortness of persecution, first essay 26th chapter. Secondly, Saint Peter in 1 Peter 1 comforts the faithful. It is the part of the faithful not to prescribe to God, but whether we are put to pain for a long time or a short time, to take it patiently. Let us think rather that in the long continuance of evils, there is also some end foreseen by the Lord; and that in the same time of breathing, we must repair the evils and return to battle.

---

### Paragraph 179

**Original (1561):**

> Last,[note 9.18] are the Godly encouraged by a most ample and large promise, in the which is also included the fourth and most grievous kind of affliction, also the bitter death it self, through fire, halter, sword, water. &c. But in case thou be not afraid of death, but vanquishing it also, shall offer up thy self unto God, than will I give thee, saith the Lord, a crown of life. Hereunto is annexed the state of the Epistle, and some of all. Therefore be thou faithful, cherefull, constant, even to the very death, For the Lord saith also in the Gospel: Who so perseveres to the end, he shall be saved. And we read that the Apoſtel hath said, if we die with Christ, we shall live with him. And truly the crown of life is none other thing, than eternal life, and that everlasting, celestial and unspeakable joy. And the Lord alluded to conflicts, after the which luckily finished, the victors are crowned. Blessed is the man, saith the Apostle S. James, that suffers temptation: because that when he shall be tried, he shall receive a crown of life, which the Lord hath promised to those of whom he is loved. Like things hath the Apostle S. Paul written also in the first to the Corinthians the ix. and in the second to Timothee the iiii. Chapter. Therefore let it be harde hereafter for no man to lose this temporal life. Where[illegible] as the same being lost for Christ, we shall receive eternal life and otherwise will we nyll we must die. Let us therefore b[illegible] content rather to die blessedly, than to live miserably, so th[illegible] we see we may so please God.

**Modern English:**

Finally, the godly are encouraged by a most ample and large promise, in which is also included the fourth and most grievous kind of affliction, and the bitter death itself, through fire, hanging, sword, water, etc. But if you are not afraid of death, but overcome it, and offer yourself to God, then I will give you, says the Lord, a crown of life. Hereunto is annexed the state of the epistle, and some of all. Therefore be faithful, cheerful, constant, even to death. For the Lord also says in the Gospel: Whoever perseveres to the end shall be saved. And we read that the Apostle has said, if we die with Christ, we shall live with him. And truly, the crown of life is nothing other than eternal life, that everlasting, celestial, and unspeakable joy. And the Lord alludes to conflicts, after which, happily finished, the victors are crowned. Blessed is the man, says the Apostle James, who suffers temptation, because when he is tested, he will receive a crown of life, which the Lord has promised to those he loves. Similar things the Apostle Paul has written also in the first to the Corinthians, chapter nine, and in the second to Timothy, chapter four. Therefore let it be difficult hereafter for any man to lose this temporal life, since, losing it for Christ, we shall receive eternal life; otherwise, we will, we must, die. Let us therefore be content rather to die blessedly than to live miserably, so that we may please God.

---

### Paragraph 180

**Original (1561):**

> Finally like as in the end of the first Epistle, he comm[illegible] nicated and applied the same wholly to all times and chu[illegible] ches,[note 9.19] least any should suppose that these things concern[illegible] him nothing: So in the end of this Epistle also, he both pre[illegible] cheth the spirit to be author of all these things, and exho[illegible] teeth all men to hear and obey diligently, and affirms thy[illegible] to be written unto all congregations in the world for edifying. Moreover the promise of life he communicates to [illegible] saying: He that ouercometh shall not be hurt of the second death. This is spoken to all men and women, if thou overcome[illegible] Therefore must we overcome the world, the Devil, the flesh[illegible] and all temptation. And we must overcome by him, which faith, by his spirit, dwelling in us: And that we should wall[illegible] that way, wherein he hath commanded us to walk. If tho[illegible] overcome,[note 9.20] thou shalt not be hurt in the second death. Th[illegible] mas of Aquine saith. That the first death is of sin, the ſeco [illegible] of pain. We understand plainly by the first death the na[illegible] rall separation of the soul from the body, which also co[illegible] meth to us for sin, as appears in the iii. of Genesis. Th[illegible] same is come to good and evil. For we be all earth, and [illegible] to earth we shall return. And by and by follows the [illegible] cond death and the second life: They that believe in Christ, overcome, feel nothing of the second death, but live, as t[illegible] Lord him self assures us in the iii. and v. Chapter of Ioh[illegible] He shall not come into judgment, but hath passed fro dea[illegible] to life. But the wicked or vnbeleuers are conueyed ſtre[illegible] ways from the corporal death to death everlasting: N[illegible] that their souls can die, that is cease to be, or that their bod[illegible] rise not again: But that being deprived of that celeſt[illegible] and divine life of Christ, they feel everlasting tourment[illegible] which state verily is ryghtly called death. These thing are unknown to worldly men, which know no other life death but this temporal: But Gods verity teaches us, th[illegible] there is both an other life and death after this, to wit the [illegible] celestial, and death infernal, or full of perpetual sorrows. That same doubtless is full of consolation, that we hear how the faithful after the debt of this temporal life payed once, they shall no more feel any tourmentes. What than doe the Monks and friars prate of purgatory? bables. &c. Let us praise our saviour Christ, which hath delivered us from death, and given us the hope of life everlasting, to whom be glory, praise. &c.

**Modern English:**

Just as in the end of the first Epistle, he communicated and applied the same wholly to all times and churches, lest any should suppose that these things concern him nothing. So in the end of this Epistle also, he both attributes the spirit to be the author of all these things, and exhorts all men to hear and obey diligently, and affirms it to be written unto all congregations in the world for edifying. Moreover, the promise of life he communicates, saying: He that overcomes shall not be hurt by the second death. This is spoken to all men and women, if you overcome. Therefore, we must overcome the world, the Devil, the flesh, and all temptation. And we must overcome by him, through faith, by his spirit dwelling in us: and that we should walk that way wherein he has commanded us to walk. If you overcome, you shall not be hurt in the second death. Thomas of Aquin says that the first death is of sin, the second of pain. We understand plainly by the first death the natural separation of the soul from the body, which also comes to us because of sin, as appears in the third chapter of Genesis. The same is come to good and evil. For we are all earth, and to earth we shall return. And immediately follows the second death and the second life: They that believe in Christ, overcome, feel nothing of the second death, but live, as the Lord himself assures us in the third and fifth chapter of John. He shall not come into judgment, but has passed from death to life. But the wicked or unbelievers are conveyed straightways from corporal death to everlasting death. Not that their souls can die, that is cease to be, or that their bodies rise not again: but that being deprived of that celestial and divine life of Christ, they feel everlasting torment, which state verily is rightly called death. These things are unknown to worldly men, which know no other life but this temporal. But God’s truth teaches us that there is both another life and death after this, to wit, the celestial, and death infernal, or full of perpetual sorrows. That same is full of consolation, that we hear how the faithful, after the debt of this temporal life is paid once, they shall no more feel any torments. What then do the monks and friars prate of purgatory, babes? Let us praise our savior Christ, which has delivered us from death, and given us the hope of life everlasting, to whom be glory, praise, and so on.

---

## Sermon 10: ¶ The first part of the third Epistle of the cu ſtancie and cofeſſion of Christ in the time of perſecutio.

### Paragraph 181

**Original (1561):**

> ANd to the messenger of the Congregation in Pergamos write: This saith he which hath the sharpe sword with two edges.
